Divine Attributes
intermediate
8 min
Necessary Attributes and Plurality of Necessary Beings
Razi replies to objections that necessary attributes imply a plurality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the claim that shared necessity or existence among attributes leads to multiple necessary beings.

Core Idea
Sharing a name does not mean sharing the mode of being.

Razi continues defending the oneness of the Necessary Being by addressing critical objections. Opponents argue that if necessity or existence is predicated of multiple things, then there must be multiple necessary beings. Razi carefully distinguishes between necessary existence of the essence and accidental attributes, and between quiddity and existence, showing that shared predicates do not entail plurality.

Guided Path
1
Understand the structure of the passage
The passage contains three objections and replies, followed by a noted difficulty. Read the Arabic text and translation first to get an overview.
Each objection is introduced by 'قوله' (His statement) and each reply by 'قلنا' (We said).
2
Examine objection 1 on necessary attributes
The objector argues that attributes like oddness and evenness are necessary in themselves, implying multiple necessary beings. Find Razi's reply that such attributes are accidental to essences, not necessary in themselves.
3
Examine objection 2 on sharing necessity
The objector says the necessary in itself shares necessity with the necessary through another. Razi replies that it is distinguished by a negative qualification, so no multiplicity follows.
A 'negative qualification' means a privative attribute (e.g., 'not dependent').
4
Examine objection 3 on sharing existence
The objector says the necessary in itself shares existence with the possible, differing in determination. Razi replies that existence is distinct from quiddity, and the quiddity may entail existence without causing plurality.
5
Note the remaining difficulty
Razi acknowledges a problem: if essence is cause of existence and both are eternal, then effect depends on cause without temporal priority. He leaves this for divine guidance.
This difficulty is not resolved in this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : "كون الثلاثة فردًا والأربعة زوجًا أمور واجبة لذواتها، فإذن واجب الوجود أكثر من واحد قلنا مرادنا من قولنا واجب الوجود لذاته الموجود الذي يكون مستقلاً ومستبدأ بذاته وتحققه وهذه صفات تكون لاحقة للماهيات فلا تكون واجبة لذواتها. قوله : "الواجب لذاته يشارك الواجب لغيره في مسمى الوجوب" قلنا : لكنه يمتاز عنه بقيد سلبي، وعلى هذا التقدير لا تلزم الكثرة. قوله : "الواجب لذاته يشارك الممكن لذاته في مسمى الموجودية ويخالفه في التعين فتلزم الكثرة قلنا : مذهبنا أن الوجود الذي به المشاركة مغاير للماهية المخصوصة التي بها المخالفة لكن لم لا يجوز أن تكون تلك الماهية مستلزمة لذلك الوجود؟ إلا أنه يبقى ههنا إشكال وهو أنكم لما جعلتم تلك الماهية علة لذلك الوجود، مع كون الماهية والوجود دائمين فقد اعترفتم بأن استناد الأثر إلى المؤثر لا يتوقف على الحدوث، وهذا الإشكال مما نستخير الله تعالى فيه.
His statement: 'The fact that three is odd and four is even are things necessary in themselves, so then the necessary of existence is more than one.' We said: What we mean by 'necessary of existence in itself' is the existent that is independent and self-subsisting in its essence and its realization. These attributes (odd/even) are accidents attached to essences, so they are not necessary in themselves. His statement: 'The necessary in itself shares with the necessary through another in the meaning of necessity.' We said: But it is distinguished from it by a negative qualification, and on this basis multiplicity does not follow. His statement: 'The necessary in itself shares with the possible in itself in the meaning of existence and differs from it in determination, so multiplicity follows.' We said: Our doctrine is that existence, by which participation occurs, is distinct from the particular essence by which difference occurs. However, why is it not possible that that essence entails that existence? Except that there remains here a difficulty: You have made that essence a cause of that existence, while the essence and existence are eternal, so you have admitted that the attribution of the effect to the cause does not depend on temporality. This difficulty is something we seek guidance from Allah about.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a series of objections and replies regarding the argument that if necessity or existence is shared among multiple things, then there would be multiple necessary beings. Razi responds to three objections. First, the claim that necessary properties like oddness and evenness imply multiple necessary beings is refuted by distinguishing between necessary existence of the essence and accidental attributes that are superadded to essences and not necessary in themselves. Second, the objection that the necessary in itself shares necessity with the necessary through another is answered by noting that the distinction is by a negative qualification, so no multiplicity follows. Third, the objection that the necessary in itself shares existence with the possible but differs in determination is met by asserting that existence is distinct from quiddity, and that the quiddity may entail existence, causing no multiplicity. However, Razi notes a remaining difficulty: if the essence is a cause of existence and both are eternal, then effect depends on cause without temporality, which is problematic. He leaves this as an unresolved issue.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود لذاته
قيد سلبي
الماهية
الوجود
التعين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that sharing a predicate implies sharing in the mode of existence.
Correction: Razi clarifies that necessary attributes are not self-subsistent; they are accidental to essences. Sharing a name does not mean sharing the mode of being.

Checkpoint
What is Razi's response to the objection that necessary attributes like oddness and evenness imply multiple necessary beings?
A) He accepts the objection and modifies his doctrine.
B) He says those attributes are not necessary in themselves but are superadded to essences.
C) He argues for the non-existence of such attributes.
D) He claims that oddness and evenness are not real.

Key Takeaway
Razi shows that shared predicates do not lead to plurality because the predicates are either accidental or distinguished by negative qualifications.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that sharing a label (like 'good' or 'powerful') between Allah and creation means they share the same reality? How does this lesson challenge your understanding of tawheed and the uniqueness of Allah?
?
Razi admits a difficulty he leaves to Allah. Are you comfortable with unresolved theological mysteries, or do you seek complete rational closure? What does this reveal about your heart's reliance on Allah versus your own intellect?
?
When you attribute necessity or existence to created things—like health, wealth, or relationships—do you subconsciously elevate them to a status that belongs only to Allah? How can you guard your heart against this subtle shirk after learning that these attributes are not self-subsistent but merely accidental?

Razi's Honest Admission of a Difficult Objection
Razi acknowledges a difficult objection about divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi honestly acknowledges a difficult objection regarding God's attributes and the limits of human reasoning in theology.

Core Idea
Razi shows that some objections have no easy answer—honesty is better than false closure.

This brief passage from Imam Razi's Al-Arbaeen presents a critical moment where a standard objection is raised against Razi's own argument. Instead of offering a quick refutation, Razi admits the difficulty and seeks divine guidance. This reveals a deep intellectual humility and a key principle in kalam: not every question has a human answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic sentence and its English translation carefully. Notice that Razi quotes the objection before responding.
The objection is about God being knowing through knowledge and powerful through power.
2
Identify the objection's logic
The objector argues that if God is knowing by knowledge and powerful by power, then these attributes are like causes. This challenges Razi's earlier arguments about effects needing causes.
This is a self-referential problem.
3
Analyze Razi's response
Razi says the question is difficult (صعب) and seeks divine guidance (نستخير الله). He does not refute, evade, or dismiss. He acknowledges the problem.
This is a rare moment of vulnerability in kalam literature.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why would a great scholar like Razi admit difficulty? What does this teach us about the role of human reason in theology?
It shows that some questions may be beyond human resolution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : "هذا منقوض بكونه تعالى عالمًا بالعلم، قادرًا بالقدرة " قلنا هذا السؤال صعب، وهو مما نستخير الله تعالى فيه.
His statement: 'This is rebutted by His being knowing through knowledge and powerful through power.' We said: This question is difficult, and it is something we seek guidance from Allah about.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This is a brief but significant passage where Razi addresses an objection. The objector points out that God is described as knowing through knowledge and powerful through power, which seems analogous to the argument that effects need causes. Razi admits that this question is difficult and leaves it as an unresolved issue, seeking divine guidance. It shows his intellectual honesty.

Key Terms
عالم بالعلم
قادر بالقدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He provides a detailed refutation.
B) He dismisses it as irrelevant.
C) He admits it is a difficult question and seeks divine guidance.
D) He changes his position.

Key Takeaway
Razi admits that the objection from God's attributes is a genuinely difficult issue, teaching us that not all theological puzzles have clear solutions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
This passage shows that even great scholars like Razi encounter limits of reason. How does that challenge your pride in your own intellect? Will you accept the same humility in your theological studies, or will you insist on having all the answers?

The Difficulty of Theological Questions
Imam Razi notes a difficult objection regarding God being knowing through knowledge and powerful through power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that some theological objections are recognized as genuinely difficult, requiring intellectual humility and reliance on divine guidance.

Core Idea
Even great scholars admit when a question is beyond easy answer.

This passage from Imam Razi's Al-Arbaeen presents a moment of scholarly humility. A critic raises an objection about God's attributes being distinct entities. Rather than dismissing it with a quick answer, Razi acknowledges the difficulty and states that he seeks Allah's guidance. This teaches us that not every theological problem has an immediate solution, and that humility is a virtue in scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the English translation. Notice the tone: Razi does not give a rebuttal here, but acknowledges difficulty.
The passage is short but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the objection
The objection is that the earlier argument is refuted by God being 'knowing through knowledge' and 'powerful through power'—implying that attributes are distinct from essence.
This objection touches on the nature of divine attributes.
3
Observe Razi's response
Razi says 'this question is difficult' and 'we seek divine guidance concerning it'. He does not provide a full answer here but refers to longer works.
This shows intellectual humility and trust in Allah.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why a great scholar would admit difficulty. What does this teach about the pursuit of knowledge and the limits of human reasoning?
Humility before difficult questions is a sign of true scholarship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب عن تلك الوجوه على سبيل التفصيل فمذكور في المطولات. قوله : "هذا منقوض بكونه تعالى عالمًا بالعلم، قادرًا بالقدرة " قلنا هذا السؤال صعب، وهو مما نستخير الله تعالى فيه.
As for the detailed answer to those aspects, it is mentioned in the lengthy works. He said: "This is refuted by His being knowing through knowledge, powerful through power." We said: This question is difficult, and it is something we seek divine guidance about.

Commentary
This short passage acknowledges a difficult objection. Someone (probably a critic) has stated that the earlier argument is refuted by the fact that God is described as 'knowing through knowledge' and 'powerful through power'—implying that attributes are distinct entities. Imam Razi admits this is a difficult question and that he seeks divine guidance (istikhara) concerning it. This shows humility and that not all problems have easy answers.

The First Refutation: Determinism or Infinite Regress
First refutation: will's suitability leads to regress or necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the first answer leads to either determinism (necessity) or infinite regress, and how Razi argues against it.

Core Idea
If God's will is fixed, He is either not free or an infinite chain is needed.

Razi begins his detailed refutation by examining the first answer that attributes the specific timing of creation to God's eternal will. He shows that this explanation collapses into two unacceptable consequences: it either implies God is not a free agent (but a necessitated cause) or leads to an infinite regress of preponderators. Let's follow his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the either/or structure.
2
Identify the two branches
The argument splits based on whether the eternal will could have been connected to another time. Identify both possibilities.
3
Work through the consequences
For each branch, trace the logical conclusion: chance/regress or necessitated agent. Understand why both are problematic.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنا بعد هذا نبطل كل واحد من تلك الأجوبة على سبيل التفصيل : أما الجواب الأول : فهو ضعيف من وجهي : الأول : أن الإرادة القديمة المتعلقة بإيجاد العالم في الوقت المعين إما أن تكون صالحة التعلق بإيجاد العالم في وقت آخر، وإما أن لا تكون صالحة لذلك، فإن كان الأول كانت الإرادة صالحة لإيجاد العالم في هذا الوقت، وصالحة للتعلق بإيجاد العالم في سائر الأوقات، فترجيح بعض التعلقات على ما سواه، إن لم يفتقر إلى فقد المرجح ترجح الممكن لا عن مرجح وإن افتقر إلى المرجح كان الكلام فيه كما في الأول، ويلزم التسلسل، وإن كان الحق هو القسم الثاني، وهو أن تلك الإرادة المتعلقة بإيجاد العالم في ذلك الوقت ما كانت صالحة للتعلق بإيجاد العالم في وقت آخر فحينئذ لا يكون هذا المؤثر فاعلا مختاراً، بل مؤثر موجبًا بالذات، وإذا كان موجبا امتنع تخلف المعلول عنه، فهذا يقتضي القول بقدم العالم لا بحدوثه. والوجه الثاني : أن تعلق إرادة الله بإيجاد العالم إما أن لا يكون مشروطًا بوقت معين، وإما أن يكون مشروطًا بوقت معين فإن لم يكن مشروطًا بوقت معين فهذا معناه أنه تعالى في الأزل أراد إيجاد العالم من غير أن تكون تلك الإرادة مختصة بوقت معين وإذا كانت الإرادة غير مقيدة بوقت معين البتة وما كانت مشروطة بشرط فائت البتة وتتب حصول المراد مع هذه الإرادة، فكان التقدم لازما. وأما إن كانت الإرادة مشروطة بوقت معين مثل أن يقال إنه تعالى أراد إيجاد العالم في وقت معين فذلك الوقت إن كان حاضرًا في الأزل لزم حصول ذلك المراد في الأزل، وإن لم يكن حاضرًا في الأزل كان حادثاً، فحينئذ ينتقل الكلام إلى كيفية إرادته لإحداث ذلك الوقت فإن كانت تلك الإرادة مشروطة بوقت آخر لزم التسلسل، وهو محال.
Then after this we refute each one of those answers in detail: As for the first answer, it is weak from two aspects: The first: that the eternal will connected to bringing the world into existence at the specific time is either suitable for being connected to bringing the world into existence at another time, or it is not suitable for that. If it is the first, then the will is suitable for bringing the world into existence at this time and suitable for being connected to bringing the world into existence at other times, so the preponderance of some connections over others, if it does not depend on a preponderator, then the possible is preponderated without a preponderator; and if it depends on a preponderator, the discussion regarding it is as in the first, and infinite regress follows. And if the correct [case] is the second division, namely that that will connected to bringing the world into existence at that time was not suitable for being connected to bringing the world into existence at another time, then at that point this effecting agent is not a free agent, but rather an agent necessitated by essence; and if it is necessitated, the effect cannot be separated from it, so this entails the claim of the eternity of the world, not its temporal origination. The second aspect: that the connection of God's will to bringing the world into existence is either not conditioned by a specific time, or it is conditioned by a specific time. If it is not conditioned by a specific time, it means that He, exalted, in pre-eternity willed to bring the world into existence without that will being specific to a particular time; and when the will is absolutely not restricted to a specific time and not conditioned by any condition, the occurrence of the willed object would follow from this will, so priority would be necessary. And if the will is conditioned by a specific time, such as saying that He, exalted, willed to bring the world into existence at a specific time, then if that time was present in pre-eternity, the occurrence of that willed object in pre-eternity would follow; and if it was not present in pre-eternity, it would be temporal; then the discussion shifts to how He willed to bring about that time: if that will were conditioned by another time, infinite regress would follow, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of the first answer, which likely claimed that the eternal will of God is the reason why the world was created at a specific time and not earlier. Razi gives two arguments (two faces) against it. First: The eternal will is either capable of being connected to creating the world at other times or not. If it is capable, then why was it connected to this time? If no preponderator, that is chance; if a preponderator is needed, infinite regress. If it is not capable, then God is not free but determined, implying the world is eternal, not temporal. Second: The will's connection to creating the world is either unconditioned by time or conditioned. If unconditioned, the will would be eternal and its object (the world) would have to exist simultaneously, so the world would be eternal. If conditioned by a specific time, then either that time was eternal (impossible) or temporal, and then the same question arises for the will that created that time, leading to infinite regress. Thus, the first answer fails.

Key Terms
الإرادة القديمة
ترجيح
المرجح
فاعل مختار
موجب بالذات
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that simply saying 'God willed it to happen at that time' is a sufficient explanation, without further analysis.
Correction: Razi shows that this leads to either determinism (God not free) or an infinite regress, so it is not a tenable position.

Checkpoint
According to the first aspect of Razi's refutation, what follows if the eternal will is not suitable for creating the world at another time?
A) The world must be temporal
B) God is not a free agent but a necessitated agent
C) Infinite regress follows
D) The world becomes possible

Key Takeaway
Razi's first refutation shows that the first answer leads to either attributing necessity to God or requiring an infinite chain of causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a decision, do you ever act without any reason? How does this passage challenge you to examine whether you attribute arbitrariness to God's will?
?
If God's will is eternal and fixed, what does that mean for your personal prayers? Are you asking God to change His will, or is your prayer itself part of a predetermined plan? How does Razi's dilemma force you to reconsider the relationship between your supplication and God's freedom?
?
Razi argues that if God's will is not suitable for other times, then He is not free. Do you genuinely believe that God is free in His choices? If so, how do you answer the problem of why He chose this specific time for creation? Let this question lead you to deeper tawakkul (reliance) or a more profound inquiry into His wisdom.

The Second Refutation: Time as Condition and Infinite Regress
Second refutation: time condition leads to eternal world or regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi's second argument shows that the will's connection to creation cannot be conditioned by a specific time without leading to either an eternal world or an infinite regress.

Core Idea
Conditioning God's will on time either makes the world eternal or requires an infinite chain of wills.

In the second aspect of his refutation, Razi considers whether the eternal will is conditioned by a specific time or not. He shows that both options lead to absurdities, thereby undermining the first answer completely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two main branches: will not conditioned by time, or conditioned by a specific time.
2
Trace the consequences of each branch
If unconditioned, the world must be eternal. If conditioned, the time itself raises the same issue, leading to regress.
3
Reflect on the overall conclusion
Understand why the first answer fails completely from this second angle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Then after this we refute each one of those answers in detail: As for the first answer, it is weak from two aspects: The first: that the eternal will connected to bringing the world into existence at the specific time is either suitable for being connected to bringing the world into existence at another time, or it is not suitable for that. If it is the first, then the will is suitable for bringing the world into existence at this time and suitable for being connected to bringing the world into existence at other times, so the preponderance of some connections over others, if it does not depend on a preponderator, then the possible is preponderated without a preponderator; and if it depends on a preponderator, the discussion regarding it is as in the first, and infinite regress follows. And if the correct [case] is the second division, namely that that will connected to bringing the world into existence at that time was not suitable for being connected to bringing the world into existence at another time, then at that point this effecting agent is not a free agent, but rather an agent necessitated by essence; and if it is necessitated, the effect cannot be separated from it, so this entails the claim of the eternity of the world, not its temporal origination. The second aspect: that the connection of God's will to bringing the world into existence is either not conditioned by a specific time, or it is conditioned by a specific time. If it is not conditioned by a specific time, it means that He, exalted, in pre-eternity willed to bring the world into existence without that will being specific to a particular time; and when the will is absolutely not restricted to a specific time and not conditioned by any condition, the occurrence of the willed object would follow from this will, so priority would be necessary. And if the will is conditioned by a specific time, such as saying that He, exalted, willed to bring the world into existence at a specific time, then if that time was present in pre-eternity, the occurrence of that willed object in pre-eternity would follow; and if it was not present in pre-eternity, it would be temporal; then the discussion shifts to how He willed to bring about that time: if that will were conditioned by another time, infinite regress would follow, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of the first answer, which likely claimed that the eternal will of God is the reason why the world was created at a specific time and not earlier. Razi gives two arguments (two faces) against it. First: The eternal will is either capable of being connected to creating the world at other times or not. If it is capable, then why was it connected to this time? If no preponderator, that is chance; if a preponderator is needed, infinite regress. If it is not capable, then God is not free but determined, implying the world is eternal, not temporal. Second: The will's connection to creating the world is either unconditioned by time or conditioned. If unconditioned, the will would be eternal and its object (the world) would have to exist simultaneously, so the world would be eternal. If conditioned by a specific time, then either that time was eternal (impossible) or temporal, and then the same question arises for the will that created that time, leading to infinite regress. Thus, the first answer fails.

Key Terms
الإرادة
مشروط
وقت معين
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that saying 'God willed it to happen at a specific time' solves the problem, but Razi shows it leads to regress.
Correction: Conditioning the will on a specific time either makes the world eternal (if time is eternal) or requires an infinite chain of wills.

Checkpoint
According to the second aspect, what follows if the will is not conditioned by a specific time?
A) The world would be temporal
B) Infinite regress follows
C) The world would be eternal
D) God becomes a free agent

Key Takeaway
Razi's second refutation demonstrates that any attempt to condition God's will on time either results in an eternal world or an infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will is not conditioned by time, what does that mean for the 'timing' of events in your life? Are you ever tempted to think that God has delayed something He promised, as if His will was waiting for a specific moment? How would this passage correct that thought? This week, consciously replace any thought of 'delay' with the conviction that everything occurs exactly when His eternal will decreed.
?
The passage shows that infinite regress is impossible. Where in your own life do you find yourself relying on a chain of worldly causes (e.g., money, health, relationships) as if they must extend infinitely? Can you trace your ultimate reliance back to the Eternal? Commit today to identify one area where you trust in created means more than in the Creator, and repent.
?
Razi argues that any condition on God's will leads to absurdity. Do you ever place 'conditions' on your trust in God—like 'if this happens then I will believe He is good' or 'if He answers this prayer my way'? How does this lesson challenge you to surrender even the condition of time or outcome? Ask Allah to grant you a will that submits unconditionally to His eternal decree.

Refutation of the Second Answer on Divine Knowledge
Razi refutes the second answer with two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's two objections to the second answer, showing that it leads to circularity or determinism.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge follows the known, not the other way around.

Razi now turns to the second attempted answer, which claimed that divine knowledge determines the timing of creation. He presents two arguments to refute it: the first shows circularity, the second shows that it implies determinism. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation, to get the overall structure of Razi's refutation.
2
Identify the first objection
Notice the first argument: knowledge depends on the known, and the event depends on will. If we make the event depend on knowledge, we get circularity.
The key term is 'الدور' (circularity).
3
Identify the second objection
The second argument: if God's knowledge necessitates the event, then God cannot do otherwise, making Him a necessitated agent, not free.
This leads to 'إيجاب بالذات' (necessitation by essence).
4
Understand the conclusion
Razi concludes that the second answer fails because it either leads to circularity or to determinism, both of which are unacceptable for a free divine agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب الثاني فهو أيضًا ضعيف من وجهين : الأول : أن العلم بحدوث العالم في ذلك الوقت تبع لحدوث العالم في ذلك الوقت؛ لأن العلم تبع للمعلوم، وحدوث العالم في ذلك الوقت تبع لإرادة إحداثه في ذلك الوقت، فلو جعلنا إحداثه في ذلك الوقت تبعًا لعلمه بوقوع العالم في ذلك الوقت لزم الدور. الثاني : : أن علم الله تعالى لما تعلق بحدوث العالم في وقت معين وبعدم حدوثه في وقت آخر تغير المعلوم، وتغير المعلوم محال، فحينئذٍ يمتنع عقلا أن لا يحدثه في ذلك الوقت الذي علم حدوثه فيه، ويمتنع عقلا أن يحدثه في الوقت الذي علم أنه لا يحدثه فيه فهذا المؤثر لا يكون فاعلاً مختاراً، بل يكون موجبا بالذات.
As for the second answer, it is also weak from two aspects: The first: that knowledge of the world's coming into being at that time is dependent on the world's coming into being at that time, because knowledge is dependent on the known; and the world's coming into being at that time is dependent on the will to bring it into being at that time. So if we make its coming into being at that time dependent on His knowledge of the world's occurrence at that time, circularity follows. The second: that when God's knowledge, exalted, is connected to the world's coming into being at a specific time and to its not coming into being at another time, the known changes; and change of the known is impossible. So then it is rationally impossible that He not bring it about at the time He knew it would come into being, and rationally impossible that He bring it about at the time He knew it would not come into being. So this effecting agent is not a free agent, but rather necessitated by essence.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage refutes the second answer, which likely argued that divine knowledge determines the time of creation. Razi gives two arguments. First: Knowledge depends on the known object. If the world's creation at a specific time is made to depend on God's knowledge of that event, then that would be circular: the knowledge requires the event, and the event requires the knowledge. So that cannot be. Second: God's knowledge is of the world's occurrence at time T and non-occurrence at other times. Since knowledge corresponds to the known, and the known (the event) is fixed, then it is impossible for God to change it. Hence, God cannot act otherwise, so He is not free but necessitated. Thus, the second answer fails.

Key Terms
العلم (knowledge)
المعلوم (the known)
التبع (dependence)
الدور (circularity)
تغير المعلوم (change of the known)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine knowledge actively causes events to happen at specific times.
Correction: Razi argues that knowledge follows the known; it is dependent on the object, not the other way around. So knowledge does not determine the timing of events.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi argue that making the world's creation dependent on God's knowledge leads to circularity?
A) Because knowledge is eternal and cannot change
B) Because knowledge depends on the known, but the known depends on will, not knowledge
C) Because God's knowledge is not detailed
D) Because the world is eternal

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the second answer by showing it leads to circularity or determinism, thus divine knowledge does not determine creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that if God's knowledge determined the time of creation, God would not be free. Do you believe God is truly free? How does this understanding affect your trust in His decisions for your life? Will you actively remind yourself that God's will is not constrained by His knowledge?
?
The passage states that knowledge follows the known. This means God's knowledge is not the cause of events but rather reflects them. How does this change your perspective on God's foreknowledge and your own free will? Will you reflect on this when you next feel predestined?
?
If God were not free (as the second answer suggests), would you still worship Him? This passage refutes that. How does it affect your gratitude that God chooses to act freely? Will you praise Him for His freedom today?

First Objection: Infinite Regress and the Benefit's Timing
Al-Razi presents the first of two objections against the benefit-based timing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's first objection to the claim that creation was delayed for a unique benefit, focusing on the problem of infinite regress and the timing of the benefit.

Core Idea
A specific benefit cannot explain God's timing without leading to infinite regress or absurdity.

In this passage, Al-Razi tackles the third answer offered by those who wonder why God created the world at a specific time. The answer claims a unique benefit attached to that moment. Al-Razi's first objection dissects the logic of that benefit's existence and produces a devastating dilemma: either the benefit already existed, making the delay pointless, or it arose at that moment, leading to an infinite chain of causes.

Guided Path
1
Read the first objection passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully, focusing on the two parts: first about the time itself, then about the benefit's existence.
Notice the structure: Al-Razi presents two sub-arguments within the first objection.
2
Analyze the time argument
Al-Razi argues that the specific time itself must have a cause. If it is from God, the question of why that time and not another recurs, leading to infinite regress.
This is a classic argument against arbitrary specification.
3
Analyze the benefit argument
Al-Razi then considers the benefit's existence before or at that time. If it existed earlier, the world should have been created earlier; if it arose at that time, its necessity or possibility leads to further problems.
The dilemma: if necessary, things can occur without cause; if possible, another cause is needed.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Al-Razi concludes that both branches are impossible, thus the third answer fails. Consider how this protects divine freedom and avoids infinite regress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب الثالث وهو أن حدوث العالم إنما اختص بذلك الوقت لاختصاص ذلك الوقت بمصلحة لا توجد في سائر الأوقات، فهو أيضًا ضعيف من وجهين : الأول : أن حدوث العالم لما توقف على حضور ذلك الوقت المختص بتلك المصلحة، فحدوث ذلك الوقت إن كان لمحدث فهو نفي الصانع، وإن كان لمحدث فالسؤال عائد في أنه لم لم يحدث قبل ذلك الوقت أو بعده؟ ويلزم التسلسل، وأيضا فالمصلحة الحاصلة في ذلك الوقت إن كانت حاصلة قبل ذلك الوقت فما لأجله حدث العالم كان حاصلا قبل ذلك الوقت فيلزم أن يحدث العالم قبل أن حدث، وإنه محال، وإن قلنا : إن تلك المصلحة ما كانت حاصلة قبل ذلك الوقت، وإنما حصلت في ذلك الوقت فإما أن يقال: إنه حدثت تلك المصلحة في ذلك الوقت، مع وجوب أن تحدث أو مع جواز أن تحدث فإن كان الأول فقد جوزتم أن يحدث الشيء لذاته، وإذا جاز ذلك في بعض الأمور، جاز مثله في كلها، ويلزم نفي الصانع، وهو محال وإن كان الثاني افتقر اختصاص ذلك الوقت بتلك المصلحة إلى مخصص آخر، والكلام فيه كما في الأول، ويلزم التسلسل، وهو محال أيضًا. والثاني : وهو أن مع العلم باشتمال ذلك الوقت على تلك المصلحة إما أن يكون الترك ممكنا أو لا يكون فإن لم يكن ممكنا كان المؤثر موجبا بالذات، لا فاعلا بالاختيار، وإن كان الترك ممكنا فلما كان الترك والفعل ممكنين مع ذلك الداعي، فإن توقف على مرجح آخر كان الكلام فيه كمنا في الأول، ويلزم التسلسل، وإن لم يتوقف كان الممكن واقعا لا عن مرجح. لا يقال : الفعل مع هذا الداعي يصير أولى بالوقوع، ولا تنتهي الأولوية إلى حد الوجوب ؛ لأنا نقول : القول بهذه الأولوية سيأتي إبطاله إن شاء الله تعالى في مسألة خلق الأفعال.
As for the third answer — that the world's occurrence was specifically at that time due to the specification of that time with a benefit not found in other times — it is also weak from two aspects: First: Since the world's occurrence depended on the presence of that time specified with that benefit, then the occurrence of that time: if it is from a maker, it is a denial of the Maker; and if it is from a maker, the question returns as to why it did not occur before that time or after it? And infinite regress follows. Also, if the benefit attained in that time was attained before that time, then the purpose for which the world occurred was attained before that time, so necessarily the world would have occurred before it occurred, which is impossible. And if we say that that benefit was not attained before that time but only came into being at that time, then it is said either that benefit occurred at that time necessarily or possibly. If the first, then you have allowed that something occurs by itself; and if that is allowed in some matters, the like is allowed in all, and denial of the Maker follows, which is impossible. If the second, then the specification of that time with that benefit requires another particularizer, and the discussion about it is as in the first, and infinite regress follows, which is also impossible. Second: With knowledge that that time contains that benefit, it is either possible to refrain [from creation] or not. If it is not possible, then the agent is necessary by essence, not freely acting. If it is possible to refrain, then since refraining and acting are both possible with that motive, if it depends on another preponderant, the discussion about it is as in the first, and infinite regress follows; and if it does not depend, then the possible occurs without a preponderant. It cannot be said: The action with this motive becomes more worthy of occurrence, and the priority does not reach the limit of necessity; for we say: The claim of this priority will be refuted, God willing, in the question of the creation of actions.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that the world was created at a specific time because that time had a unique benefit (maslaha). Al-Razi gives two objections. First: The time itself must have been created; if it was created by God, the question recurs as to why it was created then and not earlier or later, leading to infinite regress. Also, if the benefit existed before that time, the world should have been created earlier; if it arose at that time, its origin must be explained: if necessary, then things can occur without cause (leading to atheism); if possible, then its specification requires another cause, again leading to regress. Second: If God knows the benefit, He either can refrain or cannot. If He cannot, He is not free. If He can, then both acting and refraining are possible; if they need a further preponderant, regress; if not, a possible occurs without cause. The objection about priority is deferred to a later discussion.

Key Terms
مصلحة
تسلسل
مرجح
إمكان
وجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The existence of a unique benefit at a specific time explains why God created when He did without further issue.
Correction: Al-Razi shows this leads to either infinite regress or absurdities, thus it is not a valid explanation.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Razi's first objection, if the benefit existed before the specified time, what follows?
A) The world should have been created earlier.
B) The benefit must be uncaused.
C) The benefit is eternal.
D) The time is unnecessary.

Key Takeaway
The first objection shows that appealing to a specific benefit to explain the timing of creation leads to either infinite regress or contradictions, undermining the claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever justified a delay in your life by thinking 'God must have a benefit for me in this timing'? If so, does Al-Razi's argument challenge you to examine whether you are using that belief to avoid surrendering to God's absolute sovereignty? What would it mean for you to accept God's decree without needing to know the benefit?
?
When you face a situation where you cannot see any benefit, do you find yourself tempted to question God's wisdom? This lesson shows that even if a benefit were assumed, it leads to logical impossibilities. Are you willing to stop demanding a reason and instead trust that God's will is beyond your comprehension? How can you cultivate that trust starting today?
?
Al-Razi's argument exposes the danger of trying to rationalize God's actions based on human notions of benefit. Is there an area of your life—such as prayer, trials, or blessings—where you have secretly believed that God's actions must conform to your idea of what is beneficial? What step can you take to let go of that expectation and submit fully to His will?

Second Objection: Divine Freedom and the Necessity of Action
Al-Razi presents the second objection focusing on divine freedom and the need for a preponderant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's second objection, which challenges the benefit-based timing by highlighting that it compromises divine freedom or leads to an uncaused event.

Core Idea
If a benefit necessitates action, God is not free; if it does not, the action requires another cause — both problematic.

Al-Razi now presents his second objection, which focuses on the nature of God's action given the presence of a motive (the benefit). He sets up a dilemma: if God cannot refrain from creating due to the benefit, then He acts by necessity, not free will. If He can refrain, then both acting and refraining are possible with the same motive, requiring an additional preponderant or leading to an uncaused event.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection passage
Read the Arabic and translation from 'والثاني' to the end, noting the dilemma and the final remark about priority.
The passage also includes a deferred discussion about 'priority'.
2
Analyze the necessity branch
If the benefit makes it impossible for God to refrain, then God is not a free agent but a necessary cause.
This contradicts the Islamic view of God as free and choosing.
3
Analyze the possibility branch
If God can refrain, then with the benefit present, both acting and refraining are possible. If acting needs another cause, regress; if not, an uncaused event occurs.
Al-Razi anticipates an objection about 'priority' and defers it.
4
Reflect on the deferred remark
Al-Razi mentions that the claim about priority will be refuted later in the discussion on the creation of actions.
This shows the interconnectedness of theological topics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

intermediate

Why pre-eternity cannot be a preventer
Al-Razi refutes the fourth answer with two rational arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rational arguments that refute the claim that pre-eternity (azal) was a preventer of creation.

Core Idea
Pure negation cannot be a causal preventer; only positive existents can.

This lesson examines the fourth attempted answer to the problem of why God created the world when He did. Some argued that pre-eternity itself was a preventer. Al-Razi shows this is incoherent through two penetrating arguments. The first reduces the notion to a vicious circularity, the second exposes the emptiness of treating non-existence as a cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the two distinct aspects introduced with 'الأول' and 'الثاني'.
2
Understand the first argument
Follow the conditional reasoning: either pre-eternity is necessary or possible. If necessary, its preventiveness cannot cease (contradiction). If possible, it requires a cause, leading to infinite regress until the Necessary Being, at which point the problem recurs.
This argument uses the principle of determination: everything possible needs a cause.
3
Understand the second argument
Grasp that the non-existence of the world before creation is pure negation. Pure negation has no properties or distinctions, so it cannot be a 'preventer' at one time and not another.
This highlights the absurdity of treating absence as a cause.
4
Connect to the broader debate
Reflect on why this refutation is decisive: it removes any rational basis for claiming that pre-eternity delayed creation. The preventer must be a positive reality, but pre-eternity is mere non-existence.
This argument supports the view that God creates when He wills, without external constraints.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب الرابع: وهو أن حضور الأزل كان مانعا من الإيجاد، فهو أيضا ضعيف من وجهين : الأول : أن مسمى الأزل الذي جعلتموه مانعا من الإيجاد، إما أن يكون واجبًا لذاته أو ممكنا لذاته فإن كان الأول امتنع زواله وكان يجب أن لا يزول ذلك الامتناع، هذا خلف وإن كان ممكنا لذاته فلا بد لحصوله من سبب والكلام فيه كما في الأول، ولا ينقطع حتى ينتهي إلى الواجب لذاته، وحينئذ يعود المحذور المذكور. والثاني : أن عدم العالم في الأزل إلى وقت حدوثه، نفى محض، والنفي المحض لا يكون فيه اختلاف وامتياز فامتنع القول بأن ذلك العدم في الأزل مانع من الفعل، وفي لا يزال غير مانع منه.
As for the fourth answer — that the presence of pre-eternity (al-azal) was a preventer of bringing into existence — it is also weak from two aspects: First: The essence of pre-eternity which you made a preventer of bringing into existence: either it is necessary by itself or possible by itself. If the first, its removal is impossible, and that prevention must not cease — this is contradictory. If it is possible by itself, then its occurrence requires a cause, and the discussion about it is as in the first, and it does not stop until it ends at the necessary by itself, and then the mentioned difficulty returns. Second: The non-existence of the world from pre-eternity until the time of its occurrence is pure negation, and pure negation has no differentiation or distinction, so it is impossible to say that that non-existence in pre-eternity is a preventer of the act, and in 'not yet' it is not a preventer.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that the state of pre-eternity (azal) prevented God from creating the world earlier. Al-Razi gives two objections. First: The 'azal' considered as a preventer is either necessary or possible. If necessary, its preventiveness would be eternal and cannot cease (but it did cease with creation), a contradiction. If possible, it requires a cause, leading to regress until a necessary being, and the same problem recurs. Second: The non-existence of the world before its creation is just pure negation. Pure negation has no properties or distinctions; therefore, it cannot be said that the same non-existence was a preventer in pre-eternity but not later. Thus the concept is incoherent.

Key Terms
أزل
مانع
إيجاد
نفي محض
وجوب لذاته
إمكان لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Pre-eternity (azal) is a positive metaphysical state that can act as a causal barrier delaying creation.
Correction: Pre-eternity is merely the non-existence of the world before a certain time; it is pure negation with no causal efficacy. A preventer must be a positive existent.

Checkpoint
What does al-Razi say about pure negation in the second aspect?
A) It can have different properties at different times.
B) It has no differentiation or distinction.
C) It is a positive state.
D) It necessarily prevents action.

Key Takeaway
Pre-eternity as mere non-existence cannot causally prevent divine action; only a positive reality could be a real preventer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you delay an act of obedience to Allah, do you ever convince yourself that 'the time wasn't right' or 'circumstances prevented it'? In light of Al-Razi's argument that pure negation cannot be a real preventer, examine: are those excuses just empty non-existence that you have given power over your actions? What would it mean to stop attributing causal power to absences and instead take responsibility right now?
?
Think of a specific sin you keep postponing repentance for. Could it be that you are treating your past delay as if it were a 'preventer' that makes repentance impossible now? Al-Razi shows that mere non-existence (like the non-existence of your repentance yesterday) has no power to prevent action today. Are you willing to break that illusion and repent without waiting for a 'better time' that is itself pure negation?
?
Al-Razi refutes the notion that pre-eternity itself can be a preventer. In your spiritual life, do you attribute power to 'time' or 'stage of life' as if they were active agents hindering your devotion? Your youth or old age—are they positive realities that genuinely impede you, or are they merely mental labels for absence of action? How would your daily worship change if you stopped assigning causal power to time and instead focused on your own will and God's command?

Refuting the World's Pre-Creation Impossibility
Al-Razi refutes the fifth answer with two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's two refutations of the claim that the world was impossible before existence, focusing on the impossibility of essential change and the permanence of quiddity.

Core Idea
The world's possibility is intrinsic and cannot change.

In this passage, Al-Razi tackles the fifth objection to the createdness of the world: that the world was not possible before existence. He offers two decisive refutations, one based on the impossibility of essential change (inqilab al-haqa'iq) and the other based on the unchanging nature of quiddity. These arguments solidify the doctrine that the world is always possible, and its creation is a matter of divine will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and translation to grasp the overall argument. Notice the two explicit aspects Al-Razi presents.
The passage is concise; each objection is a single sentence.
2
Understand the first objection
Al-Razi argues that if the world changed from impossible to possible, that would require an alteration in its essence, which is impossible (inqilab al-haqa'iq).
This objection uses the principle that essential attributes cannot change.
3
Understand the second objection
The quiddity (mahiyya) of the world does not differ in itself; if it is receptive to existence at one time, it is always receptive, so the world is always possible.
This objection relies on the unchanging nature of quiddity.
4
Reflect on the implications
These two objections show that possibility is an intrinsic attribute, not a temporal one. Consider how this strengthens the argument for the world's createdness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب الخامس: وهو أن العالم ما كان ممكن الوجود قبل أن وجد فهو أيضًا ضعيف من وجهين : الأول : أن هذا يقتضي أن يقال : إنه كان ممتنع الوجود لذاته، ثم انقلب ممكنا لذاته، وهذا يقتضي انقلاب الحقائق، وهو محال. الثاني : أن حال الماهية من حيث هي هي لا تختلف، فإن ثبت كونها قابلة للوجود في بعض الأوقات، ثبت أنها من حيث هي هي قابلة للوجود أبداً فكانت ممكنة أبدا فيمتنع أن يكون حصول ذلك الإمكان مخصوصا بوقت دون وقت.
As for the fifth answer — that the world was not possible in existence before it existed — it is also weak from two aspects: First: This would necessitate saying that it was impossible in existence by itself, then it turned into possible by itself, and this necessitates the alteration of realities (inqilab al-haqa'iq), which is impossible. Second: The state of the essence (mahiyya) in itself does not differ; if it is established that it is receptive to existence at some times, it is established that it is in itself receptive to existence always, so it was possible always, and thus it is impossible that the attainment of that possibility is specific to a time rather than another.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage refutes the idea that the world was impossible (mumtani') before its creation. Al-Razi gives two objections. First: If the world was impossible by essence before existence, then after existence it became possible by essence, which would mean a change in the very essence of the world — this is a change in fundamental realities (inqilab al-haqa'iq), which is impossible. Second: The quiddity (mahiyya) of a thing does not change its intrinsic nature. If at one time it is receptive to existence, then that receptivity is intrinsic to it at all times. Therefore, the world was always possible, and its possibility cannot be confined to a particular moment.

Key Terms
امتناع
إمكان
انقلاب الحقائق
الماهية
قابلية الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the world was 'not yet possible' before creation, meaning possibility is a temporal attribute.
Correction: Possibility is an intrinsic attribute of quiddity; if a thing is possible at any time, it is always possible in itself.

Checkpoint
What does the first objection say about the change from impossibility to possibility?
A) It proves God's omnipotence.
B) It requires the alteration of realities.
C) It is a logical necessity.
D) It shows the world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi demonstrates that the world's possibility is essential and unchanging, refuting the idea that it was impossible before existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe God is all-powerful, but do your daily actions reflect trust that He could have created the world at any moment—or do you still subconsciously think His power is limited by time? How would your reliance on God change if you fully internalized that possibility is essential, not temporal?
?
If the world's possibility is unchanging, then your own existence was always possible in God's knowledge. How does this certainty about God's eternal plan confront your anxiety about the future? Will you let this truth uproot your fears, or will you cling to the illusion that things could have been otherwise?

Why Free Will Needs a Preponderant
Al-Razi refutes the sixth answer: that the free agent can prefer one action without a preponderant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why free choice without a preponderant leads to accident or infinite regress, and why this refutes the claim that God can choose arbitrarily.

Core Idea
Free will without a preponderant is arbitrary, not truly voluntary.

Al-Razi continues his refutation of the sixth answer, which claims that a free agent can simply prefer one action over another without any cause. He presents two compelling objections that expose the logical flaw in this view. This lesson analyzes those objections and their implications for understanding divine free will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two objections
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note that Al-Razi lists two objections against the claim that a free agent can choose without a preponderant. Try to summarize each in your own words before reading the commentary.
The passage is cut off at the end of the second objection; the conclusion is missing but the argument is clear.
2
Understand the first objection: equality leads to accident
If two possibilities are equal and one occurs without any preponderant, the occurrence is accidental (ittifaqi), not volitional (ikhtiyari). This would imply all events could be uncaused, which is absurd.
Accidental here means without purpose or choice.
3
Understand the second objection: the dilemma of requiring a preponderant
The agent either needs a preponderant to choose or not. If needed, the same difficulty returns about why that preponderant was chosen (regress). If not needed, the choice is arbitrary and uncaused.
This is a classic dilemma that challenges the coherence of uncaused choice.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how these objections together undermine the idea that a free agent can act without a reason. This reinforces the need for a preponderant in all volitional acts, including God's.
This argument is foundational in Islamic kalam for establishing that God acts with wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب السادس : وهو قولهم : ' القادر المختار يرجح أحد مقدوريه على الآخر من غير مرجح ' فهو أيضًا ضعيف من وجهين : الأول : أنه لما استوى الأمران بالنسبة إليه ثم وقع أحدهما دون الآخر كان وقوع ذلك الفعل اتفاقيًا لا اختيارياً، وإن جاز ذلك فليجز مثله في سائر الحوادث، وذلك يقتضي استغناء الحدوث عن المرجح. الثاني : وهو أن القادر على الفعل والترك إذا استوى الفعل والترك بالنسبة إليه فإما أن يقال : إنه لا يرجح أحد الطرفين على الآخر إلا إذا خص ذلك القادر ذلك الطرف بنوع ترجيح أو يقال : إنه يرجح أحد الطرفين على الآخر، وإن لم يخص ذلك القادر ذلك الطرف بنوع ترجيح، فإن كان الأول فحينئذ يتوقف رجحان ذلك الطرف على الطرف الآخر على انضياف ذلك المرجح إليه، وحينئذ يعود الإشكال من
As for the sixth answer — their statement: 'The powerful free agent (al-qadir al-mukhtar) gives preponderance to one of his two objects of power over the other without a preponderant (murajjih)' — it is also weak from two aspects: First: When the two matters are equal with respect to him, and then one occurs without the other, the occurrence of that act is accidental, not volitional. And if that is allowed, then let the like be allowed in all events, and that would necessitate that occurrences are independent of a preponderant. Second: If the agent capable of acting and refraining finds acting and refraining equal with respect to him, then either it is said: He does not give preponderance to one side over the other unless that agent specifies that side with some kind of preponderance; or it is said: He gives preponderance to one side over the other even if that agent does not specify that side with any kind of preponderance. If the first, then the preponderance of that side over the other depends on the addition of that preponderant to it, and then the problem returns from
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that God, as a free agent, can simply prefer one action over another without any preponderant factor. Al-Razi gives two objections. First: If both alternatives are equal and one occurs, that occurrence is accidental, not freely chosen. If this is allowed, it would permit all events to occur without cause, which is absurd. Second: The agent either requires a preponderant to choose or not. If a preponderant is needed, the same difficulty recurs about why that preponderant was chosen. If not needed, the choice is arbitrary. The text breaks off here, but the argument is clear: either regress occurs or the choice is uncaused.

Key Terms

مرجح
اتفاقي
اختيار
مقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Free will means choice without any reason or cause.
Correction: Without a preponderant, choice is either accidental or leads to infinite regress; true free will requires a rational preponderant.

Checkpoint
According to the first objection, if two equal possibilities occur without preponderance, what is the result?
A) The act is perfectly free.
B) The act is accidental.
C) The act is necessary.
D) The act is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Free will without a preponderant is logically incoherent; it either results in accident or infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own daily choices, do you often act without any reason, simply because you 'feel like it'? How does this lesson's argument that such acts are 'accidental, not volitional' challenge your understanding of responsibility for those actions?
?
If God's free will required a preponderant to avoid arbitrariness, what does that imply about the nature of God's wisdom? How does this affect your trust that everything He does has a wise purpose, even when you cannot see it?
?

Second Doubt: Divine Causality Distinct from Essence
Al-Razi enumerates four proofs for the distinctness of divine causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that God's efficient causality is a distinct attribute from His essence, based on four proofs.

Core Idea
Causality is a relation distinct from the essences it relates.

Al-Razi presents the second philosophical doubt against the Ash'ari position that God's attributes are identical with His essence. The philosophers argue that the concept of 'being an efficient cause' is a relation or attribute separate from God's essence. They provide four arguments to support this, followed by a potential objection. This passage sets up a key debate on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main thesis
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that the philosophers claim that 'the Necessary Being being an efficient cause' is distinct from both the essence of God and the essence of the world.
The thesis is stated in the first sentence.
2
Examine each of the four proofs
Find each of the four proofs (marked by 'أحدها', 'وثانيها', etc.). For each proof, ask: what is the logical structure? How does it show distinctness?
The fourth proof introduces temporal renewal of causality.
3
Consider the objection and the beginning of the reply
After the fourth proof, an objection is raised: perhaps causality is just the occurrence of the effect. Note how Al-Razi begins to refute it (though the text cuts off).
This objection challenges the need for a separate attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشبهة الثانية لهم في هذه المسألة : قالوا : المفهوم من كون الواجب مؤثرًا في العالم أمر مغاير لذات واجب الوجود، ولذات العالم، ويدل عليه وجوه : أحدها: أنه يمكننا أن نعقل ذات واجب الوجود ونعقل ذات العالم مع الشك في أن واجب الوجود مؤثر في العالم، ومع الشك في أن ذات العالم أثر واجب الوجود، وإذا أمكن تعقل الذاتين حال كون المؤثرية مجهولة، علمنا أن كون الواجب مؤثرًا في وجود العالم وكون العالم أثرًا لواجب الوجود أمر مغاير للذاتين. وثانيها : إن كون أحد الذاتين مؤثرة في الأخرى نسبة بين الذاتين، والنسبة بين الشيئين متوقفة عليهما، والمتوقف على الشيء مغاير له، فهذه المؤثرية والأثرية وصفان مغايران للذاتين . ثالثها: أنا نقول هذه الذات مؤثرة في تلك فنحكم على تلك الذات بالمؤثرية، والمحكوم عليه مغاير للمحكوم به لا محالة. ورابعها : أنا نقول : هذه الذات صارت مؤثرة في الأثر الفلاني، بعد أن كانت غير مؤثرة فيه، فتجدد صفة المؤثرية مع كون الذات موجودة قبل يدل على أن صفة المؤثرية مغايرة لتلك الذات. لا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يكون المراد من كونها مؤثرة في ذلك الأثر نفس حصول ذلك الأثر؟ لأنا نقول : هذا باطل، فإنا نقول : إنما وجد هذا الأثر لأجل أن
And the second doubt of theirs in this issue: they said: The concept of the Necessary Being being an efficient cause (mu'aththir) in the world is something other than (mughtayir) the essence of the Necessary Being and other than the essence of the world, and it is indicated by several proofs. One of them: that we can conceive the essence of the Necessary Being and conceive the essence of the world while doubting whether the Necessary Being is an efficient cause in the world, and while doubting whether the world is an effect of the Necessary Being; and when it is possible to conceive the two essences while the property of being an efficient cause is unknown, we know that the Necessary Being's being an efficient cause in the existence of the world and the world's being an effect of the Necessary Being are things other than the two essences. The second: that one of the two essences being an efficient cause in the other is a relation (nisba) between the two essences, and the relation between two things is dependent on them, and that which is dependent on a thing is other than it; so these—the property of being an efficient cause and the property of being an effect—are two attributes other than the two essences. The third: that we say 'this essence is an efficient cause in that one,' and we predicate efficient causality of that essence, and that which is predicated (al-mahkūm bihi) is necessarily other than that of which it is predicated (al-mahkūm 'alayhi). The fourth: that we say 'this essence became an efficient cause in such-and-such an effect after it was not an efficient cause in it,' so the renewal of the attribute of efficient causality, while the essence pre-exists, indicates that the attribute of efficient causality is other than that essence. It cannot be said: why is it not permissible that what is meant by its being efficient in that effect is just the occurrence of that effect itself? For we say: this is false, because we say: this effect only came into existence because...
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage presents the second doubt of the philosophers, which argues that the property of being an efficient cause (al-mu'aththiriyya) is distinct from both the essence of the Necessary Being and the essence of the world. They give four proofs. First, we can conceive both essences without knowing whether the cause-effect relation holds, indicating the relation is additional. Second, causality is a relation between two entities, and a relation is dependent on the relata, so it is other than them. Third, when we predicate causality of God, the predicate is distinct from the subject. Fourth, if causality was not present and then occurs, it shows it is an attribute that comes and goes, separate from the immutable essence. The passage then begins a potential objection: perhaps 'being an efficient cause' just means the existence of the effect itself. The author starts to refute this but the text cuts off. The argument is incomplete in this window.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
مؤثر
أثر
مغاير
نسبة
محكوم عليه
محكوم به

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's act of creation is identical to His essence.
Correction: The philosophers argue that causality is a relation distinct from essence; otherwise, God would be in time or His essence would change.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof that the efficient causality of the Necessary Being is distinct from His essence?
A) We can conceive God and the world without knowing whether He is the cause.
B) Efficient causality is a relation, and relations are dependent on the relata.
C) We predicate causality of God, and the predicate is distinct from the subject.
D) Causality can renew while the essence remains the same.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers use four arguments to show that divine causality is an attribute distinct from God's essence, challenging the Ash'ari position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe that God causes everything in your life. But do you treat God's causation as an extension of His essence, or as something He does? If it is a separate attribute, then God's essence remains unchanged regardless of His acts. How does this change the way you think about His involvement in your daily struggles? Will you now seek to know His essence or rely more on His actions?
?
The philosophers argue that causality can begin after not existing. Think of a time you felt God acted in your life — was that an addition to God? If not, then how do you reconcile a new divine act with an unchanging God? Does this lead you to deny the reality of new acts, or do you accept them as distinct attributes? How does this affect your gratitude for new blessings?
?
You often say 'God willed it' for events. If God's will to create is distinct from His essence, then His will is not identical to Him. Does this distance you from God, or does it make His attributes more knowable? How will this distinction affect your trust in His decree — are you trusting His essence or His will? What specific change will you make in your reliance on Him after this lesson?

The Four Proofs that Effecterness is Additional
Razi presents the philosophers' four proofs that effecterness is additional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four logical proofs that philosophers use to argue that the attribute of being an effecter (mu'aththiriyya) is additional to the essence of the Necessary Existent.

Core Idea
Effecterness is shown to be an extra attribute through four distinct logical arguments.

The second philosophical objection begins by asserting that the concept of the Necessary Being being an effecter in the world is something additional to both the divine essence and the world. To support this, the philosophers provide four distinct proofs. This lesson examines each proof in detail, showing how they use logical arguments to separate the attribute from the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the four proofs
Read the Arabic passage that lists the four proofs. Notice how each proof is introduced with 'أحدها', 'وثانيها', etc.
2
Understand the first proof
The first proof uses the possibility of doubting effecterness while conceiving the essences. This shows effecterness is not essential.
3
Examine the second proof
The second proof treats effecterness as a relation between two essences. Since the relation depends on its relata, it is additional.
This is a relational argument.
4
Analyze the third proof
The third proof argues that when we predicate effecterness of the essence, the predicate is distinct from the subject.
5
Analyze the fourth proof
The fourth proof notes that effecterness can be renewed while the essence persists, indicating that it is an additional attribute.
Renewal implies distinctness.

والشبهة الثانية لهم في هذه المسألة : قالوا : المفهوم من كون الواجب مؤثرًا في العالم أمر مغاير لذات واجب الوجود، ولذات العالم، ويدل عليه وجوه : أحدها: أنه يمكننا أن نعقل ذات واجب الوجود ونعقل ذات العالم مع الشك في أن واجب الوجود مؤثر في العالم، ومع الشك في أن ذات العالم أثر واجب الوجود، وإذا أمكن تعقل الذاتين حال كون المؤثرية مجهولة، علمنا أن كون الواجب مؤثرًا في وجود العالم وكون العالم أثرًا لواجب الوجود أمر مغاير للذاتين. وثانيها : إن كون أحد الذاتين مؤثرة في الأخرى نسبة بين الذاتين، والنسبة بين الشيئين متوقفة عليهما، والمتوقف على الشيء مغاير له، فهذه المؤثرية والأثرية وصفان مغايران للذاتين . وثالثها: أنا نقول هذه الذات مؤثرة في تلك فنحكم على تلك الذات بالمؤثرية، والمحكوم عليه مغاير للمحكوم به لا محالة. ورابعها : أنا نقول : هذه الذات صارت مؤثرة في الأثر الفلاني، بعد أن كانت غير مؤثرة فيه، فتجدد صفة المؤثرية مع كون الذات موجودة قبل يدل على أن صفة المؤثرية مغايرة لتلك الذات. لا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يكون المراد من كونها مؤثرة في ذلك الأثر نفس حصول ذلك الأثر؟ لأنا نقول : هذا باطل، فإنا نقول : إنما وجد هذا الأثر لأجل أن هذا المؤثر أثر في وجوده فعللنا وجوده لمؤثرية المؤثر فيه، فلو كانت مؤثرية المؤثر عبارة عن وجود الأثر لكان ذلك تعليلا لوجود الأثر بنفسه، وإنه محال، وإذا ثبت أن كون واجب الوجود مؤثرًا في وجود العالم وصف زائد على الذات فنقول: هذا الوصف لا جائز أن يكون وصفًا سلبياً؛ لأن قولنا : الشيء الفلاني أثر في كذا، نقيض لقولنا : إنه ما أثر في كذا ولما كان قولنا : ما أثر في كذا سلبًا محضاً وجب أن يكون قولنا : إنه أثر في كذا وصفًا ثبوتيًا زائدا على الذات. ثم نقول : كون الله تعالى مؤثرًا في وجود العالم إما أن يكون حادثا أو قديماً، فإن كان حادثًا افتقر إلى محدث فكانت مؤثرية المؤثر في إحداث تلك المؤثرية زائدا عليه فيلزم التسلسل، فإذن مؤثرية الله تعالى في العالم صفة قديمة، ولكن كون الشيء مؤثرًا في غيره، صفة إضافية لا يعقل ثبوتها إلا مع ثبوت المضافين، فإذن كون الشيء موصوفًا بصفة المؤثرية من غير وجود الأثر محال عقلاً ، وإذا كانت صفة المؤثرية قديمة يلزم كون الأثر قديماً، وذلك يوجب القول بقدم الآثار.
And the second objection for them in this issue: They said: The concept of the Necessary Being being an effecter in the world is an matter additional to the essence of the Necessary Existent and to the essence of the world. And evidence for it is aspects: One of them: that we can conceive the essence of the Necessary Existent and conceive the essence of the world while being doubtful that the Necessary Existent is an effecter in the world, and while being doubtful that the essence of the world is an effect of the Necessary Existent. And when it is possible to conceive the two essences while the attribute of being an effecter is unknown, we know that the Necessary Being being an effecter in the existence of the world and the world being an effect of the Necessary Existent is an matter additional to the two essences. And the second of them: That one of the two essences being effecter in the other is a relation between the two essences, and the relation between two things is dependent upon them, and that which is dependent upon a thing is additional to it, so this effecterness and effectness are two attributes additional to the two essences. And the third of them: We say: This essence is effecter in that, so we judge upon that essence with effecterness, and the thing judged upon is additional to the thing judged with necessarily. And the fourth of them: We say: This essence became effecter in such an effect after it was not effecter in it, so the attribute of effecterness is renewed while the essence exists before, indicating that the attribute of effecterness is additional to that essence. It should not be said: Why is it not permissible that what is meant by it being effecter in that effect is the very occurrence of that effect? Because we say: This is false, for we say: This effect only came to be because this effecter effected in its existence, so we caused its existence to be for the effecterness of the effecter in it. So if the effecterness of the effecter were an expression for the existence of the effect, that would be a causation of the existence of the effect by itself, and that is impossible. And when it is established that the Necessary Existent being an effecter in the existence of the world is an attribute additional to the essence, we say: This attribute may not be a negative attribute; because our saying: 'This thing is an effect in such-and-such' is the contradictory of our saying: 'It is not an effect in such-and-such'. And since our saying: 'It is not an effect in such-and-such' is pure negation, it is necessary that our saying: 'It is an effect in such-and-such' is a positive attribute additional to the essence. Then we say: God Most High being an effecter in the existence of the world is either originated or eternal. If it is originated, it needs an originator, so the effecterness of the effecter in bringing about that effecterness would be additional to it, leading to infinite regress. Therefore, God's effecterness in the world is an eternal attribute. But a thing being an effecter in another is a relational attribute, whose existence is not conceivable except with the existence of the two relata. Therefore, a thing being described with the attribute of effecterness without the existence of the effect is rationally impossible. And if the attribute of effecterness is eternal, the effect must be eternal, and that necessitates the assertion of the pre-eternity of the effects.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage presents the second philosophical objection against the Ash'ari doctrine of the world's creation. Imam Razi begins by stating the philosophers' claim: the concept of the Necessary Being being an 'effecter' (mu'aththir) in the world is something additional (mugheyir) both to the essence of the Necessary Existent and to the essence of the world. He then lists four proofs (wujuh) they offer. First proof: we can conceive the two essences while doubting the effecterness, showing it is additional. Second proof: effecterness is a relation between two things, and a relation is dependent on its relata, so it is additional. Third proof: we predicate effecterness of the essence, and what is predicated is other than the subject. Fourth proof: effecterness can be renewed (tajaddud) while the essence persists, showing it is additional. Then Razi presents a potential objection (la yuqalu): perhaps 'being an effecter' is nothing but the very occurrence of the effect. He refutes this: if that were so, then explaining the effect's existence by the effecter's effecterness would be explaining a thing by itself, which is impossible. He then argues that this attribute must be positive (thubuti), not negative, because its negation is pure negation. Finally, he draws the theological consequence: God's effecterness is either originated or eternal. If originated, an infinite regress follows. So it must be eternal. But effecterness is a relational attribute (sifa idafiyya) requiring the existence of both relata; if it is eternal, the effect must also be eternal. Thus the philosophers conclude that the world is pre-eternal (qadim). Razi presents this as an objection to be refuted later.

Key Terms
المؤثرية (mu'aththiriyya)
الأثرية (athariyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many proofs does the second philosophical objection present to show that the attribute of being an effecter is additional to the essence?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The philosophers use four independent logical arguments to demonstrate that the attribute of effecterness is an additional attribute, not the essence itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you can conceive of Allah's essence without His being the Creator of the world, does that shake your certainty in His attribute of creation? Where does your trust in Allah's attributes ultimately rest—on logic or on revelation?
?
The philosophers argue that the attribute of effecterness is additional to the essence. How does this affect your understanding of tawhid? Do you find yourself leaning toward the philosophers' view or against it? What is at stake for your faith?
?
Consider the fourth proof: the attribute of effecterness can be renewed while the essence remains. Does this make you think of Allah's acts as separate from His essence? How do you reconcile that with the belief that Allah is perfect and unchanging? Write down one concrete way you will actively affirm that Allah's actions flow from His essence without being identical to it.
