Refuting the Regress Arguments (Second and Third Objections)
Razi answers objections of infinite regress and mental relations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the objections that the proof leads to infinite regress, by clarifying that assent about existence does not posit a second existence and that relations are mental considerations.

Core Idea
Assenting to existence does not create a new existence; relations are mental, not real additions.

The second objection argues that conceptualizing existence and then assenting to it leads to an infinite regress of existences. The third objection extends this to relations like concomitance. Razi answers that assenting to existence is merely affirming that existence has occurred to the essence, not positing another existence. Furthermore, relations are mental considerations, not real attributes added to essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection
Read: 'وأيضًا فإنا نتصور أن الوجود ما هو؟ ثم نصدق بأن ذلك المفهوم حاصل وكائن واقع...' Understand the claim of infinite regress.
2
Read the third objection
Read: 'وأيضًا: فإنا قد نعقل ذاتين مع الذهول...' Note how it extends to relations.
3
Read the answer
Read Razi's response: 'وأما تصديقنا بأن الوجود قد وقع فليس المراد منه أن الوجود حصل له وجود آخر...' and 'وأما حديث اللازمية والملزومية وأمثالها فهي اعتبارات ذهنية...'
4
Analyze the distinction
Notice the difference between real existence (external) and mental considerations. Why are relations considered mental?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة على أن الوجود زائد على الماهية وهو أنه يمكننا أن نعقل الماهية مع الذهول عن وجودها ولو كان الوجود نفس الماهية أو جزءًا من أجزاء الماهية لكان ذلك ممتنعا. فإن قيل : هذه حجة منقوضة على قول الفلاسفة؛ فإن عندهم وجود الباري سبحانه نفس حقيقته، مع أنا نعقل وجوده ولا نعقل ماهيته. وأيضًا فإنا نتصور أن الوجود ما هو؟ ثم نصدق بأن ذلك المفهوم حاصل وكائن واقع والتصور قبل التصديق فيلزم أن يكون تصديقنا بأن الوجود واقع وحاصل دالا على حصول وجود، ويلزم منه التسلسل. وأيضًا: فإنا قد نعقل ذاتين مع الذهول عن كون أحدهما لازما للآخر، أو ملزوما له، أو كون أحدهما مؤثرًا في الآخر، أو أثرًا له، أو كون أحدهما حالا في الآخر، أو محلا له فيلزم أن يكون كون الشيء لازما للآخر وملزوما له، ومؤثرًا فيه وأثرًا له، وحالا فيه ومحلا له زائدًا على الذات، وذلك محال لإفضاء ذلك إلى التسلسل، وأيضًا فهب أن ما ذكرتم يدل على أن الوجود الخارجي زائد على الماهية ولكنه لا يفيد أن الوجود الذهني زائد على الماهية. والجواب : أنه إذا صدق على أحد الأمرين كونه معلومًا وعلى الآخر أنه غير معلوم فلو لم يثبت التغير بينهما، لكان قد صدق على الأمر الواحد أنه معلوم وأنه غير معلوم فيلزم اجتماع النفي والإثبات وإنه محال في بدائه العقول، وإذا كانت هذه المقدمة من أقوى البدهيات كان إيراد النقض عليها تشكيكاً، فلا يستحق الجواب. وأيضا : فعندنا أن وجود الله زائد على ماهيته، وأما تصديقنا بأن الوجود قد وقع فليس المراد منه أن الوجود حصل له وجود آخر، بل المراد منه أن الوجود هل حصل للماهية أم لا؟ وهذا عين الدليل الذي تمسكنا به. وأما حديث اللازمية والملزومية وأمثالها فهي اعتبارات ذهنية بخلاف الوجود الخارجي، فإنه لا يمكن أن يقال : إنه اعتبار ذهني، وإلا لزم أن يقال إنه لا موجود في الأعيان، وأن يقال: وجوده في الأعيان نفس الماهية فحينئذ يرجع ما ذكرتم من اجتماع النفي والإثبات على الشيء الواحد، وهو محال. وأما الوجود الذهني فجوابه أن الماهية كما وجدت في الأذهان عارية عن الوجود الخارجي، فقد وجدت في الأعيان عارية عن الوجود الذهني، وذلك يوجب التغاير.
The third argument that existence is additional to the essence is that we can conceive the essence while being heedless of its existence. If existence were the essence itself or a part of the parts of the essence, that would be impossible. For it is said: This argument is refuted according to the statement of the philosophers, for with them the existence of the Exalted Creator is His very reality, yet we conceive His existence and do not conceive His essence. And also: we imagine what existence is, then we assent that that concept is actual and occurring, and conception is before assent, so it would follow that our assent that existence is actual and occurring indicates the occurrence of existence, leading to infinite regress. And also: we may conceive two essences while being heedless of one being a concomitant of the other, or the other being a concomited, or one being an effect in the other, or an effect of it, or one being in a state in the other, or a locus for it; then it would follow that a thing being a concomitant of another, or a concomited, or an effect in it, or an effect of it, or in a state in it, or a locus for it is additional to the essence, and that is impossible because it leads to infinite regress. And also: suppose what you mention indicates that external existence is additional to the essence, but it does not show that mental existence is additional to the essence. The answer: If 'being known' is true of one of two things and 'being unknown' is true of the other, then if difference between them were not established, it would be true of the same thing that it is known and unknown, necessitating the conjunction of negation and affirmation, which is impossible in the primary intuitions of intellects. Since this premise is among the strongest of self-evident truths, raising a criticism against it is merely sophistry and does not deserve an answer. And also: with us, the existence of God is additional to His essence. As for our assent that existence has occurred, it does not mean that existence has another existence, but rather it means: has existence occurred to the essence or not? This is exactly the argument we relied on. As for the talk of concomitance and being concomited and the like, they are mental considerations, unlike external existence, for it cannot be said that it is a mental consideration, otherwise it would be said that there is no existent in concrete reality, and that its existence in concrete reality is the essence itself; then your conjunction of negation and affirmation on one thing would return, and that is impossible. As for mental existence, its answer is that just as the essence exists in minds stripped of external existence, so it exists in concrete reality stripped of mental existence, and that necessitates difference.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof that existence is additional to essence, followed by a series of objections and a comprehensive answer. The proof itself is concise: we can conceive an essence while being entirely heedless of its existence. If existence were identical to the essence or a part of it, such heedless conception would be impossible. Therefore, existence must be additional. The first objection comes from the philosophers: they hold that for God, existence is identical to His essence, yet we conceive His existence without conceiving His essence. The second objection argues that if we first conceive existence as a concept and then assent that it actually occurs, conception precedes assent, which would imply an infinite regress. The third objection states that we often conceive two things without knowing their relations (concomitance, causality, etc.), so those relations must be additional, leading to regress. The fourth objection concedes that the proof might show external existence is additional, but not mental existence. The answer addresses all objections. It begins by asserting the self-evident principle that if two things are known and unknown respectively, they must be distinct; otherwise contradiction arises. Since this is a primary intuition, objecting to it is mere sophistry. Then it clarifies that God's existence is indeed additional to His essence according to the author's school. Next, it explains that the assent to existence occurring does not posit a second existence; it merely affirms that existence has occurred to the essence—which is precisely the proof. The relations of concomitance etc. are mental considerations, not real additions, unlike external existence. Finally, regarding mental existence: the essence can be in the mind without external existence, and in concrete reality without mental existence, which shows the two are distinct. Thus the third proof stands.

Key Terms
تصور
تصديق
تسلسل
ملازمة
اعتبار ذهني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that assenting to existence requires another existence.
Correction: Razi explains that assent merely affirms existence for the essence, not a second existence.

Checkpoint
How does Razi answer the second objection about infinite regress?
A) He denies that conception precedes assent.
B) He says assent does not posit another existence but affirms existence for the essence.
C) He says regress is not problematic.
D) He changes the subject.

Key Takeaway
Assenting to existence is not positing a second existence; relations are mental considerations, not real additions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm that God exists, do you believe your mental act of assent adds anything to God's reality? How does Razi's answer challenge you to separate your own cognitive processes from the objective truth of God's existence?
?
Think of a time you treated a mental relationship (like causation or love) as if it were a concrete object. How does realizing that relations are merely mental considerations affect the way you view your own thoughts and their connection to the world?
?
If theological concepts like 'necessary existence' are mental considerations, does that diminish their reality for you? Or does it push you to rely more on God's self-disclosure rather than your own mental constructs? What must change in your approach to knowing God?

Distinguishing Mental and External Existence: The Fourth Objection
Razi answers the objection that only external existence is additional

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi addresses the objection that the proof only shows external existence is additional, not mental existence, and why distinguishing the two is crucial.

Core Idea
Essence can exist in mind without external existence, and in reality without mental existence—they are distinct.

A final objection concedes that the proof might show external existence is additional, but it does not prove that mental existence is also additional. Razi's answer points to the mutual exclusivity: an essence can exist in the mind stripped of external existence, and in concrete reality stripped of mental existence. This difference necessitates that mental existence and external existence are both distinct from the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth objection
Read: 'فهب أن ما ذكرتم يدل على أن الوجود الخارجي زائد على الماهية ولكنه لا يفيد أن الوجود الذهني زائد على الماهية.'
2
Read the answer
Read: 'وأما الوجود الذهني فجوابه أن الماهية كما وجدت في الأذهان عارية عن الوجود الخارجي، فقد وجدت في الأعيان عارية عن الوجود الذهني، وذلك يوجب التغاير.'
3
Consider the two modes
An essence can be in the mind without external existence (e.g., a concept of a non-existent thing) and can exist in reality without mental existence (the thing itself). This shows that both mental and external existence are additional to the essence.
4
Synthesize the overall proof
Reflect on how all four objections have been refuted. The third proof stands strong.

The Fourth Proof: God's Power Over All Things
Razi presents the fourth proof from Quran 2:284 that God is powerful over all things, implying essences are originated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses Quran 2:284 to argue that all essences are originated from God's power and were non-existent in pre-eternity.

Core Idea
God's power over all 'things' proves that essences were non-existent before creation.

Razi now presents a scriptural argument for the contingency of essences. By analyzing the divine attribute of power, he shows that the very notion of 'thing' includes essences, and God's power over them necessitates their origination. This passage is a beautiful example of how Qur'anic exegesis (tafsir) meets rational theology (kalam).

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic verse and its literal translation. Notice the key phrase 'over all things powerful'.
The verse is Quran 2:284, often recited to affirm God's omnipotence.
2
Identify the inference about 'thing'
Razi infers that the term 'thing' (shay') includes essences (mahiyyat). Understand why this is significant: if God is powerful over all things, He must be powerful over essences themselves.
This is a linguistic argument about the scope of the word 'thing'.
3
Follow the logical chain
Trace the reasoning: God's power over essences means His power can affect them by constituting or abolishing them. This requires that God's existence precedes the essences, because the effecter must precede the effect.
The logical principle is that the cause must be prior to its effect.
4
Reach the conclusion
Therefore, all essences were pure negation and absolute non-existence in pre-eternity. This supports the Ash'ari doctrine that non-existents are not 'things' and that God creates from nothing.
This refutes the view that essences are eternal.
5
Reflect on the impact
Consider how this proof uses a simple Quranic verse to derive a profound theological conclusion about the nature of creation and God's absolute sovereignty.
It exemplifies how kalam integrates revelation and reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الرابع : قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَاللهُ عَلَى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ قَدِيرُ ﴾ [البَقَرَة : ٢٨٤] وجه الاستدلال بالآية أن اسم الشيء يتناول الماهيات، فوجب أن يكون الله تعالى قادرًا على تلك الماهيات، وإنما يكون قادرًا عليها لو كان لقدرته صلاحية أن تؤثر في تلك الماهيات تقريرًا وإبطالاً، ومتى كان الأمر كذلك كان وجود الله تعالى متقدمًا على تقرير تلك الماهيات، لوجوب تقدم المؤثر على الأثر، ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ثبت أن الماهيات بأسرها نفي محض، وعدم صرف في الأزل، وذلك هو المطلوب.
The fourth proof: His saying, exalted: 'And God is over all things powerful' [al-Baqara 2:284]. The aspect of inference from the verse is that the noun 'thing' encompasses the essences, so it is necessary that God, exalted, be powerful over those essences. He is only powerful over them if His power has the capacity to effect those essences, constituting and abolishing them. And when the matter is thus, the existence of God, exalted, is prior to the constitution of those essences, due to the necessity of the priority of the effecter over the effect. And when the matter is thus, it is established that all essences are pure negation and absolute non-existence in pre-eternity, and that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhan) from the Quran. Razi cites verse 2:284 which states that God is powerful over all things. He argues that the term 'thing' (shay') includes essences (mahiyyat). Therefore, God must be powerful over these essences. For God to be powerful over them, His power must be able to affect them by bringing them into existence or abolishing them. This implies that God's existence precedes the constitution of those essences, because the effecter must precede the effect. Consequently, all essences were pure non-existence in pre-eternity. This supports the Ash'ari position that non-existents are not things, and that God creates from nothing.

Key Terms
الشيء (thing)
قادر (powerful)
تقرير (constitution)
إبطال (abolition)
مؤثر (effecter)
أثر (effect)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the verse refers only to already-existing things, implying that essences exist independently as eternal objects of God's power.
Correction: Razi argues that 'thing' applies to essences themselves, even if they are not yet actual. God's power over them means He can bring them into being, which necessitates that they were originally non-existent.

Checkpoint
What does Razi infer from God being powerful over all things?
A) That all things exist eternally.
B) That God's power precedes all essences.
C) That essences are eternal.
D) That God is not powerful over non-existents.

Key Takeaway
God's power over all things implies that essences themselves are not eternal but originated by His will, being nothing before creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very essence—your 'whatness'—was nothing but pure non-existence before God's power brought it into being, what claim do you have to any inherent worth apart from Him? Will you live today as if your existence is a constant gift from God's hand, or will you fall into the illusion of self-sufficiency?
?
God's power is over all things, including your thoughts, desires, and actions. Do you truly believe that every breath you take is contingent on His will? What would change if you began each moment with the conscious acknowledgment: 'I am nothing without Him'?
?
The proof shows that essences are not eternal but created. If even the very nature of things is determined by God's power, then what about the patterns you cling to—your habits, your identity, your sense of self? Are you willing to surrender the 'essence' of who you think you are to the One who alone defines reality?

Razi's Refutation of Desire/Hatred Argument for Non-Existents
Razi refutes the argument that desire and hatred distinguish non-existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why quiddities cannot be objects of desire or hatred and how Razi dissolves the argument for real distinctions among non-existents.

Core Idea
Desire and hatred target existence, not the unchanging quiddity.

Razi has just refuted two earlier proofs for the reality of non-existents. Now he tackles a third: the argument from divine desire and hatred. The opponents claim that some non-existents are desired (e.g., future events) and some hated (e.g., sins), implying they are distinct entities in the state of non-existence. Razi's response cuts to the core: desire and hatred apply to the coming-into-existence, not to the immutable quiddity itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: the opponent's claim, then Razi's answer.
2
Identify the opponent's claim and Razi's key distinction
The opponents argue that some non-existents are desired and some hated, proving they are distinct. Razi distinguishes between the quiddity (māhiyya) and its becoming existent (ṣayrūra).
The quiddity cannot change, so it cannot be the object of desire or hatred.
3
Understand why quiddities cannot be objects of desire/hatred
Razi states that quiddities are impossible to change or alter (tabaddul wa taghayyur). Desire and hatred are about bringing about existence, which is a change. Since quiddities are immutable, they cannot be objects of these attributes.
Thus, the opponent's proof fails: desire/hatred do not attach to the quiddity itself.
4
Reflect on the final dichotomy
Razi concludes with a logical pair: that which can be an object of desire/hatred (existence) cannot be said to be established in non-existence; and that which is established in non-existence (the quiddity) cannot be an object of desire/hatred.
This dissolves the argument by showing the opponent conflated two different things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما دليلهم الثالث : على قولهم : المعدومات يتميز بعضها عن البعض، وهو التمسك بكون بعض المعدومات مرادًا وبعضها مكروها. فجوابه أن الماهيات يمتنع عليها التبدل والتغير فيمتنع كونها متعلقة للإرادة والكراهة، بل متعلق الإرادة والكراهة صيرورتها موجودة لكن صيرورتها موجودة لا يتقرر حال العدم، فعاد ما ذكرنا من أن الذي يمكن جعله متعلق الإرادة والكراهة لا يمكن القول بثبوته في العدم، والذي يمكن القول بثبوته في العدم لا يمكن جعله متعلق الإرادة والكراهة.
As for their third proof—for their claim that non-existents are distinguished from each other—it is the adherence to the fact that some non-existents are desired and some are hated. The answer: quiddities are impossible to change and alter, so it is impossible for them to be objects of desire and hatred. Rather, the object of desire and hatred is their becoming existent, but their becoming existent is not constituted in the state of non-existence. So what we mentioned returns: that which can be made an object of desire and hatred cannot be said to be established in non-existence, and that which can be said to be established in non-existence cannot be made an object of desire and hatred.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the argument that non-existents are distinguished because some are desired (murād) and some hated (makrūh). Razi's answer is that quiddities (māhiyyāt) are immutable and cannot be subject to change, so they cannot be objects of desire or hatred. The true object of desire and hatred is the act of bringing the quiddity into existence (ṣayrūratuhā mawjūda). But this 'becoming existent' is not established in non-existence; it is a temporal event. Thus, whatever can be an object of desire/hatred cannot be fixed in non-existence, and whatever is fixed in non-existence cannot be an object of desire/hatred. The argument fails to distinguish between the quiddity and its existence.

Key Terms
إرادة (irāda)
كراهة (karāha)
ماهية (māhiyya)
صيرورة (ṣayrūra)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because God desires some things to exist, those desired things must have some reality before existence.
Correction: Razi clarifies that the desire relates to the coming-into-existence, not to the quiddity. The quiddity itself remains immutable and is not the object of desire.

Checkpoint
Why can quiddities not be objects of desire or hatred, according to Razi?
A) Because desire and hatred are attributes of God only.
B) Because quiddities are immutable and cannot change.
C) Because quiddities do not exist at all.
D) Because desire and hatred only apply to existing things.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's argument fails because desire and hatred relate to the act of existing, not to the quiddity itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi teaches that your desire is not for the unchanging essence of what you want, but for its coming into existence. When you deeply desire something, do you recognize that your attachment is to the act of it existing—and that once it exists, your desire may shift? How does this distinction change the way you evaluate your own desires and their fulfillment?
?
If quiddities are immutable, what does that say about your own core essence? Are you comfortable with the idea that your true identity is fixed, or do you try to redefine yourself through the things you bring into existence? How can you focus on aligning your actions (becoming) with your unchanging nature (quiddity) rather than chasing fleeting states?
?
Razi argues that what is fixed in non-existence cannot be an object of desire or hatred. Is there something you are holding onto or regretting that exists only in your mind (like a future possibility or a past event) rather than in reality? How can you release attachment to what is not actual and redirect your will only toward what can truly be brought into existence?

The Term 'Thing' and Non-Existent Quiddities
Razi argues that a Quranic verse implies calling non-existent a 'thing' but not conferring quiddity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that the term 'thing' can apply to non-existent entities without implying they have a quiddity or essence.

Core Idea
Calling something a 'thing' doesn't make it real; only God's power brings quiddities into existence.

In this passage, Razi engages in a key debate about the term 'thing' (شيء). Does it apply only to existent entities, or can it also refer to non-existents? He cites two Quranic verses: one suggests 'thing' can refer to a future action, the other asserts God's power over all things. Razi argues that the former only shows verbal application, while the latter proves that quiddities are created by God and not eternal. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Note the Quranic verses cited and the key terms like شيئ, معدوم, ذات, ماهية.
Pay attention to the concise style of kalam argumentation.
2
Identify the two verses and their implications
The first verse (Al-Kahf 18:23-24) is used to show that 'thing' can refer to a non-existent future action. The second (Al-Baqarah 2:284) is used to argue that quiddities occur by God's power, negating their pre-eternity.
Razi distinguishes between mere verbal usage and meaningful implication.
3
Understand Razi's conclusion
Razi concludes that his proof (based on meaning) is stronger than the opponent's (based on wording). The non-existent may be called a 'thing' but has no essence.
This supports the Ash'ari position that non-existents are not entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية: ذَلِكَ غَدًا إِلَّا أَن يَشَاءَ اللَّهُ [الكهف: 23-24]. فهذا يقتضي إطلاق اسم الشيء على المعدوم، ولا يقتضي كون المعدوم ذاتا وماهية وحقيقة، وأما قوله تعالى: ﴿وَاللَّهُ عَلَى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ قَدِيرٌ﴾ [البَقَرَة: 284] فإنه يقتضي وقوع الماهيات بالقادر، وذلك يفيد نفي كون الذوات والماهيات متحققة في الأزل. والآية التي عول الخصم عليها لا تفيد إلا مجرد اللفظ، والآية التي عولنا عليها تفيد المعنى، فكان قولنا أولى.
The Second Issue: '...that tomorrow except if Allah wills' [Al-Kahf 18:23-24]. So this necessitates applying the name 'thing' to the non-existent, but does not necessitate that the non-existent has a self (dhat), quiddity (mahiyya), and reality (haqiqa). And as for His statement, 'And Allah over everything is All-Powerful' [Al-Baqarah 2:284], it necessitates the occurrence of quiddities by the Powerful, and that indicates the negation of selves and quiddities being realized in pre-eternity. The verse which the opponent relied upon yields only mere wording, while the verse we relied upon yields meaning, so our statement is more worthy.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the application of the term 'thing' (شيء) to the non-existent (المعدوم). Razi begins by citing a Quranic verse (Al-Kahf 18:23-24) which commands saying 'except if Allah wills' regarding a future action, implying that the future action can be called a 'thing' even though it does not yet exist. However, he clarifies that this does not mean the non-existent has an essence (ذات), quiddity (ماهية), or reality (حقيقة). He then cites another verse (Al-Baqarah 2:284) that 'Allah over everything is All-Powerful.' Razi argues that this necessitates that quiddities come into existence by God's power, which negates the idea that selves and quiddities are eternal. He concludes that the opponent's verse only gives a verbal indication, while his verse provides a meaningful argument, making his position stronger.

Key Terms
شيء
معدوم
ذات
ماهية
حقيقة
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'thing' always implies existence and quiddity.
Correction: Razi shows that 'thing' can apply to non-existents without granting them quiddity; God's power brings quiddities into existence.

Checkpoint
What does Razi argue the verse 'Allah over everything is All-Powerful' (Al-Baqarah 2:284) imply?
A) Things exist eternally in God's knowledge.
B) God's power applies only to things that already exist.
C) Quiddities occur by God's power and are not pre-eternal.
D) Non-existent things are not subject to divine power.

Key Takeaway
The term 'thing' can apply to non-existents, but only God's power brings quiddities into existence; thus, non-existents have no essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan for tomorrow, do you treat your future actions as though they already have a reality fixed in pre-eternity? How does Razi's argument challenge that assumption?
?
This passage shows that only Allah's power brings quiddities into existence. In which areas of your life do you secretly believe you have the power to create your own essence or worth?
?
The opponents relied on a mere word; Razi insisted on meaning. When you interpret the Qur'an, do you ever cling to a word while ignoring the deeper significance revealed by other verses? How might you change that?

The Dilemma of Finite vs Infinite Precedence
Dilemma leading to five arguments against infinite precedence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial dilemma that if God's precedence over the world is finite, it implies both are originated, and if infinite, it is impossible.

Core Idea
Precedence must be either finite or infinite; both options lead to absurdity.

The opponents begin by posing a logical dilemma: if God precedes the world, the duration of that precedence must be either finite or infinite. If finite, then both God and the world would be originated—contradicting God's eternity. If infinite, then that too is impossible, as they will argue. This lesson sets up the framework for the five arguments that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the dilemma statement
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice how it formulates a binary choice: finitude or infinitude of precedence.
The dilemma is posed to challenge the Islamic claim of a pre-eternal God and a temporal world.
2
Understand the first option
If precedence is finite, then both God and world would have a beginning in time, making both originated—impossible for God.
3
Understand the second option
If precedence is infinite, the opponents claim it is impossible for five reasons. This lesson only introduces that claim.
The specific reasons are covered in subsequent lessons.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself: why would opponents present this dilemma? It forces a choice between two seemingly impossible alternatives.
This is a classic dialectical move in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الرابعة. تقولون: الإله قديم والعالم محدث، وإن كان أحدهما قبل الآخر فتقدم أحدهما على الآخر إما أن يكون بمقدار متناه أو غير متناه، فإن كان بمقدار متناه كان كل واحد منهما محدثاً، وذلك محال، وإن كان بمقدار غير متناه فذلك محال لوجوه. الأول: أنه على هذا التقدير قد يكون انقضاء قبل اليوم مدة غير متناهية، وانقضاء ما لا نهاية له غير معقول. الثاني: أنه إن كان يتوقف حصول اليوم على أن يقضي قبله ما لا نهاية - وانقضاء غير المتناهي محال، والموقوف على المحال محال ـ فوجب أن يمتنع حصول اليوم، وحيث حصل اليوم علمنا أن المنقضي متناه لا غير متناه. الثالث: أن المنقضي من زمان الطوفان إلى الأزل أقل من المنقضي من زماننا هذا إلى الأزل، فإذا طبقنا بين هاتين الجملتين من هذين الطرفين اللذين يلياننا وجب أن يظهر التفاوت من طرف الأزل وإلا لكان البعض مثل الكل، فتكون المدة المنقضية متناهية، فيكون تقدم الله تعالى على العالم متناهيا. الرابع: أنه لما كان تقدم الله على العالم أمرًا واجب الثبوت، وهذا التقدم لا يتقرر إلا بهذه المدة كان دوام ذات الله تعالى واستمرار بقائه مفتقرًا إلى تحقيق هذه المدة، والمفتقر إلى الغير ممكن لذاته، فيلزم أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته ممكن الوجود لذاته، وهذا خلف. الخامس: أن المدة لا تُعقل إلا بتعاقب الآنات وتوالي الأزمنة والساعات، فلو كان الباري تعالى متقدمًا على العالم بمدة لا نهاية لها ولا أول لها لزم حصول الأزمنة المتوالية في الأزل، وذلك محال؛ لأن توالي بعض الأزمنة بالبعض يقتضي كون كل واحد منها مسبوقاً بغيره، والأزل عبارة عن نفي المسبوقية بالغير، والجمع بينهما متناقض.
The fourth problem. You say: The god is eternal and the world is originated, and if one of them is before the other, then the precedence of one over the other is either by a finite measure or an infinite measure. If it is by a finite measure, then each of them would be originated, and that is impossible. And if it is by an infinite measure, then that is impossible for reasons. First: that on this assumption, there would have elapsed before today an infinite duration, and the elapsing of what has no end is inconceivable. Second: that if the coming of today is dependent on the elapsing before it of what has no end—and the elapsing of the infinite is impossible, and that which depends on the impossible is impossible—then the coming of today must be impossible; and since today has come, we know that what has elapsed is finite, not infinite. Third: that what has elapsed from the time of the Flood to pre-eternity is less than what has elapsed from our time to pre-eternity; so if we juxtapose these two sums from these two ends that are near us, the difference must appear from the side of pre-eternity, otherwise the part would be like the whole; thus the elapsed duration is finite, so the precedence of God Most High over the world is finite. Fourth: that since the precedence of God over the world is a necessarily established matter, and this precedence is not constituted except by this duration, then the perpetuity of the essence of God Most High and the continuity of His existence would be dependent on the realization of this duration; and that which depends on another is possible in itself, so it would follow that the Necessary Existent in itself is possible in itself, and this is a contradiction (khulf). Fifth: that duration is not conceivable except by the succession of moments and the sequence of times and hours. So if the Creator Most High were prior to the world by a duration having no end and no beginning, it would necessitate the occurrence of successive times in pre-eternity, and that is impossible; because the succession of some times by others requires that each one of them be preceded by another, and pre-eternity (al-azal) is an expression of the negation of being preceded by another, and combining the two is contradictory.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is an objection raised by opponents (likely philosophers) against the Islamic claim that God is eternal (qadim) and the world is originated (muhdath). The objection proceeds by presenting a dilemma: if God precedes the world, the duration of that precedence must be either finite or infinite. If finite, then both would be originated (since each would have a beginning in time). If infinite, then impossible for five reasons. Each reason is a distinct logical argument. The first argument states that an infinite past would mean an infinite duration has elapsed before today, but an infinite cannot be completed. The second argues that if today depends on the completion of an infinite past, and the completion of the infinite is impossible, then today could not occur; but today does occur, so the past must be finite. The third uses a comparison between two periods (from the Flood to pre-eternity and from now to pre-eternity) to show that the elapsed time must be finite, otherwise part would equal whole. The fourth argues that if God's precedence depends on a duration, then God's eternity would be contingent on that duration, implying that the Necessary Existent is contingent—a contradiction. The fifth argues that duration consists of successive moments; an infinite past would mean successive moments in pre-eternity, but pre-eternity by definition negates being preceded, so an infinite sequence in pre-eternity is contradictory.

Key Terms
قِدَم (qidam)
حُدوث (huduth)
تناهي (tanahi)
لاتناهي (la tanahi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the dilemma, what would it mean if the precedence of God over the world were finite?
A) God would be eternal and the world originated.
B) Both God and the world would be originated.
C) The world would be eternal.
D) God's precedence would be infinite.

Key Takeaway
The opponents force a choice: finite precedence makes God temporal, infinite precedence is claimed impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that God's existence has no beginning, and your own existence began after an infinite stretch of His precedence, does that make you feel small or accountable? How does that truth change the way you live this moment?
?
The opponent claims that infinite precedence is impossible. Have you ever struggled to fully accept that God is beyond time because your mind cannot grasp an infinite past? What do you do when your reason clashes with revelation in this way?

First Argument: Elapsing Infinity is Inconceivable
First argument against infinite precedence: elapsing infinite is inconceivable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument that an infinite duration cannot be completed because it has no end.

Core Idea
An infinite past cannot be completely traversed.

The first reason against infinite precedence is straightforward: if an infinite duration had elapsed before today, that would mean the completion of something without an end. But 'elapsing' implies reaching an end. An infinite by definition has no end, so its elapsing is inconceivable. This lesson examines that claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the first argument
Read the Arabic passage for the first reason. Note the key phrase 'انقضاء ما لا نهاية له غير معقول' (the elapsing of what has no end is inconceivable).
2
Understand the concept of elapsing
Elapsing means a process that reaches a certain point. For an infinite duration to have elapsed, it must have been completed, but completion requires an end.
This is a classic argument against an actual infinite past.
3
See the logical structure
The argument is: if infinite precedence, then an infinite duration has elapsed. But elapsing the infinite is impossible. Therefore infinite precedence is impossible.
This is a simple modus tollens.
4
Consider potential counterarguments
Some philosophers reply that an infinite past does not need to be 'completed' because it is infinite in both directions. Discuss whether this rebuttal is valid.
This lesson does not include the counter, but you can think about it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الرابعة. تقولون: الإله قديم والعالم محدث، وإن كان أحدهما قبل الآخر فتقدم أحدهما على الآخر إما أن يكون بمقدار متناه أو غير متناه، فإن كان بمقدار متناه كان كل واحد منهما محدثاً، وذلك محال، وإن كان بمقدار غير متناه فذلك محال لوجوه. الأول: أنه على هذا التقدير قد يكون انقضاء قبل اليوم مدة غير متناهية، وانقضاء ما لا نهاية له غير معقول. الثاني: أنه إن كان يتوقف حصول اليوم على أن يقضي قبله ما لا نهاية - وانقضاء غير المتناهي محال، والموقوف على المحال محال ـ فوجب أن يمتنع حصول اليوم، وحيث حصل اليوم علمنا أن المنقضي متناه لا غير متناه. الثالث: أن المنقضي من زمان الطوفان إلى الأزل أقل من المنقضي من زماننا هذا إلى الأزل، فإذا طبقنا بين هاتين الجملتين من هذين الطرفين اللذين يلياننا وجب أن يظهر التفاوت من طرف الأزل وإلا لكان البعض مثل الكل، فتكون المدة المنقضية متناهية، فيكون تقدم الله تعالى على العالم متناهيا. الرابع: أنه لما كان تقدم الله على العالم أمرًا واجب الثبوت، وهذا التقدم لا يتقرر إلا بهذه المدة كان دوام ذات الله تعالى واستمرار بقائه مفتقرًا إلى تحقيق هذه المدة، والمفتقر إلى الغير ممكن لذاته، فيلزم أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته ممكن الوجود لذاته، وهذا خلف. الخامس: أن المدة لا تُعقل إلا بتعاقب الآنات وتوالي الأزمنة والساعات، فلو كان الباري تعالى متقدمًا على العالم بمدة لا نهاية لها ولا أول لها لزم حصول الأزمنة المتوالية في الأزل، وذلك محال؛ لأن توالي بعض الأزمنة بالبعض يقتضي كون كل واحد منها مسبوقاً بغيره، والأزل عبارة عن نفي المسبوقية بالغير، والجمع بينهما متناقض.
The fourth problem. You say: The god is eternal and the world is originated, and if one of them is before the other, then the precedence of one over the other is either by a finite measure or an infinite measure. If it is by a finite measure, then each of them would be originated, and that is impossible. And if it is by an infinite measure, then that is impossible for reasons. First: that on this assumption, there would have elapsed before today an infinite duration, and the elapsing of what has no end is inconceivable. Second: that if the coming of today is dependent on the elapsing before it of what has no end—and the elapsing of the infinite is impossible, and that which depends on the impossible is impossible—then the coming of today must be impossible; and since today has come, we know that what has elapsed is finite, not infinite. Third: that what has elapsed from the time of the Flood to pre-eternity is less than what has elapsed from our time to pre-eternity; so if we juxtapose these two sums from these two ends that are near us, the difference must appear from the side of pre-eternity, otherwise the part would be like the whole; thus the elapsed duration is finite, so the precedence of God Most High over the world is finite. Fourth: that since the precedence of God over the world is a necessarily established matter, and this precedence is not constituted except by this duration, then the perpetuity of the essence of God Most High and the continuity of His existence would be dependent on the realization of this duration; and that which depends on another is possible in itself, so it would follow that the Necessary Existent in itself is possible in itself, and this is a contradiction (khulf). Fifth: that duration is not conceivable except by the succession of moments and the sequence of times and hours. So if the Creator Most High were prior to the world by a duration having no end and no beginning, it would necessitate the occurrence of successive times in pre-eternity, and that is impossible; because the succession of some times by others requires that each one of them be preceded by another, and pre-eternity (al-azal) is an expression of the negation of being preceded by another, and combining the two is contradictory.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is an objection raised by opponents (likely philosophers) against the Islamic claim that God is eternal (qadim) and the world is originated (muhdath). The objection proceeds by presenting a dilemma: if God precedes the world, the duration of that precedence must be either finite or infinite. If finite, then both would be originated (since each would have a beginning in time). If infinite, then impossible for five reasons. Each reason is a distinct logical argument. The first argument states that an infinite past would mean an infinite duration has elapsed before today, but an infinite cannot be completed. The second argues that if today depends on the completion of an infinite past, and the completion of the infinite is impossible, then today could not occur; but today does occur, so the past must be finite. The third uses a comparison between two periods (from the Flood to pre-eternity and from now to pre-eternity) to show that the elapsed time must be finite, otherwise part would equal whole. The fourth argues that if God's precedence depends on a duration, then God's eternity would be contingent on that duration, implying that the Necessary Existent is contingent—a contradiction. The fifth argues that duration consists of successive moments; an infinite past would mean successive moments in pre-eternity, but pre-eternity by definition negates being preceded, so an infinite sequence in pre-eternity is contradictory.

Key Terms
انقضاء (inqida: elapsing)
لا نهاية (la nihaya: no end)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the first argument claim about an infinite duration?
A) It is possible if God is eternal.
B) Its elapsing is inconceivable.
C) It requires a beginning.
D) It is finite.

Key Takeaway
An infinite duration cannot have elapsed because it has no endpoint to traverse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You live in time, limited by hours and days. The argument says an infinite past cannot be traversed. What does it mean for you that your Creator is not bound by time? How does that change how you think about your own finite life?
?
This argument shows that God's existence cannot be preceded by an infinite duration. Are you living as if you have infinite time, delaying repentance and good deeds? How does the finitude of your time (from your birth to now) demand a response to the timeless Lord?
?
The elapsing of an infinite duration is inconceivable. Yet you have a finite duration from your birth to today. Are you using that finite duration to seek the One who is beyond time? What concrete step will you take today to acknowledge His priority in your life?

Second Argument: Dependency on the Impossible
Second argument: today's occurrence proves past is finite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument that if today depends on the completion of an infinite past, then today could not occur, but since it does, the past must be finite.

Core Idea
Today's existence proves the past is finite.

The second argument builds on the first: if the occurrence of today depends on the prior elapsing of an infinite duration, and that elapsing is impossible, then today itself would be impossible. But today obviously exists, so the past cannot be infinite. This is a reductio ad absurdum that uses observable reality to refute an infinite past.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument
Read the Arabic passage. Notice the conditional: if today depends on infinite elapsed time, and that is impossible, then today is impossible.
2
Identify the dependency
The argument assumes that for today to occur, all previous days must have elapsed. If that series is infinite, it cannot be completed.
This is similar to the 'traversal argument'.
3
Apply modus tollens
If P (infinite past) then Q (today's occurrence impossible). Not Q (today exists). Therefore not P.
This is a valid logical form.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Since today is here, the past must have a finite extent. This supports the Islamic view that the world had a beginning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الرابعة. تقولون: الإله قديم والعالم محدث، وإن كان أحدهما قبل الآخر فتقدم أحدهما على الآخر إما أن يكون بمقدار متناه أو غير متناه، فإن كان بمقدار متناه كان كل واحد منهما محدثاً، وذلك محال، وإن كان بمقدار غير متناه فذلك محال لوجوه. الأول: أنه على هذا التقدير قد يكون انقضاء قبل اليوم مدة غير متناهية، وانقضاء ما لا نهاية له غير معقول. الثاني: أنه إن كان يتوقف حصول اليوم على أن يقضي قبله ما لا نهاية - وانقضاء غير المتناهي محال، والموقوف على المحال محال ـ فوجب أن يمتنع حصول اليوم، وحيث حصل اليوم علمنا أن المنقضي متناه لا غير متناه. الثالث: أن المنقضي من زمان الطوفان إلى الأزل أقل من المنقضي من زماننا هذا إلى الأزل، فإذا طبقنا بين هاتين الجملتين من هذين الطرفين اللذين يلياننا وجب أن يظهر التفاوت من طرف الأزل وإلا لكان البعض مثل الكل، فتكون المدة المنقضية متناهية، فيكون تقدم الله تعالى على العالم متناهيا. الرابع: أنه لما كان تقدم الله على العالم أمرًا واجب الثبوت، وهذا التقدم لا يتقرر إلا بهذه المدة كان دوام ذات الله تعالى واستمرار بقائه مفتقرًا إلى تحقيق هذه المدة، والمفتقر إلى الغير ممكن لذاته، فيلزم أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته ممكن الوجود لذاته، وهذا خلف. الخامس: أن المدة لا تُعقل إلا بتعاقب الآنات وتوالي الأزمنة والساعات، فلو كان الباري تعالى متقدمًا على العالم بمدة لا نهاية لها ولا أول لها لزم حصول الأزمنة المتوالية في الأزل، وذلك محال؛ لأن توالي بعض الأزمنة بالبعض يقتضي كون كل واحد منها مسبوقاً بغيره، والأزل عبارة عن نفي المسبوقية بالغير، والجمع بينهما متناقض.
The fourth problem. You say: The god is eternal and the world is originated, and if one of them is before the other, then the precedence of one over the other is either by a finite measure or an infinite measure. If it is by a finite measure, then each of them would be originated, and that is impossible. And if it is by an infinite measure, then that is impossible for reasons. First: that on this assumption, there would have elapsed before today an infinite duration, and the elapsing of what has no end is inconceivable. Second: that if the coming of today is dependent on the elapsing before it of what has no end—and the elapsing of the infinite is impossible, and that which depends on the impossible is impossible—then the coming of today must be impossible; and since today has come, we know that what has elapsed is finite, not infinite. Third: that what has elapsed from the time of the Flood to pre-eternity is less than what has elapsed from our time to pre-eternity; so if we juxtapose these two sums from these two ends that are near us, the difference must appear from the side of pre-eternity, otherwise the part would be like the whole; thus the elapsed duration is finite, so the precedence of God Most High over the world is finite. Fourth: that since the precedence of God over the world is a necessarily established matter, and this precedence is not constituted except by this duration, then the perpetuity of the essence of God Most High and the continuity of His existence would be dependent on the realization of this duration; and that which depends on another is possible in itself, so it would follow that the Necessary Existent in itself is possible in itself, and this is a contradiction (khulf). Fifth: that duration is not conceivable except by the succession of moments and the sequence of times and hours. So if the Creator Most High were prior to the world by a duration having no end and no beginning, it would necessitate the occurrence of successive times in pre-eternity, and that is impossible; because the succession of some times by others requires that each one of them be preceded by another, and pre-eternity (al-azal) is an expression of the negation of being preceded by another, and combining the two is contradictory.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is an objection raised by opponents (likely philosophers) against the Islamic claim that God is eternal (qadim) and the world is originated (muhdath). The objection proceeds by presenting a dilemma: if God precedes the world, the duration of that precedence must be either finite or infinite. If finite, then both would be originated (since each would have a beginning in time). If infinite, then impossible for five reasons. Each reason is a distinct logical argument. The first argument states that an infinite past would mean an infinite duration has elapsed before today, but an infinite cannot be completed. The second argues that if today depends on the completion of an infinite past, and the completion of the infinite is impossible, then today could not occur; but today does occur, so the past must be finite. The third uses a comparison between two periods (from the Flood to pre-eternity and from now to pre-eternity) to show that the elapsed time must be finite, otherwise part would equal whole. The fourth argues that if God's precedence depends on a duration, then God's eternity would be contingent on that duration, implying that the Necessary Existent is contingent—a contradiction. The fifth argues that duration consists of successive moments; an infinite past would mean successive moments in pre-eternity, but pre-eternity by definition negates being preceded, so an infinite sequence in pre-eternity is contradictory.

Key Terms
توقف (tawaqquf: dependence)
محال (muhal: impossible)
انقضاء (inqida: elapsing)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
On what does the coming of today depend according to this argument?
A) The will of God
B) The elapsing of an infinite duration before it
C) The creation of the world
D) The finitude of the past

Key Takeaway
Because today exists, the past cannot be infinite—otherwise today would be impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If today's existence proves that your past is finite, then your days are countable. How does this truth confront your habit of postponing repentance? What will you do today to prove you truly believe your time is limited?
?
You have experienced today, confirming the argument. If you had been living as if the past were infinite—never accountable—what would you be doing differently now? Name one concrete change you will make because you know the past is finite.
?
The argument shows that infinite sequences cannot be completed. Think of a cycle of sin and repentance you've repeated 'infinitely' in your mind. How will you break that cycle with a single, finite, decisive action today?

Third Argument: Part-Whole Comparison
Third argument: comparing two infinite periods leads to contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument that comparing two periods from different starting points shows the past must be finite, otherwise part would equal whole.

Core Idea
If the past were infinite, the part would equal the whole, which is absurd.

The third argument uses a clever comparison. Take the period from the Flood to pre-eternity (azal) and the period from now to pre-eternity. The second is longer, but if both are infinite, they would be equal in length. That would imply the part equals the whole, which is impossible for infinite magnitudes. Therefore, the past must be finite.

Guided Path
1
Read the third argument
Read the Arabic. Note the two periods: from the Flood to azal and from now to azal.
2
Understand the comparison
The period from now to azal includes the Flood to azal plus the time from Flood to now. So the first is a part of the second.
3
Apply the impossibility of part equals whole
If both periods are infinite, they would have the same size, but one is longer. This contradicts the axiom that a part is less than the whole.
This assumes that infinite magnitudes cannot have different sizes, a controversial point.
4
Draw the conclusion
Therefore, the durations must be finite, implying a finite precedence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

Fourth Argument: God's Eternity Would Be Contingent
Fourth argument: God's necessity would be undermined by dependence on duration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument that if God's precedence depends on a duration, then God's existence would be contingent on that duration, contradicting His necessary existence.

Core Idea
Dependence on a duration would make the Necessary Existent contingent.

The fourth argument takes a different approach: if God's precedence over the world is constituted by a duration, then God's eternity and continued existence would be dependent on that duration. Anything dependent on something else is contingent (possible in itself). So the Necessary Existent would be contingent—a contradiction. Therefore, the precedence cannot be infinite (nor finite, for that matter).

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth argument
Read the Arabic. Notice the conditional: if precedence is established by a duration, then God's permanence depends on that duration.
2
Understand the meaning of 'dependent'
God's precedence is not just a temporal priority but an ontological priority. If that priority is mediated by a time duration, then God's existence becomes dependent on that time.
3
Apply the principle: dependent things are possible
Whatever depends on another is possible in itself (mumkin li-dhatihi). Therefore, God would be possible, not necessary.
This is a key kalam distinction: the necessary (wajib) cannot depend on anything.
4
Recognize the conclusion
This leads to a contradiction (khulf): the Necessary Existent would be contingent. So infinite precedence is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the truth that God's existence depends on nothing, not even time, challenge the way you worry about your future? Are you holding onto the illusion that time controls your outcomes, rather than trusting in the Necessity of God?
?
This argument shows that God's priority over the world is not a temporal interval. Have you been thinking of God as existing 'a long time ago' rather than as the eternal present? What would change in your worship if you truly felt God's 'now'?
?
If you really internalize that God is not subject to time, does that change your impatience? Are there prayers you've given up on because 'it's been too long'? How can you realign your trust with God's timeless sovereignty?

Fifth Argument: Succession in Pre-Eternity is Contradictory
Fifth argument: infinite succession in pre-eternity is self-contradictory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth argument that an infinite succession of moments in pre-eternity contradicts the definition of pre-eternity as having no predecessor.

Core Idea
An infinite sequence of moments cannot exist in pre-eternity, which by definition negates succession.

The final argument focuses on the nature of time and pre-eternity (azal). Duration consists of successive moments. If God's precedence were infinite, then an infinite series of moments would have occurred in pre-eternity. But 'pre-eternity' (azal) means the absence of any preceding thing—it negates the concept of succession. To have both would be contradictory. Hence, infinite precedence is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the fifth argument
Read the Arabic. Note the definition of duration as succession (تعاقب الآنات) and azal as negation of precedence.
2
Understand the contradiction
If there is a succession of moments, each moment is preceded by another. This series would extend infinitely into the past. But azal means 'no predecessor'. So an infinite series in azal would mean each moment has a predecessor, contradicting azal.
3
Recognize the nature of azal
Azal is often defined as the state of having no beginning. It is not a time period but a negation of temporal priority.
4
Conclusion
Therefore, an infinite duration of precedence is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الرابعة. تقولون: الإله قديم والعالم محدث، وإن كان أحدهما قبل الآخر فتقدم أحدهما على الآخر إما أن يكون بمقدار متناه أو غير متناه، فإن كان بمقدار متناه كان كل واحد منهما محدثاً، وذلك محال، وإن كان بمقدار غير متناه فذلك محال لوجوه. الأول: أنه على هذا التقدير قد يكون انقضاء قبل اليوم مدة غير متناهية، وانقضاء ما لا نهاية له غير معقول. الثاني: أنه إن كان يتوقف حصول اليوم على أن يقضي قبله ما لا نهاية - وانقضاء غير المتناهي محال، والموقوف على المحال محال ـ فوجب أن يمتنع حصول اليوم، وحيث حصل اليوم علمنا أن المنقضي متناه لا غير متناه. الثالث: أن المنقضي من زمان الطوفان إلى الأزل أقل من المنقضي من زماننا هذا إلى الأزل، فإذا طبقنا بين هاتين الجملتين من هذين الطرفين اللذين يلياننا وجب أن يظهر التفاوت من طرف الأزل وإلا لكان البعض مثل الكل، فتكون المدة المنقضية متناهية، فيكون تقدم الله تعالى على العالم متناهيا. الرابع: أنه لما كان تقدم الله على العالم أمرًا واجب الثبوت، وهذا التقدم لا يتقرر إلا بهذه المدة كان دوام ذات الله تعالى واستمرار بقائه مفتقرًا إلى تحقيق هذه المدة، والمفتقر إلى الغير ممكن لذاته، فيلزم أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته ممكن الوجود لذاته، وهذا خلف. الخامس: أن المدة لا تُعقل إلا بتعاقب الآنات وتوالي الأزمنة والساعات، فلو كان الباري تعالى متقدمًا على العالم بمدة لا نهاية لها ولا أول لها لزم حصول الأزمنة المتوالية في الأزل، وذلك محال؛ لأن توالي بعض الأزمنة بالبعض يقتضي كون كل واحد منها مسبوقاً بغيره، والأزل عبارة عن نفي المسبوقية بالغير، والجمع بينهما متناقض.
The fourth problem. You say: The god is eternal and the world is originated, and if one of them is before the other, then the precedence of one over the other is either by a finite measure or an infinite measure. If it is by a finite measure, then each of them would be originated, and that is impossible. And if it is by an infinite measure, then that is impossible for reasons. First: that on this assumption, there would have elapsed before today an infinite duration, and the elapsing of what has no end is inconceivable. Second: that if the coming of today is dependent on the elapsing before it of what has no end—and the elapsing of the infinite is impossible, and that which depends on the impossible is impossible—then the coming of today must be impossible; and since today has come, we know that what has elapsed is finite, not infinite. Third: that what has elapsed from the time of the Flood to pre-eternity is less than what has elapsed from our time to pre-eternity; so if we juxtapose these two sums from these two ends that are near us, the difference must appear from the side of pre-eternity, otherwise the part would be like the whole; thus the elapsed duration is finite, so the precedence of God Most High over the world is finite. Fourth: that since the precedence of God over the world is a necessarily established matter, and this precedence is not constituted except by this duration, then the perpetuity of the essence of God Most High and the continuity of His existence would be dependent on the realization of this duration; and that which depends on another is possible in itself, so it would follow that the Necessary Existent in itself is possible in itself, and this is a contradiction (khulf). Fifth: that duration is not conceivable except by the succession of moments and the sequence of times and hours. So if the Creator Most High were prior to the world by a duration having no end and no beginning, it would necessitate the occurrence of successive times in pre-eternity, and that is impossible; because the succession of some times by others requires that each one of them be preceded by another, and pre-eternity (al-azal) is an expression of the negation of being preceded by another, and combining the two is contradictory.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is an objection raised by opponents (likely philosophers) against the Islamic claim that God is eternal (qadim) and the world is originated (muhdath). The objection proceeds by presenting a dilemma: if God precedes the world, the duration of that precedence must be either finite or infinite. If finite, then both would be originated (since each would have a beginning in time). If infinite, then impossible for five reasons. Each reason is a distinct logical argument. The first argument states that an infinite past would mean an infinite duration has elapsed before today, but an infinite cannot be completed. The second argues that if today depends on the completion of an infinite past, and the completion of the infinite is impossible, then today could not occur; but today does occur, so the past must be finite. The third uses a comparison between two periods (from the Flood to pre-eternity and from now to pre-eternity) to show that the elapsed time must be finite, otherwise part would equal whole. The fourth argues that if God's precedence depends on a duration, then God's eternity would be contingent on that duration, implying that the Necessary Existent is contingent—a contradiction. The fifth argues that duration consists of successive moments; an infinite past would mean successive moments in pre-eternity, but pre-eternity by definition negates being preceded, so an infinite sequence in pre-eternity is contradictory.

Key Terms
آنات (anat: moments)
أزل (azal: pre-eternity)
مسبوقية (masbuqiyya: being preceded)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fifth argument, what does 'al-azal' mean?
A) An infinite duration
B) The negation of being preceded by another
C) The beginning of time
D) The first moment

Key Takeaway
Infinite duration in pre-eternity implies succession, but pre-eternity negates succession—a contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that God's priority is not temporal—He is not 'before' the world in time. In your daily life, do you often think of God as existing within your timeline, or as completely outside it? How would your perspective on events, waiting, and patience change if you truly internalized that God is not subject to the succession of moments?
?
If pre-eternity negates all precedence, then nothing can precede God—not even your past mistakes or future hopes. Does your relationship with God feel like it is conditioned by the order of time (e.g., you must repent before He forgives), or can you trust that His atemporal nature means His mercy is not bound by sequence? What would it mean for you to live as if God's presence is not waiting at the end of time but is eternally now?

Refuting Imaginary Duration for Precedence
Razi refutes the opponent's imaginary duration claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi insists that the duration of God's precedence over the world must be real and finite, not merely imaginary or supposed.

Core Idea
God's precedence is real, so its duration cannot be merely imaginary.

In this passage, Razi addresses a potential objection: perhaps the time interval between God and creation is only an imaginary construct with no external reality. He refutes this by grounding the discussion in the reality of divine precedence. If the precedence is a real fact, then any associated duration must also be real and not just mental.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic text carefully along with its literal translation to get the exact argument.
Notice the dialectical structure: opponent's claim followed by Razi's rebuttal.
2
Identify the opponent's position
The opponent argues that the duration of precedence is merely imaginary (مدة موهومة) with no external existence. Find the key phrases that express this.
The opponent uses 'بمدة مفروضة موهومة لا تحقق لها خارج الذهن'.
3
Understand Razi's refutation
Razi counters that divine precedence is real, not depending on our suppositions. Because it is real and finite, the duration must also be real.
Razi uses logical necessity: if precedence is real and finite, then the duration must be real.
4
Grasp the logical consequence
If the precedence is finite, the duration must be finite and real. This closes the opponent's escape route of imaginary time.
This argument is part of a larger proof for the world's temporal origination (huduth).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يقال: هذه الإشكالات إنما تلزم إذا قلنا: إن الله تعالى قبل العالم وسابق عليه بمدة موجودة وزمان متحقق ونحن لا نقول بذلك، بل نقول: إنه تعالى سابق على العالم بمدة مفروضة موهومة لا تحقق لها خارج الذهن. لأنا نقول: هذا الكلام ضعيف جداً، لأن تقدم الباري تعالى على العالم إذا كان حاصلا لا بحسب فرضنا واعتبارنا بل بحسب الحقيقة والوجود سواء وجد الفرض أو لم يوجد، ثم ثبت أن هذا التقدم والسبق ليس إلا بمدة متناهية وجب أن يكون حصول تلك المدة لا بحسب الفرض فقط، بل بحسب الوجود والتحقق.
And it cannot be said: These objections only follow if we say that God most high is before the world and prior to it by an existing duration and a realized time, but we do not say that; rather we say that He most high is prior to the world by a supposed, imaginary duration (muddatin mafrudatin mawhumatin) that has no realization outside the mind. For we say: This statement is very weak, because the precedence of the Creator most high over the world, if it is realized not according to our supposition and consideration but according to reality and existence, whether the supposition exists or not, and then it is established that this precedence and priority is only by a finite duration, then it necessarily follows that the occurrence of that duration is not only according to supposition, but according to existence and realization.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage represents a dialectical exchange: an opponent tries to defend the possibility of infinite precedence by claiming that the duration is merely an imaginary construct (مدة موهومة) with no external reality. The author (Razi) refutes this by stating that the precedence of God over the world is a real, existential fact, not dependent on our mental suppositions. If it is established that this precedence is finite (as argued in the five previous proofs), then the duration itself must be existentially real, not merely imaginary. Thus the attempted escape through imaginary time fails.

Key Terms

تقدم
سبق
تحقق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The discussion is about subjective versus objective time.
Correction: It is actually about the ontological status of the interval between God and creation — whether it is a real existent or a mental construct.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Razi demonstrates that the opponent's appeal to imaginary duration fails because real precedence demands a real duration, and finitude of precedence (proven earlier) requires the duration to be real and finite.

The Boundary Between Pre-eternity and Post-eternity
An objection arguing that pre-eternity and post-eternity cannot be distinguished, leading to contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical objection that pre-eternity (azal) and post-eternity (abad) cannot be distinguished because neither has a definite boundary, leading to an apparent contradiction.

Core Idea
If pre-eternity has no end and post-eternity no beginning, how can they be opposites?

Imam Razi presents a clever objection aimed at the rational coherence of eternal attributes. The opponent argues that pre-eternity and post-eternity, being opposites, must be distinct and have a meeting point, but reflection shows that neither has a boundary, making them indistinguishable—a paradox. This passage sharpens our understanding of how theologians think about infinity and distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its key terms
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the terms 'azal', 'abad', 'taqabul', and 'khulf'. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Follow the logical structure
Identify the premise: opposites must be distinct, implying a boundary. Then follow the reasoning that any proposed boundary fails, so no distinction exists, contradicting the original premise.
The argument is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Reflect on the theological significance
Ask yourself why this objection matters for discussions of God's attributes. How would a theologian respond? The commentary will help clarify.
This connects to broader debates about divine temporality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث: أن الأزل والأبد متقابلان تقابل السلب والإيجاب، وتقابل المتناقضين والمتعاندين، وكل أمرين هذا شأنهما فلا بد وأن يتميز أحدهما عن الآخر. فعلى هذا آخر الأزل متصل بأول الأبد إلا أن القول بهذا أيضًا محال؛ لأن كل نقطة فرضناها آخر الأزل وأول الأبد فأول الأبد كان حاصلا قبل ذلك؛ لأنا لو فرضننا نقطة أخرى قبل تلك النقطة بمائة سنة لم يصر الأبد أزلا بسبب زيادة هذا القرن المتناهي عليه فإذن ليس للأزل البتة آخر ولا للأبد البتة أول، فإذن لا يتميز الأول عن الأبد البتة من كون كل واحد منهما مناقضًا للآخر ومعاندا له، هذا خلف.
The third question: That pre-eternity (al-azal) and post-eternity (al-abad) are opposed to each other in the manner of the opposition of negation and affirmation, and the opposition of contradictories and contraries. And any two things of this nature must be distinguished from one another. Accordingly, the end of pre-eternity is connected to the beginning of post-eternity, except that saying this is also impossible; because every point we suppose to be the end of pre-eternity and the beginning of post-eternity, the beginning of post-eternity would have already occurred before that; for if we suppose another point a hundred years before that point, post-eternity would not become pre-eternity due to the addition of this finite century upon it. Thus pre-eternity has absolutely no end, and post-eternity has absolutely no beginning. Thus pre-eternity is not distinguished from post-eternity at all, while each is contradictory and contrary to the other; this is a contradiction (khulf).
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents an objection concerning the relationship between pre-eternity (azal) and post-eternity (abad). The opponent argues that since these two are opposites, they must be distinct and have a boundary; the end of pre-eternity should be the beginning of post-eternity. But this leads to an absurdity: if we fix a point as the boundary, then any prior point would also be a candidate, and adding finite time does not turn post-eternity into pre-eternity. Hence neither has a definite boundary, so they cannot be distinguished, yet they are supposed to be opposites—a contradiction.

Key Terms
أزل
أبد
تقابل
خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This objection concerns the temporal beginning of the world or the age of the universe.
Correction: The objection is about the logical relationship between two conceptually eternal domains—pre-eternity (azal) and post-eternity (abad)—not about the physical universe's origin.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the third objection regarding pre-eternity and post-eternity?
A) They are the same thing.
B) They are not really opposites.
C) They cannot be distinguished, which contradicts their supposed opposition.
D) They have a clear boundary at the present moment.

Key Takeaway
The objection reveals a deep puzzle about identifying boundaries between two infinite temporal domains, challenging the assumption that opposites must be distinctly separable.
