Allah Cannot Be a Locus for Temporal Events: The Karamiyya Position
Razi states the impossibility and mentions the Karamiyya's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fundamental claim that Allah cannot be a locus for temporal events and learn the Karamiyya's dissenting view.

Core Idea
Most sects deny Allah as a locus for change, but the Karamiyya openly permit it.

Imam Razi begins the Tenth Issue by establishing the impossibility of Allah being a locus for temporal events. He then notes that the Karamiyya school openly breaks this consensus. This lesson introduces the central problem and the first sectarian position.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the basic claim.
Notice the strong assertion of impossibility.
2
Identify the consensus view
Razi states that all sects except the Karamiyya deny that Allah can be a locus for temporal events. This is the majority position.
3
Examine the Karamiyya's exception
The Karamiyya allow it. Also, some claim that most rational people secretly hold this view even if they deny it verbally. Reflect on why this might be significant.
This prepares for the later discussion of other sects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة العاشرة
في بيان أنه تعالى يمتنع أن يكون محلا للحوادث
المشهور أن الكرامية يجوزون ذلك، وسائر الطوائف ينكرونه، ومن الناس من قال: إن أكثر طوائف العقلاء يقولون بهذا المذهب وإن كانوا ينكرونه باللسان، أما المعتزلة فمذهب لأبي علي وأبي هاشم وأتباعهما أنه تعالى مريد بإرادة حادثة، لا محل، وكاره للمعاصي والقبائح بكراهة محدثة، لا في محل، وهذه الإرادات والكراهات وإن كانت موجودة لا في محل إلا أن صفة المريدية والكراهية تحدث في ذات الله تعالى وهذا قول بحدوث الحوادث في ذات الله تعالى. وأيضا: إذا حضر المرئي والمسموع حدثت في ذات الله تعالى صفة السامعية والمبصرية.
بلى، المعتزلة لا يطلقون لفظ الحدوث وإنما يطلقون لفظ التجدد، وهذا نزاع في العبارة، وأما أبو الحسين البصري فإنه يثبت علوما متجددة في ذات الله تعالى بحسب تجدد المعلومات.
وأما الأشعرية فإنهم يثبتون النسخ ويفسرونه بأنه رفع الحكم الثابت أو انتهاء الحكم، وعلى التقديرين فإنه اعتراف بوقوع التغير، لأن الذي ارتفع وانتهى فقد عدم بعد وجوده، وأيضاً يقولون: إنه تعالى عالم يعلم واحد ثم إنه قبل وقوع المعلوم يكون متعلقا بأنه سيقع، وبعد وقوعه يزول ذلك التعلق، ويصير متعلقا بأنه كان واقعاً، وهذا تصريح بتغير هذه التعلقات ويقولون أيضًا: إن قدرته كانت متعلقة بإيجاد الموجود المعين من الأزل، فإذا وجد ذلك الشيء ودخل ذلك الشيء في الوجود انقطع ذلك التعلق؛ لأن الموجود لا يمكن إيقاعه، فهذا اعتراف بأن ذلك التعلق قد زال، وكذا أيضًا الإرادة الأزلية كانت متعلقة بترجيح وجود شيء على عدمه في ذلك الوقت المعين، فإذا ترجح ذلك الشيء في ذلك الوقت امتنع بقاء ذلك التعلق؛ لأن ترجيح المترجح محال.
The Tenth Issue: On explaining that He, exalted, is impossible to be a locus for temporal events. It is well-known that the Karamiyya permit that, and the rest of the sects deny it. And among people is one who said: Most of the sects of rational people adhere to this doctrine even if they deny it verbally. As for the Mu'tazila, the doctrine of Abu Ali, Abu Hashim, and their followers is that He, exalted, is willing by a temporal will, not in a locus, and dislikes sins and abominations by a temporal dislike, not in a locus. And these wills and dislikes, even though they exist not in a locus, the attribute of being willing and disliking occurs in the essence of Allah, exalted, and this is a statement of the occurrence of temporal events in the essence of Allah, exalted. And also: when the visible and the audible are present, the attribute of hearing and seeing occurs in the essence of Allah, exalted. Yes, the Mu'tazila do not use the term 'occurrence' (ḥudūth) but rather use the term 'renewal' (tajaddud), and this is a dispute over terminology. As for Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, he affirms renewed knowledges in the essence of Allah, exalted, according to the renewal of objects of knowledge. As for the Ash'ariyya, they affirm abrogation (naskh) and interpret it as the raising of an established ruling or the termination of a ruling. On both interpretations, it is an acknowledgment of the occurrence of change, because that which is raised and terminated has ceased to exist after its existence. And also they say: He, exalted, is knowing with a single knowledge; then, before the occurrence of the known, it is related (mutaʿalliq) to it as 'it will occur', and after its occurrence that relation ceases and becomes related as 'it did occur'. This is an explicit statement of the change of these relations. And they also say: His power was related to bringing into existence a specific existent from eternity; then when that thing exists and enters into existence, that relation is severed because the existent cannot be brought about. So this is an acknowledgment that that relation has ceased. Similarly also, the eternal will was related to preferring the existence of something over its non-existence at that specific time; so when that thing is preferred at that time, it is impossible for that relation to persist, because preferring what is already preferred is impossible.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is the Tenth Issue, in which Imam Razi examines the possibility of Allah being a locus for temporal events (maḥall li-l-ḥawādith). He first states that it is impossible, but then notes that the Karamiyya openly allow it, while most other sects deny it verbally. However, some argue that most rational people actually accept it implicitly.

Razi then discusses the Mu'tazila: The followers of Abu Ali al-Jubbai and Abu Hashim hold that Allah has a will and a dislike that are temporal but not located in a substrate. Even so, the attributes of 'being willing' and 'being disliking' arise in Allah's essence. This, Razi argues, is effectively admitting temporal events in the divine essence. Similarly, when something is seen or heard, the attributes of hearing and seeing arise in Allah's essence. The Mu'tazila avoid the term 'ḥudūth' (temporal origination) and use 'tajaddud' (renewal), but Razi dismisses this as a verbal dispute.

Next, he mentions Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, who affirms that knowledges are renewed in Allah's essence corresponding to the renewal of objects of knowledge.

Finally, Razi turns to the Ash'ariyya, who hold to abrogation (naskh), which involves change: a ruling ceases to exist after having existed. They also describe divine knowledge as having different relations (taʿalluqāt) before and after an event, and divine power as having a relation to bringing something into existence that ends once it exists. Similarly, the eternal will's relation to preferring something ceases once it occurs. All of these, Razi claims, are admissions of change in the divine essence or attributes, thus bringing the Ash'ariyya closer to the view they outwardly reject.

Key Terms
محل للحوادث
الكرامية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only the Karamiyya believe Allah can be a locus for temporal events.
Correction: Razi hints that many people may implicitly hold it even if they deny it verbally.

Checkpoint
What is well-known about the Karamiyya regarding Allah as a locus for temporal events?
A) They deny it.
B) They permit it.
C) They are silent on the matter.
D) They have no opinion.

Key Takeaway
The Karamiyya are the only school that openly states Allah can be a locus for temporal events, but others may implicitly agree.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage reveals that many may deny with their tongues what they hold in their hearts. Are you honest with yourself about what you truly believe regarding Allah's attributes? Have you ever held a belief about Allah that you were afraid to express openly because of social pressure? Repent and commit to seeking the truth, even if it means standing alone.
?
The Karamiyya openly admit a view that others hide. What unspoken assumptions about Allah might you be secretly holding that contradict the teachings of the Salaf? Are you willing to examine your heart and uproot any hidden innovations, regardless of how many people around you accept them?
?
Razi mentions that most rational people actually hold the view that Allah can be a locus for temporal events, even if they say otherwise. How do you feel knowing that the majority of rational people might be wrong about something so fundamental? Does that challenge your confidence in relying on human reason alone? Determine to base your aqeedah solely on revelation and the understanding of the early generations, not on popularity or rational trends.

Mu'tazila and Abu al-Husayn: Subtle Admission of Change in God
Razi analyzes the Mu'tazila and Abu al-Husayn's positions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, despite denying temporal events in God, effectively admit change in His attributes or knowledge.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila claim temporal wills and dislikes not in a locus, yet attributes occur in God's essence.

Razi turns to the Mu'tazila, who insist that God's will and dislike are temporal but not in a locus, and then to Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, who affirms renewed knowledges. Under Razi's analysis, these positions import change into the divine essence, even if they deny the term 'ḥudūth'. This lesson unpacks their view.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the Mu'tazila section. Note the key terms: will (إرادة), dislike (كراهة), and the phrase 'not in a locus' (لا في محل).
2
Understand the distinction between temporal entities and attributes
Razi explains that even though the will and dislike are not in a locus, the attributes of 'being willing' and 'being disliking' occur in God's essence. This is a crucial point.
Similarly for hearing and seeing.
3
Examine Abu al-Husayn's view on renewed knowledges
Abu al-Husayn affirms that God's knowledges are renewed according to new objects of knowledge. This is another form of change.
Razi calls the Mu'tazila's use of 'tajaddud' a verbal dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة العاشرة
في بيان أنه تعالى يمتنع أن يكون محلا للحوادث
المشهور أن الكرامية يجوزون ذلك، وسائر الطوائف ينكرونه، ومن الناس من قال: إن أكثر طوائف العقلاء يقولون بهذا المذهب وإن كانوا ينكرونه باللسان، أما المعتزلة فمذهب لأبي علي وأبي هاشم وأتباعهما أنه تعالى مريد بإرادة حادثة، لا محل، وكاره للمعاصي والقبائح بكراهة محدثة، لا في محل، وهذه الإرادات والكراهات وإن كانت موجودة لا في محل إلا أن صفة المريدية والكراهية تحدث في ذات الله تعالى وهذا قول بحدوث الحوادث في ذات الله تعالى. وأيضا: إذا حضر المرئي والمسموع حدثت في ذات الله تعالى صفة السامعية والمبصرية.
بلى، المعتزلة لا يطلقون لفظ الحدوث وإنما يطلقون لفظ التجدد، وهذا نزاع في العبارة، وأما أبو الحسين البصري فإنه يثبت علوما متجددة في ذات الله تعالى بحسب تجدد المعلومات.
وأما الأشعرية فإنهم يثبتون النسخ ويفسرونه بأنه رفع الحكم الثابت أو انتهاء الحكم، وعلى التقديرين فإنه اعتراف بوقوع التغير، لأن الذي ارتفع وانتهى فقد عدم بعد وجوده، وأيضاً يقولون: إنه تعالى عالم يعلم واحد ثم إنه قبل وقوع المعلوم يكون متعلقا بأنه سيقع، وبعد وقوعه يزول ذلك التعلق، ويصير متعلقا بأنه كان واقعاً، وهذا تصريح بتغير هذه التعلقات ويقولون أيضًا: إن قدرته كانت متعلقة بإيجاد الموجود المعين من الأزل، فإذا وجد ذلك الشيء ودخل ذلك الشيء في الوجود انقطع ذلك التعلق؛ لأن الموجود لا يمكن إيقاعه، فهذا اعتراف بأن ذلك التعلق قد زال، وكذا أيضًا الإرادة الأزلية كانت متعلقة بترجيح وجود شيء على عدمه في ذلك الوقت المعين، فإذا ترجح ذلك الشيء في ذلك الوقت امتنع بقاء ذلك التعلق؛ لأن ترجيح المترجح محال.
The Tenth Issue: On explaining that He, exalted, is impossible to be a locus for temporal events. It is well-known that the Karamiyya permit that, and the rest of the sects deny it. And among people is one who said: Most of the sects of rational people adhere to this doctrine even if they deny it verbally. As for the Mu'tazila, the doctrine of Abu Ali, Abu Hashim, and their followers is that He, exalted, is willing by a temporal will, not in a locus, and dislikes sins and abominations by a temporal dislike, not in a locus. And these wills and dislikes, even though they exist not in a locus, the attribute of being willing and disliking occurs in the essence of Allah, exalted, and this is a statement of the occurrence of temporal events in the essence of Allah, exalted. And also: when the visible and the audible are present, the attribute of hearing and seeing occurs in the essence of Allah, exalted. As for Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, he affirms renewed knowledges in the essence of Allah, exalted, according to the renewal of objects of knowledge. As for the Ash'ariyya, they affirm abrogation (naskh) and interpret it as the raising of an established ruling or the termination of a ruling. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Commit to studying this issue with humility until your profession matches your conviction.
?

Do these acts imply a change in His essence? How does your understanding of these everyday beliefs actually shape your relationship with Allah? Write down one area where you might be inadvertently admitting change, and resolve to clarify your belief.
?
Razi shows that a mere shift in terminology (tajaddud vs. ḥudūth) can hide a profound concession. Are there areas in your own faith where you hide behind semantics to avoid a difficult truth? For instance, do you say 'Allah's mercy is renewed every morning' without thinking about what that means for His immutability? Let this lesson drive you to seek a coherent understanding of Allah's attributes, not mere slogans. What step will you take today to deepen your 'aqidah?

Ash'ariyya: Change Through Abrogation and Divine Relations
Razi examines Ash'ariyya views on abrogation and divine relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi argues that the Ash'ariyya's doctrines of abrogation and changing divine relations imply change in God.

Core Idea
Ash'ariyya affirm abrogation and changing relations in God's knowledge, power, and will—implicitly acknowledging change.

Razi's final target is the Ash'ariyya. They affirm abrogation (naskh) and describe God's knowledge, power, and will as having relations that come and go. Razi contends that this entails real change in the divine attributes. This lesson examines those arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'ariyya passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the four points: abrogation, knowledge relations, power relations, and will relations.
2
Identify the implications of abrogation
Abrogation is interpreted as raising or terminating a ruling. Both involve a ruling ceasing to exist, which is a change.
3
Understand the changing divine relations
Razi points out that the relations (ta'alluqāt) of God's knowledge, power, and will change over time. For example, knowledge relates as future before an event and as past after it.
These changes in relations are taken as evidence of change in God.

Source Text

?

The Tenth Issue: God Cannot Be a Locus for Accidents
Razi introduces the tenth issue and critiques the Mu'tazila view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's introduction to the tenth issue, the Mu'tazila position on accidents in God's essence, and why he considers their terminological avoidance a verbal dispute.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila admit change in God through 'tajaddud' but deny 'huduth' — a difference only in words.

Razi begins the tenth issue by establishing that God cannot be a locus for accidents. He notes that while most sects deny this openly, many thinkers secretly affirm it. The Mu'tazila are a key example: they claim that God's attributes of willing, disliking, seeing, and hearing occur (or renew) in His essence. Razi argues that this is essentially admitting accidents in God, despite their terminological avoidance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the translation. Notice the flow from the general issue to the specific Mu'tazila doctrine.
Pay attention to the terms 'huduth' and 'tajaddud'.
2
Identify the main positions
The passage mentions three groups: the Karamiyya (who allow God being a locus), the majority (who deny it), and the hidden believers (who affirm secretly). The Mu'tazila are a special case.
Razi claims most rational people secretly hold this view.
3
Examine the Mu'tazila doctrine
According to Abu Ali and Abu Hashim, God is willing with an originated will and dislikes with an originated dislike, not in a locus. Yet these attributes 'occur' in God's essence. Similarly for seeing and hearing.
Notice the word 'yahduthu' (occurs) — they use it despite avoiding 'huduth'.
4
Understand the terminological dispute
The Mu'tazila replace 'huduth' with 'tajaddud'. Razi calls this a dispute in wording, not substance.
This is the key takeaway: the difference is verbal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة العاشرة في بيان أنه تعالى يمتنع أن يكون محلا للحوادث المشهور أن الكرامية يجوزون ذلك، وسائر الطوائف ينكرونه، ومن الناس من قال : إن أكثر طوائف العقلاء يقولون بهذا المذهب وإن كانوا ينكرونه باللسان، أما المعتزلة فمذهب لأبي علي وأبي هاشم وأتباعهما أنه تعالى مريد بإرادة حادثة، لا محل، وكاره للمعاصي والقبائح بكراهة محدثة، لا في محل، وهذه الإرادات والكراهات وإن كانت موجودة لا في محل إلا أن صفة المريدية والكراهية تحدث في ذات الله تعالى وهذا قول بحدوث الحوادث في ذات الله تعالى. وأيضا : إذا حضر المرئي والمسموع حدثت في ذات الله تعالى صفة السامعية والمبصرية. بلى، المعتزلة لا يطلقون لفظ الحدوث وإنما يطلقون لفظ التجدد، وهذا نزاع في العبارة.
The Tenth Issue: An exposition that He, exalted, is impossible to be a locus for accidents. It is well-known that the Karamiyya permit that, and the rest of the sects deny it. Among people is one who said: 'Most groups of the rational people hold this doctrine even if they deny it with the tongue.' As for the Mu'tazila, the doctrine of Abu Ali, Abu Hashim, and their followers is that He, exalted, is willing with a will that is newly originated (haditha), not in a locus, and dislikes sins and abominations with a newly originated dislike (karaha muhdatha), not in a locus. These wills and dislikes, although they exist not in a locus, yet the attribute of being willing and being disliking occurs (yahduthu) in the essence of God, and this is a statement of the occurrence of accidents in the essence of God. Also: when the seen and the heard are present, the attribute of being hearing and being seeing occurs in the essence of God. Yes, the Mu'tazila do not use the term 'occurrence' (huduth) but rather use the term 'renewal' (tajaddud), and this is a dispute in terminology.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage opens the tenth issue of the book, which aims to establish that God cannot be a locus (mahall) for accidents (hawadith). Razi begins by noting the well-known position of the Karamiyya, who permit it, while all other sects deny it. However, he immediately introduces a more nuanced claim: that most rational people actually affirm this doctrine even if they deny it verbally. He then examines the Mu'tazila as a case study. According to Abu Ali al-Jubba'i, Abu Hashim, and their followers, God is willing with a will that is originated (haditha) and not in a locus, and He dislikes with a dislike that is originated (muhdatha) and not in a locus. Although these attributes exist not in a locus, the very fact that the attributes 'willingness' and 'disliking' occur in God's essence implies that accidents occur in His essence. Similarly, when objects of seeing and hearing come into existence, the attributes of being seeing and hearing occur in God's essence. The Mu'tazila, however, avoid the word 'huduth' (origination) and instead use 'tajaddud' (renewal). Razi points out that this is merely a verbal dispute, not a substantive difference.

إرادة حادثة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila completely deny any occurrence or renewal in God's essence.
Correction: The Mu'tazila affirm renewal (tajaddud) of attributes but avoid the term huduth. Razi argues this is a verbal difference, not a substantive one.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the real difference between the Mu'tazila and those who affirm that God is a locus for accidents?
A) The Mu'tazila deny any occurrence in God's essence, while the others affirm it.
B) The Mu'tazila affirm renewal (tajaddud) but deny origination (huduth), which Razi considers a verbal dispute.
C) The Mu'tazila affirm origination but deny renewal.
D) The Mu'tazila agree entirely with the Karamiyya.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila position on accidents in God's essence is essentially the same as other thinkers, differing only in terminology by using 'tajaddud' instead of 'huduth'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which areas of your life do you use different words to describe something you know is wrong, softening the reality to avoid confronting it? How does Razi's exposure of the Mu'tazila's terminological evasion challenge you to stop hiding behind comfortable language?
?
When you think about God's attributes, are you tempted to define them in ways that fit your own reasoning rather than what revelation teaches? What would it cost you to fully submit to the truth that God is beyond your mental categories?
?
Have you ever held onto a theological position simply because it sounded more rational, even when it implied consequences you wouldn't verbally accept? How will you ensure your beliefs about God are shaped by the Qur'an and sunnah, not by your desire for intellectual comfort?

Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and the Ash'ariyya: Implications of Change in God
Razi presents Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's view and the Ash'ari argument from abrogation and relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi argues that both the Mu'tazili Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and the Ash'ariyya implicitly admit change in God through their doctrines.

Core Idea
Denying change in God while affirming abrogation and renewed relations is self-contradictory.

Razi continues his critique of those who deny change in God by examining two major theological schools. He shows that their own doctrines—renewed knowledge for al-Basri, abrogation and shifting relations for the Ash'ariyya—inescapably involve change. This passage forces us to consider whether any theological system can avoid attributing some form of change to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the entire passage once.
Get an overview of the argument. Notice that Razi first mentions Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, then spends most of the passage on the Ash'ariyya.
2
Identify the two groups and their key doctrines.
Find where Razi presents Abu al-Husayn's view on 'renewing knowledges' and then the Ash'ari doctrines of naskh, changing relations of knowledge, power, will, and visibility.
These are separate admissions of change.
3
Analyze how each doctrine implies change.
For each, ask: What changes? For naskh, a ruling ceases. For knowledge-relation, it shifts from future to past. For power-relation, it ends when the object exists. For will-relation, it ends when the preferred occurs. For visibility, the non-existent becomes seen.
Razi argues these are changes in God's attributes or relations.
4
Examine the final argument about vision.
Razi uses a hypothetical dialogue to show that if someone insists the non-existent is seen, absurdities follow. He concludes that God's seeing changes from non-existent to existent, affirming change.
This ties together the theme.
5
Reflect on the overall conclusion.
Consider how these examples undermine the claim that God is absolutely unchanging.
Razi's point is that even opponents inadvertently admit taghayyur.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أبو الحسين البصري فإنه يثبت علوما متجددة في ذات الله تعالى بحسب تجدد المعلومات . وأما الأشعرية فإنهم يثبتون النسخ ويفسرونه بأنه رفع الحكم الثابت أو انتهاء الحكم، وعلى التقديرين فإنه اعتراف بوقوع التغير، لأن الذي ارتفع وانتهى فقد عدم بعد وجوده ،وأيضاً يقولون : إنه تعالى عالم يعلم واحد ثم إنه قبل وقوع المعلوم يكون متعلقا بأنه سيقع، وبعد وقوعه يزول ذلك التعلق، ويصير متعلقا بأنه كان واقعاً، وهذا تصريح بتغير هذه التعلقات ويقولون أيضًا : إن قدرته كانت متعلقة بإيجاد الموجود المعين من الأزل، فإذا وجد ذلك الشيء ودخل ذلك الشيء في الوجود انقطع ذلك التعلق ؛ لأن الموجود لا يمكن إيقاعه، فهذا اعتراف بأن ذلك التعلق قد زال، وكذا أيضًا الإرادة الأزلية كانت متعلقة بترجيح وجود شيء على عدمه في ذلك الوقت المعين، فإذا ترجح ذلك الشيء في ذلك الوقت امتنع بقاء ذلك التعلق ؛ لأن ترجيح المترجح محال. وأيضًا : توافقنا على أن المعدوم لا يكون مرئيا ولا مسموعاً، فالعالم قبل أن كان موجودا لم يكن مرئيا، ولا كانت الأصوات مسموعة، وإذا خلق الألوان والأصوات صارت مرئية ومسموعة . فهذا اعتراف بحدوث هذه التعلقات ولو أن جاهلا التزم كون المعدوم مرئيا ومسموعا قيل له : الله تعالى هل كان يرى العالم وقت عدمه معدوما أو كان يراه وجوداً؟ لا سبيل على القسم الثاني ؛ لأن رؤية المعدوم موجودًا غلط، وهو على الله تعالى محال، ثم إذا أوجده فإنه يراه موجودًا لا معدوما، وإلا عاد حديث الغلط، فعلمنا أنه تعالى كان يرى العالم وقت عدمه معدوماً، ووقت وجوده موجوداً، وهذا يوجب ما ذكرناه.
As for Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, he affirms renewing knowledges in the essence of God, exalted, according to the renewal of known things. As for the Ash'ariyya, they affirm abrogation (naskh) and interpret it as the removal of the established ruling or the expiration of the ruling. On both interpretations, it is an admission of the occurrence of change, because what is removed and expired has ceased to exist after having existed. Also, they say: He, exalted, is knowing with one knowledge. Then before the occurrence of the known thing, that knowledge is related to its future occurrence; after its occurrence, that relation ceases, and it becomes related to its having occurred. This is an explicit statement of the change of these relations. They also say: His power was related to bringing the specific existent into existence from eternity; when that thing exists and enters into existence, that relation is cut off, because the existent cannot be brought into existence. So this is an admission that that relation has ceased. Similarly, the eternal will was related to preferring the existence of something over its non-existence at that specific time; when that thing is preferred at that time, the persistence of that relation is impossible, because preferring what is already preferred is absurd. Also, we agree that the non-existent cannot be seen or heard. So the world, before it existed, was not seen, nor were sounds heard; when colors and sounds are created, they become seen and heard. This is an admission of the occurrence of these relations. And if an ignorant person insisted that the non-existent is seen or heard, we would say to him: Did God, exalted, see the world at the time of its non-existence as non-existent, or did He see it as existent? The second alternative is impossible, because seeing the non-existent as existent is an error, and that is impossible for God. Then when He brings it into existence, He sees it as existent, not as non-existent; otherwise the error would return. Thus we know that He, exalted, saw the world at the time of its non-existence as non-existent, and at the time of its existence as existent. This necessitates what we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage continues the argument that various groups implicitly admit change in God despite denying it explicitly. First, Abu al-Husayn al-Basri (a Mu'tazili) affirms that knowledge in God is renewed according to the renewal of known objects – a clear admission of change. Then Razi examines the Ash'ariyya. He points to several of their doctrines that imply change: (1) Abrogation (naskh): whether defined as removal or expiration, it means a ruling ceases to exist after having existed, implying change in God's will or command. (2) God's knowledge: before an event occurs, knowledge is related to its future occurrence; after, it relates to its having occurred. This change in the relation (ta'alluq) is a change. (3) God's power: from eternity, power is related to bringing a specific being into existence; once it exists, that relation ends. (4) God's will: the eternal will is related to preferring existence at a specific time; when that time comes, the relation cannot persist. (5) Visibility: we agree the non-existent is not seen; thus when the world comes into existence, God's seeing changes from seeing it as non-existent to seeing it as existent – a change in relation. Razi drives home that these are admissions of change (taghayyur) in God's attributes or relations.

Correction: The Ash'ariyya do not explicitly admit change, but their doctrines entail it, as Razi demonstrates.

Checkpoint
Which Ash'ari doctrine does Razi use to argue that they admit change in God?
A) The belief that God's essence is simple.
B) The doctrine of abrogation (naskh) of rulings.
C) The belief that God's will is eternal and unchanging.
D) The denial of any relations in God.

Key Takeaway
Razi demonstrates that both the Mu'tazili Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and the Ash'ariyya, despite denying change in God, affirm doctrines that entail change in His attributes or relations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray for something, do you subconsciously assume that God's will changes in response to your supplication? Razi shows that affirming change in God's relations (like will) is problematic. How does this challenge you to trust that God's eternal will already encompasses your prayer, and that your prayer is the means by which His decree unfolds, not a cause of change in Him? Can you truly accept this without feeling that your prayer is less effective?
?
When you sin, do you feel that God's knowledge of you changes? That He now knows you as a sinner whereas before He knew you as virtuous? Razi points out that the Ash'ari doctrine of relations forces a change in God's knowledge relation. How does this expose the anthropomorphic tendencies in your own heart? Are you willing to affirm that God's knowledge of your sin is eternal and unchanging, yet still strive to repent? What does that demand of your humility?
?
The passage ends with Razi's argument about visibility: God sees the world as non-existent before creation and as existent after. Do you ever think of God's 'seeing' of your actions as changing—like He sees your good deeds differently than your bad? Razi's logic shows that change in these relations is unavoidable even for those who deny it. How does this press you to worship a God who is beyond all temporal change, yet intimately aware of every moment of your life?

Philosophers and Abu al-Barakat on New Attributes in God
Razi presents the philosophers' view on relations and Abu al-Barakat's explicit affirmation of renewed attributes in God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers' theory of relations leads to new attributes in God, and how Abu al-Barakat explicitly affirms renewed wills and knowledges in God's essence.

Core Idea
Even philosophers who deny change in God end up affirming new relations in His essence.

Razi presents a surprising alignment: the philosophers, despite their apparent denial of change in God, actually hold that new relations occur in God's essence with each event. He then highlights Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi, who goes further by explicitly affirming renewed wills and knowledges in God. This passage reveals that many rational thinkers accept some form of change in God, even if they deny it verbally.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two positions
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that Razi presents two distinct views: the philosophers' general theory of relations, and Abu al-Barakat's specific explicit affirmation.
Pay attention to the transition: 'As for the philosophers...' and 'As for Abu al-Barakat...'
2
Understand the philosophers' theory of relations
The philosophers believe that relations (idafat) exist externally. Therefore, whenever an event occurs, God has a new relation of 'being with' that event, which is a relational attribute occurring in His essence.
This is how the philosophers, despite denying accidents in God, end up affirming a kind of change.
3
Examine Abu al-Barakat's explicit affirmation
Abu al-Barakat goes further by explicitly stating that God has renewed wills and renewed knowledges. He argues that this is necessary to acknowledge God as the Creator of the world.
Note his paradoxical statement about reverence and transcendence.
4
Reflect on Razi's conclusion
Razi concludes that most rational groups actually hold this view, even if they deny it with their tongues. Consider why he thinks this is significant.
This is a rhetorical move to show widespread agreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفلاسفة : فهم مع أنهم أبعد الناس في الظاهر عن هذا القول قائلون به، وذلك لأن مذهبهم أن الإضافات موجودة في الأعيان وعلى هذا فكل حادث يحدث فإن الله تعالى يكون موجودًا معه، وكونه تعالى مع ذلك الحادث وصف إضافي حدث في ذاته. وأما أبو البركات البغدادي - وهو من أكابر الفلاسفة المتأخرين ـ فإنه صرح في كتابه " المعتبر " بإثبات إرادات محدثة وعلوم محدثة في ذات الله تعالى، وزعم أنه لا يتصور الاعتراف بكونه تعالى إلها لهذا العالم إلا مع هذا المذهب، ثم قال : " الإجلال من هذا الجلال واجب والتنزيه من هذا التنزيه لازم " فإذا حصل الوقوف على هذا التفصيل ظهر أن هذا المذهب قال به أكثر فرق العقلاء، وإن كانوا ينكرونه باللسان.
As for the philosophers, although they are apparently the farthest of people from this statement, they are in fact adherents of it. That is because their doctrine is that relations (idafat) exist in external entities. On this basis, whenever an event occurs, God, exalted, exists together with it, and His being with that event is a relational attribute (wasf idafi) that occurs in His essence. As for Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi – and he is among the greatest of the later philosophers – he declared explicitly in his book 'al-Mu'tabar' the affirmation of renewed wills and renewed knowledges in the essence of God, exalted, and claimed that it is inconceivable to acknowledge that He, exalted, is God of this world except with this doctrine. Then he said: 'Reverence due to this majesty is obligatory, and transcendence due to this transcendence is necessary.' When one becomes aware of this detailed analysis, it becomes apparent that most groups of the rational people hold this doctrine, even if they deny it with the tongue.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage covers the philosophers' position. Despite being apparently far from the idea that God is a locus for accidents, they actually affirm it. Their doctrine of relations (idafat) existing in external entities means that when a new event occurs, God has a new relation of 'being with' that event. This relation is an attribute that occurs in God's essence. Razi then focuses on Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi, a later philosopher, who explicitly affirms renewed wills and renewed knowledges in God's essence in his work 'al-Mu'tabar'. Abu al-Barakat claims that to acknowledge God as the God of this world is impossible without this doctrine. He then makes a paradoxical statement: 'Reverence due to this majesty is obligatory, and transcendence due to this transcendence is necessary.' This means that while one must maintain God's transcendence, the evidence forces this conclusion. Razi concludes that most rational groups actually hold this view even if they deny it verbally.

The Threefold Division of Attributes: Real, Mixed, and Pure Relations
Razi classifies attributes into three categories and illustrates pure relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's classification of attributes into three categories and how change occurs only in relations, not in essence or real attributes.

Core Idea
Attributes are either purely real, real with relations, or purely relational.

Razi begins by laying out a fundamental classification for understanding divine attributes. He distinguishes between attributes that are intrinsic, those that necessarily involve a relation, and those that are purely relational. This distinction is crucial for theological arguments about change in God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three categories
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Razi explicitly numbers the three kinds of attributes.
Pay attention to the examples: blackness, knowledge, and spatial relations.
2
Understand the first category: real attributes free from relations
These are intrinsic qualities like blackness that do not imply a relation to something else. They stand alone.
3
Understand the second category: real attributes with relations
Attributes like knowledge and power are real yet necessarily involve a relation to their objects. This is a mixed category.
Note the term 'ta'alluq' (connection) used for power.
4
Understand the third category: pure relations
These are relations like 'before' or 'to the right' that can change without any change in the subject's essence or real attributes.
The example of sitting left/right clarifies this.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
Razi concludes that change in relations is unavoidable. This matters for debates about divine immutability.
The passage ends mid-sentence but the classification is complete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الصفات على ثلاثة أقسام : أحدها : صفات حقيقية عارية عن الإضافات كالسواد والبياض. وثانيها : الصفات الحقيقية التي تلزمها الإضافات كالعلم والقدرة، وذلك لأن العلم صفة حقيقية تلزمها إضافة مخصوصة إلى المعلوم، وكذا القدرة صفة حقيقية، ولها تعلق بالمقدور، وذلك التعلق إضافة مخصوصة بين القدرة وبين المقدور وثالثها : الإضافات المحضة والنسب المحضة مثل كون الشيء قبل غيره، ومثل كون الشيء يمينًا لغيره أو يسارًا له، فإنك إذا جلست على يمين إنسان ثم قام ذلك الإنسان وجلس في الجانب الآخر منك، فقد كنت يمينًا له ثم صرت الآن يسارًا له، فههنا لم يقع التغير في ذاتك ولا في صفة حقيقية من صفاتك، بل في محض الإضافات. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: أما وقوع التغير في الإضافات فلا خلاص عنه، وأما
And know that attributes are of three kinds: First, real attributes free from relations (idafat), like blackness and whiteness. Second, real attributes to which relations adhere, like knowledge and power. This is because knowledge is a real attribute to which a specific relation to the known adheres; similarly, power is a real attribute, and it has a connection (ta'alluq) to the empowered, and that connection is a specific relation between power and the empowered. Third, pure relations and pure relative notions (nisab), such as a thing being before another, or a thing being to the right or left of another. For if you sit on the right of a person, then that person stands and sits on your other side, you were to his right and now you are to his left. Here no change has occurred in your essence nor in any real attribute of yours, but only in pure relations. If you understand this, we say: As for the occurrence of change in relations, there is no escape from it, and as for...
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is a classification of attributes. Razi begins by stating that attributes fall into three categories. The first category is 'real attributes free from relations' (sifat haqiqiyya 'ariyya 'an al-idafat), exemplified by blackness and whiteness. These are intrinsic qualities that do not imply a relation to something else. The second category is 'real attributes to which relations adhere' (sifat haqiqiyya talzamuhu al-idafat), such as knowledge and power. Knowledge is a real attribute of the knower, but it necessarily entails a relation to the known object; similarly, power entails a relation to the empowered object. The third category is 'pure relations and pure relative notions' (al-idafat al-mahda wa al-nisab al-mahda), such as being before, to the right, or to the left. Razi provides an example: if you are sitting to someone's right, and then they move to your other side, you become to their left. No change occurs in your essence or in any real attribute; the change is only in the pure relation. Razi then concludes that change in relations is unavoidable – presumably even for God – but the text is cut off. This classification is crucial for understanding how Razi argues that admitting change in relations does not necessarily imply change in God's essence.

Key Terms
صفات حقيقية
الإضافات
النسب المحضة
التعلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All attributes are either purely real or purely relational; there is no mixed category.
Correction: Razi identifies a third category: real attributes that entail relations, such as knowledge and power.

Checkpoint
Which category of attributes includes knowledge and power according to Razi?
A) Real attributes free from relations.
B) Real attributes to which relations adhere.
C) Pure relations only.
D) None of the above.

Key Takeaway
Razi divides attributes into three types, showing that some real attributes necessarily involve relations, and changes can occur in relations without affecting essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's name 'al-Mutakabbir' (The Supreme), do you see it as a real attribute or a pure relation? How does that distinction affect the reverence you feel in worship?
?
Razi shows that knowledge is both a real attribute and involves a relation. Consider your own role as a student of Islam: is your knowledge merely a relational label, or does it transform your essence? Which does Razi's division suggest?
?
If pure relations can change without affecting essence, what does that mean for your understanding of God's unchanging nature when you reflect on His attributes like 'the Creator'? Does it bring you comfort that God's essence remains untouched by the act of creation? Why or why not?

The Temporality of Relations: The Argument from Visibility
Razi argues for temporality of visibility relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that attachments (taʿalluqāt) such as visibility are temporal, based on the agreed fact that the non-existent cannot be seen.

Core Idea
Visibility relations arise only when objects exist, proving they are created.

In this passage, Razi presents a straightforward argument for the temporality of relations (iḍāfāt). He begins with a universally agreed premise—that the non-existent is not visible—and shows that the world's becoming visible only after creation implies that the relation of visibility is a temporal occurrence. This lesson walks through that reasoning and the refutation of a potential objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice the initial agreement: 'the non-existent cannot be seen.'
2
Identify the argument
Razi argues that the world was not visible before existence but became visible after creation. This shows the relation of visibility occurred in time.
3
Consider the objection and refutation
Razi then considers an ignorant person who insists the non-existent is visible. He refutes by showing that would imply God sees the non-existent as existent, which is an error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة العاشرة. وأيضًا : توافقنا على أن المعدوم لا يكون مرئيا ولا مسموعاً، فالعالم قبل أن كان موجودا لم يكن مرئيا، ولا كانت الأصوات مسموعة، وإذا خلق الألوان والأصوات صارت مرئية ومسموعة . فهذا اعتراف بحدوث هذه التعلقات. ولو أن جاهلا التزم كون المعدوم مرئيا ومسموعا قيل له : الله تعالى هل كان يرى العالم وقت عدمه معدوما أو كان يراه وجوداً؟ لا سبيل على القسم الثاني ؛ لأن رؤية المعدوم موجودًا غلط، وهو على الله تعالى محال، ثم إذا أوجده فإنه يراه موجودًا لا معدوما، وإلا عاد حديث الغلط، فعلمنا أنه تعالى كان يرى العالم وقت عدمه معدوماً، ووقت وجوده موجوداً، وهذا يوجب ما ذكرناه. وأما الفلاسفة : فهم مع أنهم أبعد الناس في الظاهر عن هذا القول قائلون به، وذلك لأن مذهبهم أن الإضافات موجودة في الأعيان وعلى هذا فكل حادث يحدث فإن الله تعالى يكون موجودًا معه، وكونه تعالى مع ذلك الحادث وصف إضافي حدث في ذاته. وأما أبو البركات البغدادي - وهو من أكابر الفلاسفة المتأخرين ـ فإنه صرح في كتابه " المعتبر بإثبات إرادات محدثة وعلوم محدثة في ذات الله تعالى، وزعم أنه لا يتصور الاعتراف بكونه تعالى إلها لهذا العالم إلا مع هذا المذهب، ثم قال : " الإجلال من هذا الجلال واجب والتنزيه من هذا التنزيه لازم " فإذا حصل الوقوف على هذا التفصيل ظهر أن هذا المذهب قال به أكثر فرق العقلاء، وإن كانوا ينكرونه باللسان.
The Tenth Issue. Also: We agree that the non-existent (al-maʿdūm) cannot be seen nor heard. So the world before it existed was not visible, nor were sounds audible. When colors and sounds were created, they became visible and audible. So this is an admission of the occurrence of these attachments (taʿalluqāt). If an ignorant person insisted that the non-existent is visible and audible, it would be said to him: Did God, exalted, see the world at the time of its non-existence as non-existent or did He see it as existent? There is no way to the second division, because seeing the non-existent as existent is an error, and that is impossible for God, exalted. Then when He brings it into existence, He sees it as existent, not as non-existent, otherwise the error returns. So we know that He, exalted, saw the world at the time of its non-existence as non-existent, and at the time of its existence as existent, and this necessitates what we mentioned. As for the philosophers (falāsifa), despite being the farthest people in appearance from this statement, they still affirm it, because their doctrine is that relations (iḍāfāt) exist externally (al-aʿyān). On this basis, every event that occurs, God exalted exists with it, and His being with that event is a relational attribute (waṣf iḍāfī) that occurs in His essence. As for Abū al-Barakāt al-Baghdādī—one of the greatest later philosophers—he explicitly affirmed in his book al-Muʿtabar the establishment of temporal wills and temporal knowledges in God's essence, and claimed that acknowledgment of Him being God of this world is inconceivable except with this doctrine. Then he said: 'Reverence for this majesty is obligatory, and transcendence from this transcendence is necessary.' So once this detail is understood, it becomes clear that this doctrine is held by most groups of rational people, even if they deny it with the tongue.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage presents an argument for the temporality of relations (iḍāfāt) based on the visibility of the non-existent. Razi begins by observing that we all agree that the non-existent cannot be seen or heard. Thus before the world existed, it was not visible; sounds were not audible. When colors and sounds were created, they became visible and audible. This shows that these relations (the visibility and audibility of things) occur only when the things exist. He then refutes a hypothetical objection: if someone insists the non-existent is visible or audible, they would be asked: Did God see the world when it was non-existent as non-existent or as existent? He couldn't have seen it as existent because that would be an error—seeing non-existent as existent—which is impossible for God. So He must have seen it as non-existent. Then when He creates it, He sees it as existent. Thus the relation of seeing changes, proving the change in relations. Razi then notes that even the philosophers, who outwardly deny change in God, actually affirm temporal relations because their ontology admits relations as existing externally. He specifically cites Abu al-Barakāt al-Baghdādī, who explicitly affirmed temporal wills and knowledges in God's essence, arguing that without this, God cannot be recognized as God of this world. Razi concludes that most rational groups actually hold this view despite verbal denial.

Key Terms
المعدوم
التعلقات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God sees everything as existent, even before it exists.
Correction: Razi clarifies that God sees non-existent as non-existent, and only sees it as existent after creation. Seeing non-existent as existent would be an error.

Checkpoint
What premise does Razi use to argue that visibility relations are temporal?
A) The non-existent cannot be seen.
B) God sees all things.
C) The world is eternal.
D) The philosophers agree.

Key Takeaway
The visibility relation is temporal because the non-existent is not visible, and the world became visible only after creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You exist because God willed it, and He sees you as an existent being. If He sees you now in a state of disobedience, will you continue as if He does not see, or will you change this moment into a new relation of obedience?
?
The argument shows that God's seeing changes as the object changes—He sees the non-existent as non-existent. Are you aware that your Lord sees you in every state you are in, whether you remember Him or forget? Does that awareness make you more mindful of your actions in the next hour?
?
Razi refutes those who claim the non-existent is visible. But what about your own self? Do you often pretend to be something you are not in God's sight? The truth is that He sees you exactly as you are. Will you drop the pretense and face Him honestly?

The Philosophers' Position on Temporal Relations
Razi shows philosophers affirm temporal relations and cites Abu al-Barakat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how even the philosophers, despite outwardly denying change in God, actually affirm temporal relations (iḍāfāt) and some even affirm temporal attributes in God's essence.

Core Idea
Philosophers affirm temporal relations in God, and Abu al-Barakat explicitly affirms temporal wills and knowledge in God.

Razi now turns to the philosophers, who are often seen as denying any change in God. He argues that their ontology of relations (iḍāfāt) existing externally leads them to admit that relational attributes occur in God. He highlights Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi, who went further by explicitly affirming temporal wills and knowledges in God's essence. This lesson explores this surprising convergence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the second part of the Arabic and translation. Notice that Razi says the philosophers are 'the farthest people in appearance from this statement' yet they still affirm it.
2
Understand the reasoning
The philosophers hold that relations exist externally. So when an event occurs, God's being 'with' that event is a new relational attribute, hence temporal.
3
Examine Abu al-Barakat's view
He explicitly states temporal wills and knowledges in God's essence, arguing that without this, God cannot be recognized as God of this world.

Source Text

Three Kinds of Attributes and the Karramiyya Debate
Razi classifies attributes into three kinds and applies the classification to the change debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's threefold classification of attributes and how it clarifies the debate about change in God's attributes between the Karramiyya and other sects.

Core Idea
Attributes are either real, real-with-relations, or pure relations — and only the last change.

Before we can talk about whether God's attributes change, we need a clear vocabulary. Razi provides that by dividing all attributes into three categories: those that are purely intrinsic, those that are intrinsic but involve relations, and those that are nothing but relations. This classification then becomes the key to understanding a major sectarian disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification
Read the first part of the passage (up to the example) to see the three kinds of attributes. Note the definitions and examples for each kind.
Pay attention to the examples: blackness/whiteness for the first kind, knowledge/power for the second, and spatial relations for the third.
2
Understand the example of pure relations
Razi gives an example of spatial relations (right/left) to illustrate pure relations. Think about why this example effectively shows that change can occur without any change in the essence or real attributes.
The example shows that relations are context-dependent and do not affect the intrinsic nature.
3
Apply the classification to the debate
Razi then applies the classification to the issue of change in God. Note that everyone agrees relations can change (e.g., God's relation to creation), but the Karramiyya affirm change in real attributes, while others deny it.
This pinpoints the exact area of disagreement: whether real attributes of God can change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الصفات على ثلاثة أقسام : أحدها : صفات حقيقية عارية عن الإضافات كالسواد والبياض. وثانيها : الصفات الحقيقية التي تلزمها الإضافات كالعلم والقدرة، وذلك لأن العلم صفة حقيقية تلزمها إضافة مخصوصة إلى المعلوم، وكذا القدرة صفة حقيقية، ولها تعلق بالمقدور، وذلك التعلق إضافة مخصوصة بين القدرة وبين المقدور. وثالثها : الإضافات المحضة والنسب المحضة مثل كون الشيء قبل غيره، ومثل كون الشيء يمينًا لغيره أو يسارًا له، فإنك إذا جلست على يمين إنسان ثم قام ذلك الإنسان وجلس في الجانب الآخر منك، فقد كنت يمينًا له ثم صرت الآن يسارًا له، فههنا لم يقع التغير في ذانك ولا في صفة حقيقية من صفاتك، بل في محض الإضافات. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: أما وقوع التغير في الإضافات فلا خلاص عنه، وأما وقوع التغير في الصفات الحقيقية فالكرامية يثبتونه، وسائر الطوائف ينكرونه، فظهر الفرق في هذا الباب بين مذهب الكرامية، ومذهب غيرهم.
Know that attributes are of three kinds: First: real attributes (ṣifāt ḥaqīqiyya) devoid of relations (iḍāfāt), like blackness and whiteness. Second: real attributes that entail relations, like knowledge and power; because knowledge is a real attribute that entails a specific relation to the known, and likewise power is a real attribute, and it has an attachment (taʿalluq) to the object of power, that attachment being a specific relation between power and the object of power. Third: pure relations (iḍāfāt maḥḍa) and pure correlations (nisab maḥḍa), such as a thing being before another, or such as a thing being to the right or left of another. For if you sit to the right of a person, then that person gets up and sits on your other side, you were to his right and now you are to his left. Here change has not occurred in your essence nor in any real attribute of your attributes, but in pure relations. If you understand this, we say: As for the occurrence of change in relations, there is no escaping it. As for the occurrence of change in real attributes, the Karramiyya affirm it, and all other sects deny it. Thus the difference between the doctrine of the Karramiyya and the doctrine of others becomes clear.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage provides a classification of attributes into three kinds, which is essential for understanding the debate about change in God. Razi first defines the first kind: real attributes that are free from any relation, like blackness and whiteness. These are intrinsic qualities that do not involve a relation to something else. The second kind: real attributes that entail relations, such as knowledge and power. Knowledge is a real quality of the knower, but it necessarily involves a relation to the known object. Similarly, power involves a relation to the object over which one has power. The third kind: pure relations and pure correlations, which are not real attributes but merely relative positions, like being before or after, or being to the right or left. Razi illustrates this with an example: if you are to the right of someone and they move, you become left of them. Your essence and your real attributes (like your height) do not change; only the relation changes. After this classification, Razi applies it: change in relations is unavoidable—everyone admits that relations can change even regarding God (e.g., God created the world, so the relation of Creator to creature occurs). But change in real attributes is the point of contention: the Karramiyya affirm that real attributes can occur in God (temporal attributes), while all other sects deny it. Thus the classification clarifies the precise difference between the Karramiyya and others.

Key Terms
صفات حقيقية
إضافات
تعلق
كرامية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That relations are real attributes; Razi distinguishes pure relations from real attributes that entail relations.
Correction: Pure relations (like spatial relations) are not real attributes; they are mere relationships that can change without affecting the subject's essence or real attributes.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's classification, which type of attribute is power (qudra) considered?
A) A pure relation
B) A real attribute devoid of relations
C) A real attribute that entails a relation
D) A temporal attribute of the Karramiyya

Key Takeaway
Razi's classification of attributes into three kinds shows that change in relations is uncontroversial, while change in real attributes is the disputed point with the Karramiyya.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If change in pure relations is unavoidable, even for God, what does that tell you about your own need to adapt to changing circumstances while keeping your core character intact? Are you too rigid or too changeable in your commitments?
?
Razi says real attributes do not change in God. Which of your own attributes (character traits, beliefs) do you treat as unchangeable truths, and which do you allow to shift? Are you confusing a relation with a real attribute?
?
The Karramiyya believed God's attributes can change. Reflect: Is there an area in your life where you stubbornly refuse change, thinking it would compromise your essence, when actually it is only a relation that needs adjusting?

The Implication of Temporal Attributes: Razi's First Proof
Razi argues that temporal attributes would imply imperfection in God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first argument against temporal attributes in God, and why it entails that God cannot possess any attribute that begins in time.

Core Idea
If an attribute of God were temporal, God would have been imperfect before it—impossible.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents his first proof against the Karramiyya sect, who claimed that attributes can occur in God over time. Razi relies on a simple but powerful principle: every divine attribute must be an attribute of perfection and majesty. Using this, he constructs a logical argument that demonstrates the impossibility of temporal attributes in God. Read the passage and see how the logic unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Get a sense of the argument's flow.
Note that Razi begins by stating there are multiple proofs; this is the first.
2
Identify the key premises
Identify the two key premises: (1) every attribute of God is an attribute of perfection, and (2) lacking an attribute of perfection is deficiency.
3
Follow the conditional logic
Follow the conditional: if an attribute were temporal (muḥdatha), then before its occurrence, God's essence would be without that perfection, hence deficient.
Deficiency in God is impossible.
4
Understand the conclusion
The argument concludes that temporal attributes in God are impossible. Ask yourself: why does this argument not apply to attributes like 'Creator' which are relational?
Razi's argument is about essential perfections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يدل على فساد قول الكرامية وجوه : الحجة الأولى: أن كل ما كان من صفات الله تعالى فلا بد وأن يكون من صفات الكمال ونعوت الجلال، فلو كانت صفة من صفاته محدثة لكان ذاته قبل حدوث تلك الصفة خاليًا عن صفة الكمال والخالي عن صفة الكمال ناقص، فيلزم أن ذاته كانت ناقصة قبل تلك الصفة فيها، وذلك محال، فثبت أن حدوث الصفة ذات الله تعالى محال.
That which indicates the corruption of the Karramiyya's statement are several proofs. The first proof: Every one of God's attributes must be attributes of perfection (kamāl) and qualifiers of majesty (jalāl). If an attribute of His were temporal (muḥdatha), then His essence before the occurrence of that attribute would be devoid of an attribute of perfection; that which is devoid of an attribute of perfection is deficient (nāqiṣ). So it would follow that His essence was deficient before that attribute; that is impossible. Thus it is established that the occurrence of an attribute in God's essence is impossible.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument against the Karramiyya doctrine that temporal attributes can occur in God. Razi states that every divine attribute must be an attribute of perfection and majesty. The logic is: if an attribute of God were temporal (coming into existence after a state of non-existence), then before its occurrence, God's essence would lack that attribute of perfection. Lacking an attribute of perfection implies deficiency. Therefore, before that attribute occurred, God would have been deficient. But deficiency in God is impossible. Therefore, the occurrence of any attribute in God is impossible. This argument relies on the premise that all divine attributes are perfections, and that God cannot have been deficient at any time.

Key Terms

نعوت الجلال
محدثة
ناقص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that attributes like will and speech can temporally occur in God (Karramiyya position).
Correction: Razi shows that if any attribute were temporal, God would have been imperfect before its occurrence, which is impossible. Therefore, no divine attribute can be temporal.

Checkpoint
What does Razi identify as the consequence of a temporal attribute occurring in God?
A) God would have multiple attributes
B) God's essence would have been deficient before that attribute
C) That attribute would be a relation
D) God would be subject to change

Key Takeaway
Razi's first argument establishes that temporal attributes are incompatible with God's perfection, as they would imply a prior state of deficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Razi's argument forces you to confront a logical truth: if God had ever lacked a perfection, He would have been deficient. Does this truth change the way you approach concepts like prayer or your own neediness before God? What would it mean to truly believe that God needs nothing from you?
?
If you were to explain to someone why God cannot acquire new perfections, what in this lesson would you hold onto? Are you willing to let this argument reshape your awe of God's eternal completeness today?

Second Argument's First Premise: Receptivity as a Necessary Concomitant
Razi states the second argument and proves the first premise: receptivity must be a necessary concomitant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that if God's essence were receptive to temporal attributes, that receptivity must be a necessary concomitant of His essence, and why this leads to a rejection of the Karramiyya view.

Core Idea
Eternal receptivity must be a necessary attribute, not accidental.

Razi's second argument against temporal attributes in God is more intricate. He begins by asserting a key conditional: if God's essence could receive a temporal attribute, then that receptivity would have to be an eternal, necessary feature of His essence. This logical move is crucial because it sets up the tension between eternal receptivity and the temporal nature of events. In this lesson, we will explore the first premise and its proof against infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the overall argument statement
Begin by reading the opening lines where Razi states the second argument. Notice how he directly connects receptivity to necessity and eternity.
The Arabic uses conditional 'law' - 'if'.
2
Examine the first premise
Read the detailed exposition of the first premise: that receptivity must be a necessary concomitant. Follow the proof by elimination: if not necessary, it would be accidental, leading to infinite regress.
This is a common kalam argument form: showing that the alternative leads to absurdity.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that the first premise establishes that the receptivity is eternal and necessary. This sets the stage for the next premises about the possibility of events in eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : لو كانت ذاته قابلة للصفة المحدثة لكانت تلك القابلية من لوازم ذاته، وكانت تلك القابلية أزلية - وثبوت القابلية يستلزم صحة وجود المقبول - فلو كانت قابلية الحوادث أزلية لكان وجود الحوادث في الأزل ممكنا إلا أن هذا محال؛ لأن الحوادث ما لها أول والأزل لا أول له ، والجمع بينهما محال. واعلم أن هذا الدليل مبني على ثلاث مقدمات : المقدمة الأولى : أنه لو كانت ذاته قابلة للصفة المحدثة لكانت تلك القابلية لوازم ذاته، والدليل عليه أنها لو لم تكن من اللوازم لكانت من العوارض، فكانت الذات قابلة لتلك القابلية، فقبول تلك القابلية إن كان من اللوازم فهو المقصود، وإن كان من العوارض فيفتقر إلى قابلية أخرى، ويلزم إما التسلسل وإما الانتهاء إلى قابلية تكون من لوازم الذات، وهو المطلوب. المقدمة الثانية : أن القابلية إذا كانت أزلية وجب أن يكون المقبول صحيح الوجود في الأزل، والدليل عليه أن كون الشيء قابلا لغيره نسبة بين القابل والمقبول والنسبة بين المنتسبين متوقفة على تحقق كل واحد من المنتسبين فصحة النسبة تعتمد وجود المنتسبين، ولما كانت صحة اتصاف الباري بالحوادث حاصلة في الأزل لزم أن تكون صحة وجود الحوادث حاصلة في الأزل. المقدمة الثالثة : أن حدوث الحوادث في الأزل غير ممكنة، والدليل عليه ما ذكرنا أنه يقتضي الجمع بين ثبوت الأزلية وبين عدمها، وكل ذلك محال.
The second proof: If His essence were receptive to the temporal attribute, then that receptivity (al-qābiliyya) would be among the necessities (lawāzim) of His essence, and that receptivity would be eternal (azaliyya). The affirmation of receptivity entails the possibility (ṣiḥḥa) of existence of the recipient (al-maqbūl). So if the receptivity of events were eternal, then the existence of events in eternity would be possible, but that is impossible, because events have a beginning and the eternal has no beginning, and combining them is impossible. Know that this proof is based on three premises. The first premise: If His essence were receptive to the temporal attribute, then that receptivity would be a necessary concomitant of His essence. The proof of that is: if it were not necessary, it would be accidental (ʿārid), so the essence would be receptive to that receptivity. If that acceptance of receptivity were among the necessities, that is what is sought; if it were accidental, it would require another receptivity, leading either to infinite regress or termination at a receptivity that is a necessary concomitant of the essence, which is the desired conclusion. The second premise: If the receptivity were eternal, then the recipient (al-maqbūl) must be sound for existence in eternity. The proof of that is: a thing's being receptive to another is a relation (nisba) between the receptive and the recipient, and the relation between two related things depends on the realization of each of the two relata. So the soundness of the relation depends on the existence of the relata. Since the soundness of God's being qualified by events was obtained in eternity, it follows that the soundness of the existence of events was obtained in eternity. The third premise: The occurrence of events in eternity is impossible. The proof of that is what we mentioned: it would entail combining the affirmation of eternality with its negation, and all of that is impossible.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument against the Karramiyya doctrine of temporal attributes in God. The argument is more complex and is built on three premises. Razi begins with the conditional: if God's essence were receptive (qābila) to a temporal attribute, then that receptivity would be a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of His essence, and it would be eternal. Eternal receptivity would imply that the recipient (the attribute) is possible to exist in eternity, because the relation of receptivity requires both relata to be real. But events (ḥawādith) have a beginning, while eternity has no beginning, so the existence of events in eternity is impossible. Hence, the premise of receptivity leads to contradiction. Razi then elaborates the three premises: (1) The receptivity must be a necessary concomitant of the essence; otherwise an infinite regress of acceptances would follow. (2) Eternal receptivity entails the possibility of the recipient in eternity, because a relation requires both terms to be actual. (3) Events cannot occur in eternity because they are temporal by definition, and combining eternality with non-eternality is contradictory. Thus the Karramiyya position is refuted.

Key Terms
قابلية
لوازم
أزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the receptivity could be accidental without consequence.
Correction: Razi shows that accidental receptivity would lead to an infinite regress of acceptances, so it must be necessary.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the first premise in Razi's second argument?
A) Temporal attributes cannot exist in God.
B) Receptivity to temporal attributes must be a necessary concomitant of God's essence.
C) God's essence is composed of parts.
D) Events can exist in eternity.

Key Takeaway
The first premise of Razi's second argument establishes that any receptivity in God's essence would be a necessary, eternal concomitant, not an accidental attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you believe that God cannot acquire new attributes, what does that mean for your relationship with Him? Are you content with a God who is unchanging in His perfection, or do you secretly wish He would accommodate your changing desires? Examine your heart: do you truly trust in the perfection of God's eternal nature, or do you wish He were different?
?
Razi argues that any receptivity in God would lead to an infinite regress. Is there an area in your life where you are trying to add something to God that He does not need—such as expecting Him to change His attributes to suit your preferences? Reflect on whether you are trying to remake God in your own image, and commit to accepting Him as He has revealed Himself.
?
The concept of a 'necessary concomitant' means an attribute that cannot be separated from God's essence. Do you truly believe that God's attributes—like His power, knowledge, and justice—are perfect and eternal, without any need for change? Or do you sometimes wish He were less demanding, more lenient, or different? Ask yourself: will you submit to the God who is necessarily perfect, or will you worship a god of your own making?

The Second and Third Premises: Eternal Receptivity and the Impossibility of Events in Eternity
Razi presents the second and third premises of his argument, completing the refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second and third premises: that eternal receptivity entails the possibility of events in eternity, and that events cannot occur in eternity because they have a beginning.

Core Idea
Eternal receptivity would make events possible in eternity, but events are temporal—a contradiction.

Having established that receptivity must be a necessary and eternal concomitant, Razi now builds on that. The second premise argues that eternal receptivity implies the possibility of the recipient's existence in eternity. The third premise shows that events cannot exist in eternity because they are defined as having a beginning. Together, these premises complete the refutation of temporal attributes in God. This lesson unpacks these two crucial steps.

Guided Path
1
Read the second premise
Read the second premise carefully: if receptivity is eternal, then the existence of the recipient (the temporal attribute) must be possible in eternity. Note the reasoning about the relation between receptive and recipient.
Razi uses a relational argument: a relation requires both relata to be actual.
2
Understand the third premise
Read the third premise: events cannot occur in eternity because they are defined as having a beginning, while eternity has no beginning. This is a direct contradiction.
This is a definitional argument: the nature of events contradicts eternity.
3
See the overall conclusion
Combine the two premises: eternal receptivity leads to possibility of events in eternity, but events cannot be eternal, so the initial assumption (receptivity) is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The proof of that is: if it were not necessary, it would be accidental (ʿārid), so the essence would be receptive to that receptivity. The second premise: If the receptivity were eternal, then the recipient (al-maqbūl) must be sound for existence in eternity. First Argument Against Karramiyya: Attributes Must Be Perfections
Razi gives the first argument against Karramiyya based on divine perfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's logical proof that divine attributes cannot be originated because they are perfections, and why this refutes the Karramiyya position.

Core Idea
Originated attributes would imply deficiency in God, which is impossible.

Razi begins his refutation of the Karramiyya sect by establishing a foundational principle: all divine attributes are perfections. From this, he constructs a simple yet powerful argument that originated attributes cannot belong to God. This lesson unpacks the logical steps of that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the plain meaning.
Notice the concise style typical of kalam proofs.
2
Identify the premise
The first sentence states that every divine attribute is a perfection. This is the key premise.
3
Follow the conditional reasoning
Razi uses an 'if-then' structure: if an attribute were originated, then before it God would lack that perfection, implying deficiency. Trace this logic.
The term 'محدثة' (originated) is central.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since deficiency in God is impossible, originated attributes cannot occur in God. The conclusion is stated firmly.
5
Reflect on the refutation
Consider how this argument specifically targets the Karramiyya claim that God can be a locus for temporal events (accidents).
This argument does not apply to attributes that are not perfections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى: أن كل ما كان من صفات الله تعالى فلا بد وأن يكون من صفات الكمال ونعوت الجلال، فلو كانت صفة من صفاته محدثة لكان ذاته قبل حدوث تلك الصفة خاليًا عن صفة الكمال والخالي عن صفة الكمال ناقص، فيلزم أن ذاته كانت ناقصة قبل تلك الصفة فيها، وذلك محال، فثبت أن حدوث الصفة ذات الله تعالى محال.
The first proof: Whatever is among the attributes of God, exalted, must be among the attributes of perfection and the characteristics of majesty. So if an attribute among His attributes were originated, then His essence before the occurrence of that attribute would be devoid of the attribute of perfection, and the one devoid of the attribute of perfection is deficient. Thus it follows that His essence was deficient before that attribute in it, and that is impossible. So it is established that the occurrence of an attribute in the essence of God, exalted, is impossible.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument against the Karramiyya's claim that God can be a locus for originated attributes. The argument is based on the principle that all divine attributes are perfections. Razi argues that if an attribute of God were originated (muhdath), then before that attribute existed, God would have been without that perfection, implying deficiency. Since deficiency in God is impossible, originated attributes cannot attach to Him. This is a straightforward a priori argument from divine perfection.

Key Terms
صفات الكمال
نعوت الجلال
محدثة
النقص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that no attributes can be originated in any being.
Correction: The argument only applies to divine attributes because they are perfections. Created beings can have originated attributes without implying deficiency.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's first argument, what would be entailed if an attribute of God were originated?
A) God would gain new perfection
B) God would have been deficient before that attribute
C) The attribute would be eternal
D) The attribute would be accidental

Key Takeaway
Divine attributes are perfections; therefore, they cannot be originated, as that would imply deficiency in God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Did you, in that moment, unconsciously attribute a shortcoming to Him? How can you replace that thought with the certainty that His action at that moment was a perfection in disguise?
?
Razi’s argument shows that originated attributes would imply deficiency. But you often speak of God as 'responding' to your prayers or 'changing' His decree. Do your words truly reflect a God who is eternally perfect with no new attributes? What specific phrase can you adopt that honors His perfection without implying origination?
?
If God cannot be deficient, then every attribute He has is maximal. Name one attribute of God that you routinely fail to trust as perfect—for example, His justice, mercy, or power. Write down a concrete situation where you can act today as if that attribute is fully perfect, even if you don't feel it.
