Fourth Objection: The Impossibility of Refraining as an Object of Power
Razi presents a fourth objection arguing that refraining is not a possible object of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' objection that refraining cannot be an object of God's power, and how this leads to the conclusion that God is a necessary cause.

Core Idea
Refraining is not an object of power, so God can only create, making Him a necessary cause.

The philosophers raise a fourth objection against the Ashʿarite view that God can freely choose to act or refrain. They argue that refraining (tark) is simply remaining upon original non-existence, which cannot be an object of power because power is an effective attribute and non-existence is pure negation. If God cannot refrain, then He must always act, collapsing into a necessary cause. This passage presents the reasoning behind that objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two arguments
Read the Arabic passage carefully and note the two distinct reasons given why original non-existence cannot be an object of power.
The first reason focuses on the nature of power and non-existence; the second on persistence.
2
Analyze the definition of refraining
Understand that the philosophers define refraining as 'remaining upon original non-existence'. Consider why this definition is crucial to their argument.
3
Trace the conclusion
Follow the reasoning from the two reasons to the final conclusion that the powerful agent becomes a necessitating cause (mujib) with no distinction from a natural cause.
Pay attention to the phrase 'ينقلب القادر موجبًا'.
4
Reflect on the theological stakes
Consider why this objection is significant: it challenges the Islamic doctrine of divine free will and the ability to choose not to create.
The Ashʿarite theologians will counter this by redefining refraining as the absence of action, not a positive act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الرابع: الترك عبارة عن البقاء على العدم الأصلي فالعدم الأصلي لا يصلح أن يكون مقدور الوجهين.
الأول : أن القدرة صفة مؤثرة والعدم نفي محض، فلا يكون للمقدور أثر فيه البتة فامتنع كون العدم مقدوراً.
الثاني : هو أن العدم الأصلي باقٍ كما كان قبل ذلك والباقي حال بقائه لا يكون مقدورًا فإذن الترك عبارة عن بقاء الشيء على عدمه الأصلي، والعدم الباقي لا يصلح يكون مقدورًا نظرًا إلى كونه عدماً، ونظرًا إلى كونه باقيا ، فثبت أن الترك لا يصلح أن يكون مقدورًا البتة، فلم يكن القادر قادرًا إلا على الفعل ولا قدرة له على الترك البتة فثبت أن القادر له صلاحية التأثير في الوجود وليس له صلاحية الترك، فحينئذ ينقلب القادر موجبًا ولا يبقى بينه وبين الموجب فرق البتة.
The fourth question: Refraining (at-tark) is an expression for remaining upon the original non-existence. The original non-existence is not suitable to be an object of power (maqdūr) from two aspects. The first: that power is an effective attribute, and non-existence is pure negation, so the object of power has no effect upon it at all. Therefore, it is impossible for non-existence to be an object of power. The second: that the original non-existence is remaining as it was before, and the remaining during its state of remaining is not an object of power. Thus refraining is an expression for the thing's remaining upon its original non-existence, and the remaining non-existence is not suitable to be an object of power, considering it as non-existence and considering it as remaining. So it is established that refraining is not at all suitable to be an object of power. Therefore the powerful one (al-qādir) is only powerful over the act, and has no power over refraining at all. So it is established that the powerful one has the capability to influence existence but does not have the capability for refraining. Then the powerful one turns into a necessitating cause (mūjib), and no difference remains between him and the necessitating cause at all.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is a fourth objection (السؤال الرابع) from the philosophers, arguing that refraining (not acting) cannot be an object of power for God. They give two reasons: 1) Power is an effective attribute, and non-existence is pure negation; power cannot affect nothing. 2) The original non-existence persists; a persisting state cannot be an object of power because it is already present. Thus, God can only bring about existence, not refrain from it. Consequently, God is necessitated to act whenever He wills, which eliminates free choice and makes Him a necessary cause. This objection targets the theological notion that God can choose to create or not create.

Key Terms
الترك
العدم الأصلي
القدرة
المقدور
الموجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that refraining is a positive act of will that God can choose.
Correction: The philosophers define refraining as merely remaining upon non-existence, not a separate act. The objection assumes that if refraining were a positive object of power, it would require affecting non-existence, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of this objection?
A) God can refrain from acting.
B) God can only create existence, not refrain, so He is necessitated.
C) Non-existence is an object of power.
D) Power has no effect on existence.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' fourth objection posits that if refraining cannot be an object of power, then God must always act, contradicting the possibility of divine free choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If refraining is not an object of power, does that mean you have no real ability to omit an action? When you choose not to sin, is that a genuine power or just the absence of action? How does this challenge your understanding of your own moral responsibility before God?
?
The philosophers claim that if God cannot refrain, He becomes a necessary cause like fire. If that were true, would God be unjust in punishing evil, since He could not have refrained from creating a world containing evil? How does this force you to rethink the balance between God's power and His justice in your own heart?
?
This objection reduces the Creator to a blind force. Reflect: Have you ever acted as if God is a 'necessary cause'—expecting Him to act in predictable ways—rather than a free Lord who may choose to withhold or bestow? How can you today worship Him as a voluntary agent, not as a mechanical cause?

The First Theological Response to the Problem of Preponderance
Razi presents the first theological opinion and its initial critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first theological opinion on how a free agent's act becomes preponderant without reaching necessity, and its initial critique.

Core Idea
A motive makes the act more likely, but not necessary, preserving free agency.

Before examining the theologians' responses, Razi summarizes the philosophers' objections. The first question challenges how a free agent can choose one of two equally possible acts. In answer, some theologians proposed that a motive tips the scale, making the act preponderant but not necessary. Razi then critiques this view, beginning with the problem of the state of equivalence.

Guided Path
1
Set the context
Note that Razi first says 'These are the totality of the philosophers' questions' before moving to the answer. This lesson focuses on the answer to the first question.
2
Identify the first theological opinion
The opinion: the act depends on a motive (dāʿī), and with the motive, the act becomes 'more likely' (awlā) but not necessary.
This is an attempt to avoid determinism while explaining preference.
3
Understand the distinction preserved
Because the act does not become necessary, the difference between a necessitating cause (mūjib) and a free agent (qādir) remains intact.
The opinion aims to reconcile free will with causality.
4
Recognize the critique
Razi states this opinion is weak from two aspects. The first aspect is introduced: at the time when act and refraining are equal, the motive does not tip the scale because it hasn't arisen yet.
The passage cuts off here, but the critique continues in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذه مجموع أسئلة الفلاسفة في هذا المقام
والجواب على السؤال الأول هو أن نقول :
للمتكلمين في هذا المقام قولان:
أحدهما : أن صدور الفعل عن القادر موقوف على الداعي إلا أن الفعل مع الداعي يصير أولى بالوقوع، إلا أنه لا ينتهي إلى حد الوجوب، فلأجل أنه صار أولى بالوقوع صار الوقوع راجحًا على اللاوقوع ولأجل أنه لا ينتهي إلى حد الوجوب يبقى الفرق بين الموجب والقادر.
واعلم أن هذا الكلام ضعيف من وجهين :
الأول وهو أن في الوقت الذي كان الفعل والترك في حيز التساوي كان
These are the totality of the philosophers' questions in this context. And the answer to the first question is that we say: The theologians have two opinions in this context. One of them: that the occurrence of the act from the powerful one (al-qādir) depends on the motive (ad-dāʿī), except that the act with the motive becomes more likely (awlā) to occur, but it does not reach the limit of necessity. Because it became more likely, occurrence becomes preponderant over non-occurrence; and because it does not reach the limit of necessity, the difference remains between the necessitating cause (al-mūjib) and the powerful one (al-qādir). Know that this statement is weak from two aspects: The first is that at the time when the act and refraining were in the domain of equivalence...
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage serves as a transition: first, Razi summarizes that the preceding objections constitute all the philosophers' questions in this context. Then he begins the answer to the first question (which was the strong argument about preponderants). He states that theologians (المتكلمين) have two opinions. He presents the first opinion: that the act depends on a motive, and with the motive the act becomes more likely (أولى) but not necessary. This preserves a distinction between free and necessary causation because the preference does not reach necessity. However, Razi immediately notes that this opinion is weak for two reasons, and he begins to explain the first reason: when act and refraining are equivalent (in the domain of equality), the motive would have no effect. The passage cuts off, but the critique is based on the state of equivalence before the motive arises.

Key Terms
أسئلة الفلاسفة
الداعي
أولى بالوقوع
الوجوب
الراجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first opinion might be taken as a complete solution that fully explains free choice without any remaining problem.
Correction: Razi immediately critiques the view, showing that it fails to explain how the motive itself becomes effective when act and refraining are initially equal.

Checkpoint
What does the first theological opinion claim about the act with a motive?
A) It becomes necessary.
B) It becomes more likely but not necessary.
C) It becomes impossible.
D) It remains completely undetermined.

Key Takeaway
The first theological answer posits that a motive makes an act more likely but not necessary, yet this fails to explain how the motive itself becomes effective when the act and refraining are initially equal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an act of worship, do you sense that your motive makes it 'more likely' but not compulsory? Are you aware that you could still choose otherwise? How does this awareness affect your sincerity and connection with Allah?
?
Consider a sin you struggle with. Do you feel that your desires make the sin 'more likely' to the point of inevitability? How does this lesson's distinction between 'more likely' and 'necessary' challenge that feeling and open a door for repentance?
?
In your daily decisions, do you treat your motives as determining forces or as weights that tip the scale while you retain ultimate choice? What would change in your life if you truly internalized that you are a qādir (powerful agent) and not a mūjib (necessitated cause)?

The Third Objection: God's Foreknowledge Necessitates All Events
Philosophers argue God's foreknowledge necessitates all events, denying choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical argument that God's perfect foreknowledge seems to necessitate all events, reducing Him to a necessitating cause rather than a free agent.

Core Idea
God's foreknowledge apparently turns Him into a necessity, not a chooser.

This lesson presents a powerful philosophical challenge to divine free will. The argument claims that because God knows everything perfectly, whatever He knows will happen must happen—leaving no room for choice or contingency. As you read the passage, notice how the philosopher builds a logical chain from omniscience to determinism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then the accompanying literal translation. Let the argument's flow come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the key terms: 'موجب بالذات' and 'فاعل بالاختيار'.
2
Identify the logical steps
The argument has a clear structure: premise (God knows all), impossibility of contradiction, conclusion for known occurrences, conclusion for known non-occurrences, final conclusion. Trace each step.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Ask yourself: Why does the impossibility of contradiction lead to God being a necessitating cause? What does 'موجب بالذات' mean as opposed to 'فاعل بالاختيار'?
This connects to the core question of divine free will in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث : لاشك أنه تعالى عالم بجميع المعلومات وهو يعلم أن الشيء الفلاني يقع في الوقت الفلاني، والشيء الفلاني لا يقع في الوقت الفلاني، وخلاف المعلوم محال الوقوع ؛ لأن عدم وقوع الشيء مع العلم بوقوع الشيء ضدان، والضدان ممتنعان متنافيان لذاتيهما، وإذا كان كذلك فما علم الله تعالى وقوعه كان واجب الوقوع ممتنع العدم وما علم عدمه كان واجب العدم ممتنع الوجود، ولا خروج عن هذين القسمين، فيكون الله تعالى موجبًا بالذات لا فاعلا بالاختيار.
The third question: There is no doubt that He, exalted, is knower of all known things, and He knows that a certain thing occurs at a certain time, and a certain thing does not occur at a certain time. And contradiction of the known is impossible of occurrence, because non-occurrence of a thing together with knowledge of its occurrence are two opposites, and opposites are impossible and mutually exclusive by their essences. And if it is so, then what God knows to occur is necessarily occurring, impossible of non-existence, and what He knows not to occur is necessarily non-existent, impossible of existence. And there is no exit from these two divisions. Thus God is necessitating by essence (mūjib bi al-dhāt), not agent by choice (fāʿil bi al-ikhtiyār).
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage presents the third philosophical objection against divine voluntary action. The objection argues from God's omniscience: since God knows all future events with certainty, whatever He knows will happen must happen, and whatever He knows will not happen must not happen. This is because the occurrence of an event alongside God's knowledge of its non-occurrence would be a contradiction, and contradictions are impossible. Therefore, all events are necessary in relation to God's knowledge, and God is not a free agent but a necessitating cause (mūjib bi al-dhāt). The objection aims to show that divine knowledge eliminates the possibility of choice, reducing God to a necessary cause.

Key Terms
عالم
علم
وجوب
إمكان
موجب بالذات
فاعل بالاختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God's foreknowledge directly causes events, similar to natural causation, leaving no role for divine will.
Correction: The objection argues from the impossibility of contradiction, not from causation. It claims that knowledge necessitates the known event by logical necessity, not by efficient causality.

Checkpoint
What is the main claim of the third philosophical objection?
A) God's power is limited by His knowledge.
B) God's knowledge necessitates all events, making Him a necessitating cause.
C) God's knowledge is irrelevant to the occurrence of events.
D) God's choice is incompatible with His ignorance.

Key Takeaway
The objection uses God's omniscience to argue for determinism, posing a challenge to divine voluntarism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used the idea that 'everything is already written' as an excuse to avoid making a difficult choice? How does this objection expose that excuse as a surrender of responsibility, not a submission to God?
?
If you truly believe God knows every sin you will commit before you do it, does that make you more careless about your actions, or more desperate for His mercy? Where does your heart actually stand?
?
When you sin, do you ever think 'God already knew I would do this, so it's not fully my fault'? Read the argument again: does it give you comfort, or does it terrify you that your choices might be mere illusions?

The Philosophers' Objection: Refraining is Not an Object of Power
A philosophical argument that refraining is not an object of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why philosophers argue that refraining cannot be an act of power, and how this challenges the concept of a freely choosing God.

Core Idea
If refraining is beyond power, then even God cannot choose not to act.

This lesson presents a powerful philosophical objection that threatens the very notion of divine free choice. The argument claims that 'refraining'—the act of not doing something—cannot be a genuine object of power, because power produces effects and non-existence is not an effect. If true, this would mean that a powerful agent like God is necessarily a necessitating cause, not a free chooser.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice that the passage defines refraining and then gives two reasons.
2
Distinguish the two reasons
Identify the two arguments against refraining being an object of power: one based on the nature of power and non-existence, the other based on the persistence of original non-existence.
3
Understand the conclusion
See how the two reasons lead to the claim that a powerful agent (qādir) can only act, not refrain, thus becoming a necessitating cause (mūjib).
This conclusion directly challenges the idea of divine free choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الرابع: الترك عبارة عن البقاء على العدم الأصلي فالعدم الأصلي لا يصلح أن يكون مقدور الوجهين. الأول : أن القدرة صفة مؤثرة والعدم نفي محض، فلا يكون للمقدور أثر فيه البتة فامتنع كون العدم مقدوراً. الثاني : هو أن العدم الأصلي باقٍ كما كان قبل ذلك والباقي حال بقائه لا يكون مقدورًا فإذن الترك عبارة عن بقاء الشيء على عدمه الأصلي، والعدم الباقي لا يصلح يكون مقدورًا نظرًا إلى كونه عدماً، ونظرًا إلى كونه باقيا ، فثبت أن الترك لا يصلح أن يكون مقدورًا البتة، فلم يكن القادر قادرًا إلا على الفعل ولا قدرة له على الترك البتة فثبت أن القادر له صلاحية التأثير في الوجود وليس له صلاحية الترك، فحينئذ ينقلب القادر موجبًا ولا ويبقى بينه وبين الموجب فرق البتة.
The fourth question: Refraining (tark) is an expression for remaining upon the original non-existence. The original non-existence is not suitable to be an object of power (maqdūr) in two respects. First: Power (qudra) is an effective attribute, and non-existence is pure negation, so the object of power has no effect in it at all, therefore it is impossible for non-existence to be an object of power. Second: The original non-existence remains as it was before, and that which remains while remaining is not an object of power. So then refraining is an expression for a thing remaining on its original non-existence, and the remaining non-existence is not suitable to be an object of power, considering it is non-existence, and considering it is remaining. So it is established that refraining is not at all suitable to be an object of power. Thus the powerful (qādir) is only powerful over action and has no power over refraining at all. So it is established that the powerful has the capacity to effect existence and does not have the capacity to refrain. Then the powerful turns into a necessitating cause (mūjib) and no difference remains between him and the necessitating cause at all.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth philosophical objection. It argues that 'refraining' (tark) from an action is not something that can be an object of power (maqdūr). The argument is twofold: First, power is an attribute that produces an effect, but non-existence (ʿadam) is a pure negation and cannot receive an effect. Second, the original non-existence is already present and remains; what remains cannot be brought about by power. Therefore, refraining is simply the continuation of original non-existence, not an act that power can produce. Hence, a powerful agent (qādir) can only act, not refrain. Consequently, God (who is all-powerful) cannot choose to refrain from acting; He must act, making Him a necessitating cause (mūjib) indistinguishable from a necessary agent. This objection targets the possibility of divine choice by denying that refraining is within God's power.

Key Terms
الترك
العدم الأصلي
قدرة
مقدور
موجب
قادر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Refraining is an action that God can perform.
Correction: The philosophers argue that refraining is not an action but the continuation of non-existence, which cannot be an object of power because power only affects existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does power not apply to non-existence?
A) Because non-existence is not a real thing.
B) Because power is an effective attribute and non-existence is pure negation.
C) Because non-existence can be created by power.
D) Because power only applies to actions, not to refraining.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that refraining cannot be an object of power, thereby challenging the notion that God can freely choose to refrain from acting.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you believe you could have refrained by your own power? According to this passage, refraining is not an object of power. How does this force you to reconsider your need for God's help in avoiding sin? Will you now turn to Him in prayer before temptation?
?
The philosophers claim that if a powerful being cannot refrain, it becomes a necessitating cause, not a free chooser. Do you sometimes treat God's will in your life as a mechanical cause—expecting His actions to happen inevitably—rather than as the free choice of a personal Lord? How will you remind yourself daily that God freely chooses to care for you?
?
Consider an area in your life where you feel powerless to stop a harmful habit. This lesson argues that refraining is beyond human power. Does this make you feel excused, or does it drive you to rely on God's power? What concrete step will you take this week to acknowledge your dependence on Him for self-control?

Razi's Definition of Qadir: Refuting the Determinism Objection
Razi defines qadir and refutes the second objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's definition of qadir (capable) and how it refutes the objection that God cannot be qadir due to eternal knowledge.

Core Idea
Qadir means choosing act or omission is conceivable, not simultaneous.

Razi now answers a second objection that might have been raised against divine omnipotence. The objection likely claimed that because God's knowledge is eternal, He cannot be called qadir, since a capable agent must be able to do otherwise. Razi clarifies the true meaning of qadir, showing that it does not imply the impossible power to choose both act and omission at once, but rather the logical possibility of each alternative. This subtle but crucial distinction preserves God's attribute of power without falling into absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice that Razi begins by stating what qadir does NOT mean before giving the correct definition.
2
Understand the rejected definition
Razi rejects the idea that qadir means someone who, when choosing to act, also chooses to omit. Ask yourself: why would that be like combining opposites?
This is key to grasping the objection's flaw.
3
Grasp the correct definition
Razi states that qadir is one from whom choosing the act and choosing the opposite are both conceivable. Reflect on why this is a valid definition and how it applies to God.
The word 'conceivable' (yatasawwar) is crucial.
4
Connect to divine power
Razi concludes that this meaning is intelligible for God, so He is qadir. Consider how this answers the objection about determinism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب عن السؤال الثاني : فهو أن ليس القادر عبارة عن الذي عند اختيار الفعل بتصور منه اختيار الترك، فإن ذلك يجري مجرى الجمع بين الضدين، وهو محال، بل القادر هو الذي يتصور منه اختيار الفعل وبالعكس وهذا المعنى معقول في حق الله تعالى، فكان قادرًا. المسألة الحادية عشرة
As for the answer to the second question: It is that 'capable' (qadir) does not mean one who, when choosing the act, simultaneously chooses omission, for that would be like combining two opposites, which is impossible. Rather, the capable agent is one from whom choosing the act and conversely are conceivable. And this meaning is intelligible with respect to God, so He is capable. The eleventh issue.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage answers the second objection. The objection likely claimed that since God's knowledge and will are eternal, He cannot be called qadir because He cannot do otherwise. Razi's answer clarifies the definition of qadir: it does not mean that the agent must simultaneously choose both act and omission (which is impossible, like combining opposites). Rather, qadir means that it is conceivable (yatasawwar) for the agent to choose the act and also conceivable to choose the opposite. This meaning is logically applicable to God, so He is qadir. The phrase 'the eleventh issue' is a chapter heading.

Key Terms
قادر (qadir)
اختيار (ikhtiyar)
جمع بين الضدين (jam' bayn al-diddayn)
مستحيل (muhal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that qadir requires the ability to both act and omit at the same instant.
Correction: Qadir only requires that each alternative (act or omission) is conceivable for the agent; not that both be chosen at once.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's definition, what does 'qadir' mean?
A) One who must act at all times
B) One who necessarily produces both act and omission
C) One from whom choosing the act and choosing the opposite are both conceivable
D) One who has no control over his actions

Key Takeaway
Razi defines qadir as one for whom choosing an action or its opposite is logically conceivable, not as one who must choose both at once.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own inability to sometimes resist sin, does that make you question whether you are truly 'qadir' in the sense Razi defines for God? What does that reveal about your need for divine help?
?
Do you ever catch yourself thinking of God as somehow constrained or forced to act in a certain way? How does Razi's insistence that conceivability of both act and omission applies to God challenge that thought?
?
If you truly believed that you are capable of choosing either to pray or to skip prayer in every moment, would your prayer habits change? What is the real barrier in your heart?

Eternal Will and Divine Freedom: The Objection and Answer
Razi presents an objection about eternal will implying determinism and answers with original capability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends divine freedom against the objection that eternal will implies necessitation, using the concept of original capability.

Core Idea
Eternal will does not turn God into a machine; original capability preserves freedom.

This passage presents a powerful objection against divine freedom: if God's will is eternal and unchangeable, doesn't that make Him a mere necessitator? Razi's answer is subtle and profound. Let's see how he uses the idea of 'original capability' to defend the distinction between a free agent and a necessary cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the objection is presented as something 'it should not be said' — a rhetorical device.
2
Understand the underlying reasoning
Ask yourself: Why does eternal will seem to force God to act? Trace the argument: if the will is eternally attached to a specific act at a specific time, then at that time it becomes impossible for God not to act—otherwise the eternal attachment would be severed, implying change in God.
This is a classic dilemma for divine free will.
3
Grasp Razi's answer: original capability
Razi's answer is brief: 'The original capability was present.' What does that mean? It means that from eternity, God had the potential to either act or refrain—even though His will determined one specific outcome. This original capability is what distinguishes a free agent from a necessitating cause.
Think of it as God having 'two-way power' from the very beginning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : نفرض الكلام في الشيء الذي تعلقت إرادته وقدرته في الأزل بإيجاده في لا يزال، فنقول : لا حال من الأحوال يشار إليه إلا ويمتنع من الله تعالى في ذلك الوقت أن لا يوجد ذلك الفعل، إذ لو لم يوجد لانقطع ذلك التعلق المستمر من الأزل إلى ذلك الوقت، وذلك يقتضي تغير صفات الله تعالى وزوال ذلك التعلق القديم، وكل ذلك محال، وإذا كان كذلك فلا حال يشار إليه إلا ويجب عقلا كونه تعالى موجدًا لذلك الفعل في ذلك الوقت الخاص ويمتنع أن لا يكون مؤثرًا فيه، فهذا يكون موجبا لا قادرًا. لأنا نقول : الصلاحية الأصلية كانت حاصلة، وهذا القدر يكفي في الفرق بين الموجب والمختار.
It should not be said: We posit the case of the thing to which His will and power were eternally attached to bring it into existence at a time that will be. Then we say: There is no state that can be pointed to except that it is impossible for God at that time not to bring that act into existence, for if it were not brought into existence, that continuous attachment from eternity to that time would be severed, and that would necessitate a change in God's attributes and the disappearance of that eternal attachment. All of that is impossible. And if that is the case, then there is no state that can be pointed to except that it is rationally necessary that He is the bringer-into-existence of that act at that specific time, and it is impossible that He not be effective in it. So He would be a necessitator (mujib), not a capable agent (qadir). [The answer:] We say: The original capability (al-salahiyya al-asliyya) was present, and this much suffices for the distinction between the necessitator and the free agent (mukhtar).
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and its answer. The objection argues that since God's will and power are eternal and unchangeably attached to a specific act at a specific future time, it becomes impossible for God not to perform that act at that time. If He did not, the eternal attachment would be severed, implying change in God, which is impossible. Therefore, God is a necessitator (mujib), not a free agent (qadir). Razi's answer is succinct: the original capability (al-salahiyya al-asliyya) was always present. This means that from eternity, God had the capability to both act and not act, even though His will determines one specific outcome. This original capability is enough to distinguish a free agent from a necessitating cause, because a necessitator never had such capability from the outset.

Key Terms
صلاحية أصلية (salahiyya asliyya)
موجب (mujib)
مختار (mukhtar)
تعلق (ta'alluq)
إرادة (irada)
قدرة (qudra)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does Razi answer the objection that eternal will makes God a necessitator?
A) He denies the eternity of will
B) He argues that original capability was present from eternity
C) He says that God can change His will
D) He claims that the objection is irrelevant

Key Takeaway
The concept of original capability (al-salahiyya al-asliyya) preserves divine freedom even though God's will is eternal and unchangeable, by affirming that God had the potential to either act or refrain from the beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult choice, do you ever tell yourself 'I had no choice'? How does God's original capability, which shows that even with eternal knowledge He remains free, challenge your excuses?
?
If you believe God's will is unchangeable yet He is perfectly free, does that change how you relate to Him in prayer? Are you willing to ask for change, believing He can truly choose to respond?
?
The objector saw eternal will as turning God into a machine. Do you ever treat God's will as a mechanical force, rather than a personal, free act? How can you restore awe and intimacy in your relationship with the One whose will is both certain and free?

Knowledge Follows the Act: Razi's Answer
Razi presents a dependency argument to defend divine freedom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the objection that God's eternal knowledge constrains His free agency by showing that knowledge is ontologically dependent on the act.

Core Idea
Knowledge is subordinate to the act, not its cause.

Razi addresses a common objection: if God eternally knows an act will occur, doesn't that make the act necessary and limit His freedom? In this passage, he clarifies the order of dependence — knowledge follows the act, which itself depends on power and will. This preserves God's free agency while affirming His omniscience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice the repeated phrase 'subordinate to' (تبع).
The passage is a single logical argument, so read it as a chain.
2
Identify the chain of subordination
Identify the three elements: knowledge, act, and power/will. Follow how Razi links them: knowledge is subordinate to the act, and the act is subordinate to power and will.
The chain is linear: power/will → act → knowledge.
3
Understand the conclusion
Razi concludes that since knowledge is subordinate to the act and the act is subordinate to power/will, knowledge cannot hinder power/will. Reflect on why this solves the objection.
The key is ontological order: what depends on something else cannot constrain that upon which it depends.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب عن السؤال الثالث : فهو أن تعلق العلم بوقوع الفعل في ذلك الزمان المعين تبع لوقوع الفعل في ذلك الزمان المعين ووقوعه في ذلك الزمان المعين تبع لتأثير القدرة والإرادة في ذلك الزمان، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك كان تعلق العلم بوقوعه في ذلك الزمان المعين تبعًا لتبع تعلق القدرة والإرادة بإيقاعه في ذلك الزمان، فيمتنع أن يكون تعلق العلم مانعا من تعلق القدرة والإرادة.
As for the answer to the third question: It is that the attachment of knowledge (ta'alluq al-'ilm) to the occurrence of the act at that specific time is subordinate to (taba') the occurrence of the act at that specific time, and its occurrence at that specific time is subordinate to the effect of power and will at that time. And if it is so, then the attachment of knowledge to its occurrence at that specific time is subordinate to the subordination of the attachment of power and will to its bringing about at that time. Therefore, it is impossible for the attachment of knowledge to be a hindrance to the attachment of power and will.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage answers the third objection, which likely argued that God's eternal knowledge of the act would constrain the act, making it necessary. Razi's answer is based on the principle of ontological dependence: knowledge (al-'ilm) is subordinate (taba') to the known object. The act occurs first as a result of power and will, then knowledge attaches to it. Therefore, the attachment of knowledge is subordinate to the attachment of power and will. Since knowledge follows the act and does not precede it in the order of causation, it cannot be a hindrance to power and will. This preserves God's free agency.

Key Terms
تعلق العلم (ta'alluq al-'ilm)
تبع (taba')
قدرة (qudra)
إرادة (irada)
إيقاع (iqa')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's eternal knowledge of an act makes the act necessary and limits His freedom.
Correction: Razi shows that knowledge is subordinate to the act, which is subordinate to power and will. Therefore, knowledge cannot constrain power and will; rather, it depends on them.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's answer, what is the relationship between God's knowledge and His act?
A) Knowledge necessitates the act
B) Knowledge is subordinate to the act
C) Knowledge and the act are independent
D) The act is subordinate to knowledge

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is subordinate to His act, which depends on His power and will, so knowledge cannot constrain His free agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that God's knowledge is subordinate to His act, which is caused by His power and will. When you reflect on your own life, do you sometimes feel that God's foreknowledge leaves you without real choice? How does this teaching challenge or confirm that feeling? Will you today surrender your actions to God's will, trusting that His knowledge does not negate your responsibility?
?
If knowledge is subordinate to the act, then God's knowledge of your sins does not cause them. How does this affect the way you think about your own failures and God's mercy? Will you stop blaming God for your wrong choices and instead turn to Him in repentance?
?
This lesson shows that God's power and will are primary, and His knowledge follows. If you truly believed that God's will is the ultimate cause of all that happens, how would it change the way you pray and rely on Him? Will you begin this day by acknowledging that every act depends entirely on His power and will, and not on your own strength?
